Causality and Created Things
intermediate
7 min
Two Replies to the Objection
Two answers to the objection: divine permission and composite nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two responses to the objection against the formative power: the appeal to divine permission and the argument from the composite nature of semen.

Core Idea
The objection is flawed because it ignores divine permission and the complexity of the material substrate.

Having understood the powerful objection against the formative power, we now turn to the responses. The author provides two answers, the first addressing the agent-side and the second the receiver-side. These replies defend the possibility of the formative power by invoking divine creative action and by noting that semen is not a simple homogeneous substance.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage
Start with the Arabic from 'فإن قيل' to the end. Follow the dialectical structure: an objection (فإن قيل) and a reply (قلنا), then two explicit answers.
Notice the shift: the objection is now about the articulating power, but the author redirects the discussion to the role of powers as instruments.
2
Analyze the first answer
The first answer (عن الأول) addresses the agent side: the impossibility is only an improbability (استبعاد) and is not truly impossible if it occurs by God's permission and creation.
This answer relies on the distinction between absolute impossibility and what is merely improbable given the power's nature.
3
Analyze the second answer
The second answer (عن الثاني) grants the simplicity of the power and similarity of semen parts, but argues that semen is composed of bodies of different natures, so the action on the composite is not the same as on parts.
This rejects the assumption that the action on a composite must be uniform.
4
Reflect on the theological implications
Both answers ultimately attribute the complex outcome to divine agency: the first explicitly says 'by the permission of its Creator'; the second allows the composite to have a diversity that accommodates complexity.
Think about how this fits into the broader kalam view that created powers are only instruments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ونفاها بعضهم) :
.
للقطع باستحالة صدور مثل هذه الأفعال التي هي العمدة في الاستدلال على قدرة الصانع وعلمه وحكمته عن قوة بسيطة عديمة الشعور، حالة في مادة متشابهة الأجزاء أو الالتصاق ] (۱) .
إشارة إلى ما ذكره الإمام واختاره بعض الحكماء المتأخرين. وهو أن العقل قاطع بامتناع صدور هذه الأفعال المختلفة، والتركيبات العجيبة الدالة على غاية القدرة والحكمة على قوة بسيطة ليس لها شعور أصلا، مع أنها حالة في جسم متشابه الأجزاء، أو متشابه الامتزاج على اختلاف الرأيين ، إذ عند أرسطو جزء المنى كالكل في الاسم والحد من غير اختلاف في الحقيقة لكونه منفصلاً عن الانثيين فقط، وعند أبقراط أجزاء المنى مختلفة بالحقائق، متمايزة في نفس الأمر، إذ يخرج من اللحم جزء شبيه به ومن العظم جزء شبيه به، وكذا سائر الأعضاء، غاية الأمر أنها غير متمايزة في الحس، وهو معنى تشابه الامتزاج، ولكل
من الفريقين احتجاجات مذكورة في موضعها .
فعلى الأول: يلزم أن يكون الشكل الحادث من فعل المصورة في المنى هو الكرة على ما هو شأن فعل القوة الغير الشاعرة في المادة المتشابهة . وعلى الثاني : يلزم أن يكون الحاصل كرات مضمومة بعضها إلى بعض، وأن لا يبقى وضع الأعضاء وترتيبها على نسبة واحدة لكون المنى رطوبة سيالة لا يحفظ الوضع والترتيب.
لم
فإن قيل : إنما يمتنع اختلاف آثار القوة العديمة الشعور، في المادة الواحدة لو تفد القوة المفصلة فيها تمييز أجزاء (۱) ، واختلاف مواد للأعضاء .
·
قلنا : فيعود الكلام إلى القوة المفصلة . فإن اعترفوا بأن القوى في مرتبة الوسائط والآلات لا الفواعل والمؤثرات والمؤثر إنما هو خالقها القادر المختار الفعال لما يشاء، فقد اهتدوا ولم يبق سبيل إلى إثبات القوى والحاصل أن ما يدرك بعلم التشريح (۲) من الصور والكيفيات والأوضاع في بدن الإنسان يمتنع أن يجعل فعل القوة المصورة في مادة المنى إما من جهة الفاعل فلكونه عديم الشعور وإما من جهة القابل، فلكونه متشابهاً .
•
وقد يجاب عن الأول بأنه استبعاد وإنما يمتنع لولم يكن ذلك بإذن خالقها، بمعنى أنه خلقها كذلك، وأوجدها كذلك. وعن الثاني : بأنه لو سلّم بساطة القوة المصورة، وتشابه أجزاء المنى، فلاخفاء في أنه من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع وحينئذ لا يلزم أن يكون الحيوان كرة أو كرات، إذ لا يلزم أن يكون فعل القوة في المركب
فعلها في واحد واحد من الأجزاء (۳).
He said (and some denied them): Because of the certainty of the impossibility of the occurrence of such actions, which are the mainstay in inferring the power, knowledge and wisdom of the Maker, from a simple power devoid of consciousness, subsisting in a substance whose parts are similar or of adhesion, indicating what the Imam mentioned and what some later philosophers chose. That is, reason is decisive in the impossibility of the occurrence of these diverse actions and wondrous compositions indicating utmost power and wisdom from a simple power that has no consciousness at all, while it is subsisting in a body whose parts are similar, or of similar mixture according to the two opinions. For according to Aristotle, the part of semen is like the whole in name and definition without difference in reality because it is separated only from the testicles. According to Hippocrates, the parts of semen are different in realities, distinct in themselves, for from flesh comes a part resembling it, and from bone a part resembling it, and likewise the other organs. The point is that they are not distinct in sensation, and that is the meaning of similarity of mixture. Each of the two groups has proofs mentioned in its place. According to the first, it would follow that the shape arising from the action of the formative power in the semen would be spherical, as is the case of the action of the unconscious power in homogeneous matter. According to the second, it would follow that the result would be spheres joined to one another, and that the position of the organs and their arrangement would not remain in one proportion because the semen is a flowing moisture that does not preserve position and arrangement. Then if it is said: The diversity of effects of an unconscious power in one matter is only impossible if the articulating power does not provide a differentiation of parts and a diversity of materials of the organs. We say: The discussion returns to the articulating power. If they acknowledge that the powers are in the rank of intermediaries and instruments, not efficient causes and effectors, and the only effector is their Creator, the Powerful, the Free Chooser, the Doer of what He wills, then they have been guided and there remains no way to prove the powers. The conclusion is that what is perceived by anatomy of forms, qualities, and positions in the human body is impossible to make the action of the formative power in the substance of semen, either from the side of the agent because it is unconscious, or from the side of the receiver because it is similar. And it may be answered concerning the first that it is an improbability, and it would only be impossible if that were not by the permission of its Creator, meaning that He created it thus and brought it into existence thus. Concerning the second: Even if one grants the simplicity of the formative power and the similarity of the parts of the semen, there is no hiding that it is from bodies of different natures, and then it does not necessarily follow that the animal would be a sphere or spheres, for an action of the power in a composite is not necessarily identical to its action in each individual part.
Source pages p. 257

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the existence of a formative power (al-musawwira) in the semen, followed by a multi-part reply. The author begins by stating that some philosophers deny the formative power on the basis of a decisive proof: it is impossible for such complex and orderly actions, which serve as evidence for the Maker's power, knowledge, and wisdom, to originate from a simple, unconscious power existing in a homogeneous or similarly mixed substance. He attributes this argument to the Imam (al-Ghazali?) and some later philosophers. The proof is that reason definitively judges that a simple, unconscious power cannot produce diverse actions and wondrous compositions. This is supported by considering the two classical views on the composition of semen: Aristotle held that each part of semen is identical to the whole in essence, while Hippocrates held that parts are distinct and correspond to different organs. On Aristotle's view, if the formative power acted on homogeneous semen, it would produce a simple shape like a sphere. On Hippocrates' view, it would produce contiguous spheres without proper arrangement, since semen is fluid and cannot maintain position. The objection then considers a possible counter: the diversity of effects might be due to the articulating power (al-mufassila) providing differentiation. The reply (qulna) turns the discussion back to the articulating power: if one admits that all such powers are merely instruments and not true agents, and that the only real agent is the Creator who is powerful, free, and does as He wills, then the objection loses its force and there is no need to posit such powers at all. Then the author gives a second answer, addressing two aspects of the objection. First (the agent side), he argues that the impossibility is only an improbability; it is not truly impossible if the action occurs by God's permission and creation. Second (the receiver side), he argues that even if the formative power is simple and the semen similar, semen is composed of bodies of different natures, so the action of the power on the composite is not necessarily the same as on its parts, and thus the production of a complex organism does not entail a spherical shape.

Key Terms
المصورة
الاستبعاد
الإذن
القوة المفصلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The answers reject the objection entirely by claiming the formative power is real and independent.
Correction: The answers actually diminish the role of the formative power, either by attributing its effect to God's permission or by showing that the material substrate itself provides the complexity, thereby reducing the need for a powerful agent.

Checkpoint
How does the first answer (عن الأول) respond to the objection that a simple unconscious power cannot produce complex forms?
A) It argues that the power is not simple but complex
B) It says the impossibility is only an improbability, and it occurs by God's permission
C) It claims that the power is actually conscious
D) It denies that the forms are complex

Key Takeaway
The two answers defend the possibility of the formative power by attributing its effect to divine permission and by noting the composite nature of the material, thus preserving the theological principle of God's ultimate agency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own body, do you see it as a product of impersonal biological forces, or as a direct act of God's creative permission? What would change in your gratitude and worship if you truly internalized that every detail of your formation exists only by His إذن?
?
The objection in this lesson assumes that an unconscious power cannot produce complex design. Do you ever live as if your own abilities and achievements are purely your own doing, forgetting that you are mere instrument and that God alone is the true Agent? How would you need to reframe your daily successes to reflect this truth?
?
The second answer points out that the composite nature of semen itself prevents simple spherical outcome. Think of a area in your life where you assume monotony or simplicity, but which in reality is complex and designed by God. Are you overlooking His intricate design in your circumstances? What would it mean to actively look for His wisdom in the composite details of your life today?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
Two Objections to the Formative Power from Uniform Semen
Two philosophical objections against the formative power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objections raised against the idea that an unconscious formative power could produce the complex shape of the human fetus from uniform semen.

Core Idea
An unconscious force cannot produce intricate designs from uniform matter.

In this passage, Taftazani presents two scenarios that expose the difficulty of attributing the complex anatomy of the fetus to a blind power acting on simple, homogeneous matter. These objections set the stage for a deeper discussion about the true agent of creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the two objections
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the two distinct scenarios: first, if the power acts on uniform matter; second, if the power acts on fluid matter.
The objections are based on the nature of the power (unconscious) and the nature of the matter (uniform/fluid).
2
Understand the necessary outcomes
In each scenario, the necessary outcome is a simple shape (sphere or cluster of spheres) because an unconscious power cannot produce variation. Also, the fluidity of semen would cause the arrangement to be lost.
These outcomes contradict the observed complexity.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why these objections are philosophically significant: they challenge the sufficiency of natural causes without a conscious designer.
This connects to the broader theme of causality in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى الأول: يلزم أن يكون الشكل الحادث من فعل المصورة في المنى هو الكرة على ما هو شأن فعل القوة الغير الشاعرة في المادة المتشابهة . وعلى الثاني : يلزم أن يكون الحاصل كرات مضمومة بعضها إلى بعض، وأن لا يبقى وضع الأعضاء وترتيبها على نسبة واحدة لكون المنى رطوبة سيالة لا يحفظ الوضع والترتيب. لم فإن قيل : إنما يمتنع اختلاف آثار القوة العديمة الشعور، في المادة الواحدة لو تفد القوة المفصلة فيها تمييز أجزاء ، واختلاف مواد للأعضاء . · قلنا : فيعود الكلام إلى القوة المفصلة . فإن اعترفوا بأن القوى في مرتبة الوسائط والآلات لا الفواعل والمؤثرات والمؤثر إنما هو خالقها القادر المختار الفعال لما يشاء، فقد اهتدوا ولم يبق سبيل إلى إثبات القوى والحاصل أن ما يدرك بعلم التشريح من الصور والكيفيات والأوضاع في بدن الإنسان يمتنع أن يجعل فعل القوة المصورة في مادة المنى إما من جهة الفاعل فلكونه عديم الشعور وإما من جهة القابل، فلكونه متشابهاً . • وقد يجاب عن الأول بأنه استبعاد وإنما يمتنع لولم يكن ذلك بإذن خالقها، بمعنى أنه خلقها كذلك، وأوجدها كذلك. وعن الثاني : بأنه لو سلّم بساطة القوة المصورة، وتشابه أجزاء المنى، فلاخفاء في أنه من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع وحينئذ لا يلزم أن يكون الحيوان كرة أو كرات، إذ لا يلزم أن يكون فعل القوة في المركب فعلها في واحد واحد من الأجزاء.
Based on the first (scenario), it would be necessary that the shape produced by the action of the formative power (al-quwwa al-musawwira) in the semen is a sphere, as is the case with the action of an unconscious power in uniform matter. And based on the second (scenario), it would be necessary that the result is spheres joined to one another, and that the position and arrangement of the limbs do not remain in a single proportion because semen is a fluid moisture that does not preserve position and arrangement. 
If it is said: The difference of effects from an unconscious power is impossible only if the detailed power (al-quwwa al-mufaṣṣila) does not distinguish between parts and the materials of the limbs are different. 
We say: Then the discussion returns to the detailed power. If they admit that the powers are in the rank of intermediaries and instruments, not agents and effectors, and that the true agent is only their Creator, the Powerful, the Chosen, the Doer of whatever He wills, then they have been guided and there remains no way to prove the powers. The conclusion is that what is perceived through the science of anatomy in terms of forms, qualities, and arrangements in the human body is impossible to attribute to the action of the formative power in the matter of semen, either from the aspect of the agent, because it is unconscious, or from the aspect of the recipient, because it is uniform. 
And it may be answered regarding the first (objection) that it is a mere improbability, and it would be impossible only if it were not by the permission of its Creator, meaning that He created it thus and brought it into existence thus. And regarding the second (objection): If the simplicity of the formative power and the uniformity of the parts of semen are granted, there is no concealment that it (semen) consists of bodies of different natures, and then it is not necessary that the animal be a sphere or spheres, since it is not necessary that the action of the power in the composite be its action in each individual part.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-answer pair regarding the philosophical claim that the formative power (al-quwwa al-musawwira) in the semen produces the complex shape of the fetus. The objector argues from two scenarios: if semen were uniform matter and the power unconscious, the resulting shape would be simple (a sphere) or a cluster of spheres, not the intricate arrangement of limbs. The answer first notes that this assumes the power is unconscious; if the objector concedes that powers are mere instruments and that God is the true agent, then the difficulty disappears. Then a second answer addresses the uniformity of semen: even if the power is simple and semen uniform, semen is composed of substances with different natures, so the power's effect on the whole composite need not be the same as on each component part, thus avoiding the conclusion that the fetus must be spherical.

Key Terms
القوة المصورة
القوة المفصلة
المنى
المادة المتشابهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why can't the formative power produce the complex shape of the fetus?
A) Because the power is unconscious and the matter is uniform.
B) Because the power is too weak.
C) Because the matter is heterogeneous.
D) Because the power is immaterial.

Key Takeaway
The objections argue that an unconscious power acting on uniform or fluid matter cannot produce the complex, stable shape of the human body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the intricate structure of your own body—your limbs, organs, and their precise arrangement—do you truly believe it emerged from an unconscious, formless substance? If not, what does that mean for your relationship with the One who gave you your form?
?
The passage argues that fluid matter cannot hold a stable shape. Your own life is fluid, full of changing circumstances and desires. What is the 'arrangement' that gives your life coherence? Are you consciously submitting to the Designer, or are you letting the shifting tides of your own impulses dictate your form?
?
If an unconscious power cannot produce order, then what does the order in your daily worship—the specific movements and recitations—tell you about the One who prescribed them? Are you performing them as a routine produced by habit, or as a deliberate act shaped by a conscious Master?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
7 min
Answering the Objections: Powers as Instruments and the Composite Nature of Semen
Responses to the objections and conclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two-fold response to the objections: first, by redefining powers as instruments of God, and second, by appealing to the composite nature of semen.

Core Idea
The true agent is God; powers are merely instruments, and semen's composite nature allows varied effects.

Having presented the objections, Taftazani now offers the answers. The first answer shifts the causal framework: if the objector concedes that powers are merely instruments and God is the true agent, the difficulty dissolves. The second answer addresses the uniformity of semen, noting that it is composed of different natures, so the power's effect on the composite is not limited to one simple shape.

Guided Path
1
Read the response chain
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the dialectical structure: 'If it is said... We say...' and then the final conclusion.
The argument moves through several stages: from the detailed power to the admission of God as agent, then the specific answers.
2
Identify the first answer
The first answer involves conceding that powers are intermediaries; the true agent is God. This removes the necessity that the power must produce spherical shapes.
This answer shifts the theological framework from natural causation to divine causation.
3
Identify the second answer
The second answer addresses the uniformity of semen: even if the power is simple and semen uniform, the semen is composed of different natures, so the power's action on the whole can differ from its action on parts.
This answer preserves the possibility of complex shapes without requiring multiple powers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى الأول: يلزم أن يكون الشكل الحادث من فعل المصورة في المنى هو الكرة على ما هو شأن فعل القوة الغير الشاعرة في المادة المتشابهة . وعلى الثاني : يلزم أن يكون الحاصل كرات مضمومة بعضها إلى بعض، وأن لا يبقى وضع الأعضاء وترتيبها على نسبة واحدة لكون المنى رطوبة سيالة لا يحفظ الوضع والترتيب. لم فإن قيل : إنما يمتنع اختلاف آثار القوة العديمة الشعور، في المادة الواحدة لو تفد القوة المفصلة فيها تمييز أجزاء ، واختلاف مواد للأعضاء . · قلنا : فيعود الكلام إلى القوة المفصلة . فإن اعترفوا بأن القوى في مرتبة الوسائط والآلات لا الفواعل والمؤثرات والمؤثر إنما هو خالقها القادر المختار الفعال لما يشاء، فقد اهتدوا ولم يبق سبيل إلى إثبات القوى والحاصل أن ما يدرك بعلم التشريح من الصور والكيفيات والأوضاع في بدن الإنسان يمتنع أن يجعل فعل القوة المصورة في مادة المنى إما من جهة الفاعل فلكونه عديم الشعور وإما من جهة القابل، فلكونه متشابهاً . • وقد يجاب عن الأول بأنه استبعاد وإنما يمتنع لولم يكن ذلك بإذن خالقها، بمعنى أنه خلقها كذلك، وأوجدها كذلك. وعن الثاني : بأنه لو سلّم بساطة القوة المصورة، وتشابه أجزاء المنى، فلاخفاء في أنه من أجسام مختلفة الطبائع وحينئذ لا يلزم أن يكون الحيوان كرة أو كرات، إذ لا يلزم أن يكون فعل القوة في المركب فعلها في واحد واحد من الأجزاء.
Based on the first (scenario), it would be necessary that the shape produced by the action of the formative power (al-quwwa al-musawwira) in the semen is a sphere, as is the case with the action of an unconscious power in uniform matter. And based on the second (scenario), it would be necessary that the result is spheres joined to one another, and that the position and arrangement of the limbs do not remain in a single proportion because semen is a fluid moisture that does not preserve position and arrangement. 
If it is said: The difference of effects from an unconscious power is impossible only if the detailed power (al-quwwa al-mufaṣṣila) does not distinguish between parts and the materials of the limbs are different. 
We say: Then the discussion returns to the detailed power. If they admit that the powers are in the rank of intermediaries and instruments, not agents and effectors, and that the true agent is only their Creator, the Powerful, the Chosen, the Doer of whatever He wills, then they have been guided and there remains no way to prove the powers. The conclusion is that what is perceived through the science of anatomy in terms of forms, qualities, and arrangements in the human body is impossible to attribute to the action of the formative power in the matter of semen, either from the aspect of the agent, because it is unconscious, or from the aspect of the recipient, because it is uniform. 
And it may be answered regarding the first (objection) that it is a mere improbability, and it would be impossible only if it were not by the permission of its Creator, meaning that He created it thus and brought it into existence thus. And regarding the second (objection): If the simplicity of the formative power and the uniformity of the parts of semen are granted, there is no concealment that it (semen) consists of bodies of different natures, and then it is not necessary that the animal be a sphere or spheres, since it is not necessary that the action of the power in the composite be its action in each individual part.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents an objection-answer pair regarding the philosophical claim that the formative power (al-quwwa al-musawwira) in the semen produces the complex shape of the fetus. The objector argues from two scenarios: if semen were uniform matter and the power unconscious, the resulting shape would be simple (a sphere) or a cluster of spheres, not the intricate arrangement of limbs. The answer first notes that this assumes the power is unconscious; if the objector concedes that powers are mere instruments and that God is the true agent, then the difficulty disappears. Then a second answer addresses the uniformity of semen: even if the power is simple and semen uniform, semen is composed of substances with different natures, so the power's effect on the whole composite need not be the same as on each component part, thus avoiding the conclusion that the fetus must be spherical.

Key Terms
القوة المصورة
القوة المفصلة
المنى
المادة المتشابهة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is anti-scientific and rejects natural causes.
Correction: The argument does not deny natural causes; it reinterprets them as instruments of God's power, a common theological stance in kalam.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why is the first objection only an improbability?
A) Because the power is unconscious.
B) Because God could have created the power to produce such effects.
C) Because the matter is uniform.
D) Because the detailed power intervenes.

Key Takeaway
The objections are answered by affirming God as the true agent and noting the composite nature of semen, allowing complex forms without requiring a spherical fetus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, you often attribute events to secondary causes—your effort, your intelligence, other people's actions. After reading that 'the true agent is only the Creator,' are you willing to see these as mere instruments? What would change in your reliance on them?
?
The objector claimed that an unconscious power cannot produce complex forms, and the answer was that it is possible by God's permission. Is your trust in God's permission such that you can accept His will even when natural explanations seem sufficient? Or do you unconsciously treat natural causes as independent?
?
If all powers are instruments of God, then your own power to choose and act is also an instrument. Does that thought humble you? Or does it feel like a threat to your autonomy? Ask yourself: are you ready to live as a servant whose every 'act' is really God's creation?

The Objection: Powers as Instruments Before the Soul
Taftazani states the objection against the philosophers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection that the powers of the soul cannot exist or act before the soul itself comes into being.

Core Idea
If powers are instruments, they cannot operate before the soul exists.

Before exploring the answers, we first need to understand the problem: if the soul's powers are its tools and servants, how can they be at work forming the body before the soul arrives? Taftazani sets up this objection to show the tension in the philosophers' account.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation. The objection has two parts: (1) powers as instruments cannot originate before the soul, and (2) their acting on their own would only make sense if the soul arises after the body.
Notice the term 'al-tanaffus' (ensoulment) is key.
2
Identify the core premise
Ask yourself: what assumption underlies the objection? The premise is that instruments cannot function independently of their user.
This is a logical objection, not an empirical one.
3
Connect to the bigger debate
Consider that this objection targets the philosophers' view that the soul's powers (like the generative and formative) act during embryonic development.
The next lessons show how philosophers disagreed and how a resolution was proposed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما الاعتراض) بأن قوى النفس آلات لها وخوادم فيمتنع حدوثها قبل التنفس، وفعلها بذاتها فإنما يتوجه لو جعل النفس حادثة بعد البدن والمصورة عن قوى نفس المولد، كالغاذية والنامية. ودل على اضطرابهم في ذلك، اضطرابهم في أن الجامع للأجزاء، والحافظ لها ماذا ؟ فذكر الإمام أن الجامع لأجزاء بدن الجنين نفس الأبوين، ثم يبقى المزاج في تدبير نفس الأم إلى أن يستعد كحدوث نفس تكون هي الحافظة له والجامعة لسائر الأجزاء. ونقل ابن سينا أن الجامع نفس الأبوين، والحافظ للاجتماع أولاً القوة المصورة لذلك البدن، ثم نفسه الناطقة . وصرح في الشفاء بأن الجامع للاسطقسات بدن كل حيوان والمؤلف لها على ما يصلح والحافظ لنظامه على ما ينبغي هي النفس التي له، والأشبه ما قيل، أن المتصرف أولاً نفس الأبوين بقواها إلى أن يفرز من الأخلاط، ما يصلح مادة للمنى، وبعدها لصورة تحفظ مزاجه، ثم يتكامل في الرحم إلى أن يستعد لنفس يصدر عنها، مع حفظ المزاج الأفعال النباتية، فتجذب الغذاء إلى تلك المادة، وتعدها لقبول نفس يصدر عنها مع ما سبق الأفعال الحيوانية، وهكذا إلى الناطقة].
He said: (As for the objection) that the powers of the soul are its instruments and servants, so their occurrence before ensoulment is impossible, and their acting by themselves is only directed if the soul is made to occur after the body, and the formative power comes from the powers of the generative soul, like the nutritive and growth powers. And their confusion about that is indicated by their confusion about what unites the parts and preserves them. For al-Imam (al-Razi) mentioned that the unifier of the parts of the fetus's body is the soul of the parents, then the mixture remains under the management of the mother's soul until it becomes ready for the occurrence of a soul that would be the preserver of it and the unifier of the remaining parts. And Ibn Sina transmitted that the unifier is the soul of the parents, and the preserver of the union initially is the formative power of that body, then its rational soul. And he stated explicitly in al-Shifa that the unifier of the elements of every animal's body, the composer of them as is appropriate, and the preserver of its system as it should be is the soul that belongs to it. And the most plausible view is what was said: that the first director is the soul of the parents with its powers until there is secreted from the humors what is suitable as matter for the semen, and after that (the semen) for a form that preserves its mixture, then it develops in the womb until it becomes ready for a soul from which, along with the preservation of the mixture, the vegetative actions proceed, so it attracts nourishment to that matter and prepares it to accept a soul from which proceed, along with what preceded, the animal actions, and so on until the rational soul].
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents an objection regarding the temporal priority of the soul's powers. The objector argues that if the generative and formative powers are instruments of the soul, they cannot exist or act before the soul comes into being, yet philosophers claim these powers operate during embryonic development before the soul is present. Taftazani then shows the confusion among philosophers by citing differing views on what unites and preserves the parts of the fetus. Al-Imam al-Razi holds that the parents' soul unites the parts initially, then the mother's soul manages the mixture until a new soul emerges. Ibn Sina says the parents' soul unites, but the formative power initially preserves the union, then the rational soul takes over. In al-Shifa, Ibn Sina states that the soul itself is the unifier and preserver from the start. The preferred view combines these: the parents' soul directs initially with its powers until the semen is formed, then a form-preserving mixture develops, leading eventually to vegetative, then animal, then rational souls.

Key Terms
قوى النفس
المولدة
المصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the soul and its powers are created simultaneously at conception.
Correction: The objection shows that if powers are instruments, they must come after the soul, not before. The philosophers' account implies powers act before the soul, which is contradictory.

Checkpoint
According to the objection, why is it impossible for the soul's powers to occur before ensoulment?
A) Because the powers are independent entities.
B) Because the powers are instruments and servants of the soul.
C) Because the soul is created at the same time as the body.
D) Because the powers are not needed for development.

Key Takeaway
The objection highlights a logical problem: if the soul's powers are its instruments, they cannot function before the soul exists.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your spiritual powers (like gratitude, patience, or trust in God) are merely instruments you use, they cannot operate before you truly connect with your soul. When was the last time you tried to act in a way that felt like using a tool without the soul behind it? What was the result, and did it feel hollow?
?
The objector argues that if powers act on their own, then the soul would come after the body. Similarly, if you perform acts of worship mechanically without the soul's presence, are you effectively putting your actions before your inner connection? How can you reverse this order to let your soul lead your worship?
?
Consider the formative power (al-musawwira) from the generative soul. This suggests that some powers can exist from another source before your rational soul fully emerges. What external influences—culture, family, habits—shape your 'soul' before you even choose? How can you ensure that your core is formed by sincere devotion rather than passive acceptance?

Philosophers' Confusion on Uniting and Preserving the Fetus
Taftazani enumerates three conflicting views on the unifier and preserver of the fetus

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the disagreement among philosophers about what unites and preserves the parts of the fetus during development.

Core Idea
Philosophers gave conflicting accounts of what unites and preserves the fetus.

After presenting the objection, Taftazani shows that the philosophers themselves are inconsistent. They disagree on the identity of the 'unifier' (al-jāmiʿ) and 'preserver' (al-ḥāfiẓ) of the fetus's parts. This confusion weakens their position.

Guided Path
1
Read the three views
Read the Arabic and translation. Identify the views of al-Imam al-Razi, the transmitted view of Ibn Sina, and the explicit statement in al-Shifa.
Notice how each view assigns a different agent for uniting and preserving.
2
Compare the views
Al-Razi says the parents' soul unites, then the mother's soul manages. Ibn Sina says the parents' soul unites, but the formative power initially preserves, then the rational soul. Al-Shifa says the soul itself does everything.
Ask which agent is emphasized: parents' soul, formative power, or the fetus's own soul?
3
Identify the confusion
The existence of three different accounts indicates that the philosophers were not certain. This is the 'confusion' (iḍṭirāb) Taftazani points out.
This supports the idea that the philosophers' theory of the soul is unsettled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما الاعتراض) بأن قوى النفس آلات لها وخوادم فيمتنع حدوثها قبل التنفس، وفعلها بذاتها فإنما يتوجه لو جعل النفس حادثة بعد البدن والمصورة عن قوى نفس المولد، كالغاذية والنامية. ودل على اضطرابهم في ذلك، اضطرابهم في أن الجامع للأجزاء، والحافظ لها ماذا ؟ فذكر الإمام أن الجامع لأجزاء بدن الجنين نفس الأبوين، ثم يبقى المزاج في تدبير نفس الأم إلى أن يستعد كحدوث نفس تكون هي الحافظة له والجامعة لسائر الأجزاء. ونقل ابن سينا أن الجامع نفس الأبوين، والحافظ للاجتماع أولاً القوة المصورة لذلك البدن، ثم نفسه الناطقة . وصرح في الشفاء بأن الجامع للاسطقسات بدن كل حيوان والمؤلف لها على ما يصلح والحافظ لنظامه على ما ينبغي هي النفس التي له، والأشبه ما قيل، أن المتصرف أولاً نفس الأبوين بقواها إلى أن يفرز من الأخلاط، ما يصلح مادة للمنى، وبعدها لصورة تحفظ مزاجه، ثم يتكامل في الرحم إلى أن يستعد لنفس يصدر عنها، مع حفظ المزاج الأفعال النباتية، فتجذب الغذاء إلى تلك المادة، وتعدها لقبول نفس يصدر عنها مع ما سبق الأفعال الحيوانية، وهكذا إلى الناطقة].

For al-Imam (al-Razi) mentioned that the unifier of the parts of the fetus's body is the soul of the parents, then the mixture remains under the management of the mother's soul until it becomes ready for the occurrence of a soul that would be the preserver of it and the unifier of the remaining parts. And Ibn Sina transmitted that the unifier is the soul of the parents, and the preserver of the union initially is the formative power of that body, then its rational soul. According to Ibn Sina's statement in al-Shifa, what is the unifier and preserver of the elements of an animal's body?
A) The soul of the parents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the complexity of your own creation and preservation, do you attribute it to shifting forces or to the constant care of a single, all-knowing Creator? How does this passage unsettle the assumption that human reason alone can give a consistent account of such matters?
?
The philosophers gave contradictory answers to what unifies and preserves your body. In your daily life, who or what do you actually trust to preserve your well-being and purpose? Are you resting on the same kind of shifting human opinion, or on something more certain?
?
If even the greatest minds disagreed on the source of your physical unity, what makes you think you can rely on your own understanding to unify your spiritual life? What would it mean for you to submit the ‘unifying’ of your soul to the One who created it?

The Preferred View: Gradual Development of Souls
Taftazani presents the preferred view of gradual soul development

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the preferred account that reconciles the objection and the confusion through a stepwise emergence of vegetative, animal, and rational souls.

Core Idea
The parents' soul directs initially, then successive souls emerge as the fetus develops.

After highlighting the confusion, Taftazani presents the most plausible view (al-ashbah). This view offers a coherent developmental sequence: the parents' soul acts first via its powers, then a form-preserving mixture prepares for a vegetative soul, then an animal soul, and finally the rational soul. This accounts for the powers acting before the soul by attributing them to earlier souls.

Guided Path
1
Read the preferred view carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the stages: parents' soul secretion of semen, form preserves mixture, then vegetative actions, then animal actions, culminating in rational soul.
The key term 'al-mutaṣarrif' (the director) refers to the agent at each stage.
2
Identify the agents at each stage
First stage: parents' soul directs with its powers. Second stage: a form preserves the mixture. Third: a soul for vegetative actions. Fourth: a soul for animal actions. Fifth: rational soul.
The powers are never without a soul; they belong to whichever soul is currently directing.
3
Connect back to the objection
This view answers the objection: powers never act before a soul because the parents' soul (and subsequent souls) are present at each stage. There is no gap.
This is why it is 'plausible' – it avoids the original problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما الاعتراض) بأن قوى النفس آلات لها وخوادم فيمتنع حدوثها قبل التنفس، وفعلها بذاتها فإنما يتوجه لو جعل النفس حادثة بعد البدن والمصورة عن قوى نفس المولد، كالغاذية والنامية. ودل على اضطرابهم في ذلك، اضطرابهم في أن الجامع للأجزاء، والحافظ لها ماذا ؟ فذكر الإمام أن الجامع لأجزاء بدن الجنين نفس الأبوين، ثم يبقى المزاج في تدبير نفس الأم إلى أن يستعد كحدوث نفس تكون هي الحافظة له والجامعة لسائر الأجزاء. ونقل ابن سينا أن الجامع نفس الأبوين، والحافظ للاجتماع أولاً القوة المصورة لذلك البدن، ثم نفسه الناطقة . وصرح في الشفاء بأن الجامع للاسطقسات بدن كل حيوان والمؤلف لها على ما يصلح والحافظ لنظامه على ما ينبغي هي النفس التي له، والأشبه ما قيل، أن المتصرف أولاً نفس الأبوين بقواها إلى أن يفرز من الأخلاط، ما يصلح مادة للمنى، وبعدها لصورة تحفظ مزاجه، ثم يتكامل في الرحم إلى أن يستعد لنفس يصدر عنها، مع حفظ المزاج الأفعال النباتية، فتجذب الغذاء إلى تلك المادة، وتعدها لقبول نفس يصدر عنها مع ما سبق الأفعال الحيوانية، وهكذا إلى الناطقة].

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You were not always a rational soul. At one point, you were only a vegetative and animal soul. How does that humbling truth affect your pride in your intellect? Will you use your rational soul to know your Creator, or will you let it be enslaved by your animal desires?
?
The parents' soul directed your earliest moments without your awareness. Now you have a rational soul that can choose. Are you directing your own development toward God, or are you letting the 'soul of the world' dictate your path?
?
If every stage of your development required a new soul from Allah, how does that change your dependence on Him? Is there any moment when you can act without His creation of a new power within you?

Objection: Instrument Before User?
Taftazani presents the objection that the generative powers act before the soul and gives the beginning of an answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical objection that the instrument (e.g., generative powers) cannot precede its user (the soul) in existence, and how the beginning of the answer concedes some common ground.

Core Idea
An instrument cannot act before its user exists — that would be absurd.

The philosophers argue that the generative and formative powers are instruments of the soul. But if the soul comes into existence only after the bodily mixture, then those powers would exist and act before the soul. Taftazani presents this objection and then begins to formulate a response. This lesson examines the objection and the initial move of the answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through. Notice how Taftazani sets up the problem: the philosophers' own assumptions lead to a contradiction.
The passage is incomplete; the answer will continue in the next lesson.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms al-muwallida (generative), al-muṣawwira (formative), an-nafs (soul), al-āla (instrument), and dhū l-āla (instrument user). Understand their roles.
These terms are central to Islamic philosophical cosmology.
3
Consider the beginning of the answer
Taftazani starts his response by conceding that the soul is not eternal — this removes one possible escape for the philosophers. Note that the full answer is cut off.
The answer will likely argue that the powers operate under divine direction even before the soul's creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يورد ههنا سؤال. وهو أن الفلاسفة يجعلون المولدة والمصورة وغيرهما قوى للنفس وآلات لها والنفس حادثة بعد حدوث المزاج وتمام صور الأعضاء فالقول باستناد صور الأعضاء إلى المصورة قول بحدوث الآلة قبل ذي الآلة وفعلها بنفسها من غير مستعمل إياها وهو باطل. وجوابه بعد تسليم أن النفس ليست بقديمة كما هو رأي بعض الفلاسفة ولا
A question is raised here. It is that the philosophers make the generative (al-muwallida) and formative (al-muṣawwira) and other powers as powers for the soul and instruments for it, while the soul occurs after the occurrence of the mixture and the completion of the forms of the limbs. So the statement that the forms of the limbs are attributed to the formative power is a statement that the instrument occurs before the one who uses it (dhū l-āla), and its action by itself without a user, which is false. Its answer, after conceding that the soul is not eternal, as is the view of some philosophers, and not
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents another objection against the philosophers. The objection states that since the philosophers claim the generative and formative powers are instruments of the soul, and the soul only comes into existence after the bodily mixture and the completion of the limb forms, then attributing the formation of the limbs to the formative power would mean the instrument exists and acts before its user (the soul), which is absurd. The answer begins by conceding that the soul is not eternal (as some philosophers claim), but the text cuts off before the full response is given. The implication is that the answer will show how the instruments can still operate under divine direction or through a different causal framework.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence mentioned in the objection?
A) The soul exists without a body.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The objection shows the absurdity of an instrument acting without its user. Where in your worship are you performing acts mechanically, without the presence of your heart (the user)? How can you ensure your soul is present before your body acts?

Accidental Powers Cannot Produce Precise Actions
Argues that precise natural actions cannot emanate from accidental powers without divine permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the precise and ordered actions in nature cannot be attributed to accidental powers lacking knowledge and will, and why they point to a powerful, wise Creator.

Core Idea
Precise natural order demands a Creator, not blind accidents.

This lesson explores a powerful argument from design: the intricate forms and processes in nature are too perfect to be caused by mere accidents inherent in physical organs. The author challenges the sufficiency of accidental powers (like 'formative power') and invokes both rational intuition and Qur'anic authority to show that all such actions depend on divine permission.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the rhetorical questions and the appeal to wonder at nature's complexity.
2
Identify the author's main objection
Find why the author rejects the idea that accidental powers can produce precise actions. Look for terms like 'قدرة', 'إرادة', 'علم'.
3
Observe the shift to scriptural and philosophical support
Notice how the author cites the Qur'an and later philosophers who returned to 'sound nature' to affirm divine permission.
This strengthens the argument from reason with revelation.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Ask yourself: If even philosophers admit these powers act by permission, what does that imply about accidental powers as sufficient causes?
The passage concludes they are not sufficient; the true cause is Allah.

ومنها : أنه كيف تصدر هذه الأفعال المتقنة المحكمة على النظام المخصوص عن القوى، التي هي أعراض قائمة بالأعضاء لا يتصور لها قدرة أو إرادة أو علم، خصوصاً إذا تؤمل في الصور العجيبة، والأشكال الغريبة، والنقوش المؤتلفة، والألوان المختلفة الموجودة في أنواع النبات والحيوان، فإن العقل لا يكاد يذعن لصدورها عن القوة التي سموها مصورة. وإن فرضنا كونها مركبة، وكون المواد مختلفة. كيف وقد ورد الكتاب الإلهي في عدة مواضع ، باستناد جميع ذلك إلى الله سبحانه ، وأشار إلى دلالتها على كونه قادراً حكيماً ، وصانعاً قديماً. والفلاسفة أيضاً لما رجعوا إلى الفطرة السليمة. صرحوا بأن هذه القوى، إنما تفعل ذلك بإذن خالقها القدير وموجدها الحكيم الخبير.
And among them: how can these precise, well-ordered actions according to a particular system emanate from powers that are accidents subsisting in organs, which cannot be imagined to have power, will, or knowledge, especially when one considers the wonderful forms, strange shapes, harmonious designs, and varied colors found in the types of plants and animals? For the intellect can hardly assent to their emanation from the power they call 'formative'. Even if we suppose them to be composite and the materials diverse. How, when the divine Book has stated in several places ascribing all that to God, and indicated that they indicate His being powerful, wise, and an eternal Maker. And the philosophers also, when they returned to sound nature, declared that these powers only do that by the permission of their powerful Creator and wise, all-knowing Originator.

Commentary
This passage presents an objection against the sufficiency of accidental powers to produce such precise and well-ordered actions. The author argues that the actions of nutrition, growth, and generation are too perfect and systematic to be performed by mere accidents (a‘rād) that inhere in organs, since accidents lack power, will, and knowledge. He emphasizes the amazing forms and colors in nature, which defy attribution to a mere 'formative power'. Even if we assume composite materials, the intellect struggles to accept such order from such weak powers. He then cites the Qur'an, which attributes all these processes to God, indicating His power and wisdom. Finally, he notes that the philosophers themselves, when guided by sound nature, admitted that these powers act only by the permission of their powerful and wise Creator.

Necessity of Perception for Vegetative Actions and the Nature of Life
Proves that vegetative actions require perception, and concludes that plants are not alive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the precise actions of vegetative powers require a perception distinct from the human soul, and why plants are not considered alive because life necessitates sensation.

Core Idea
Life requires sensation; plants lack it, so they aren't alive.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid presents a subtle argument for the necessity of perception in the seemingly automatic actions of nutrition, growth, and generation. The author distinguishes this perception from the human soul and from animal perception, concluding that such perception belongs to a different category. In a concluding remark, he states that plants are not alive because life is defined by the capacity for sensation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to understand the overall argument.
Notice the flow from proof to conclusion.
2
Identify key claims
Identify the three main claims: (1) perception is necessary for vegetative movements, (2) this perception is not from the human soul, (3) plants are not alive because life requires sensation.
The author uses a logical sequence: if perception is needed, and it's not human or animal, then it must be another perception; thus plants, lacking sensation, are not alive.
3
Reflect on the definition of life
Consider how the author defines life restrictively as requiring sensation. Compare this with common understanding that growth implies life.
This challenges the misconception that growth alone indicates life.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال، نحن نعلم قطعاً أن ما في التغذية والتنمية والتوليد من الحركات، إلى الجهات المختلفة، ومن الإلصاقات ومن التشكيلات لا يصح بدون الإدراك. وأن هذا الإدراك ليس للنفس الإنسانية، فإن هذه الأفعال دائمة في البدن، والنفس غافلة عنها، وتحدس حدساً موجباً لليقين، أن الحيوانات العجم أيضاً، لا تدرك أفعال هذه القوى في أبدانها . فإذن هو إدراك موجود آخر له اعتبار بهذه الأنواع . قال (خاتمة) النبات كما أنه ليس بحيوان، ليس بحي، لأن الحياة صفة تقتضي الحس
And among them is one who said: We know with certainty that the movements in nutrition, growth, and generation towards different directions, and the attachments and formations cannot be valid without perception. And this perception is not that of the human soul, for these actions are continuous in the body while the soul is oblivious of them. And one conjectures with certainty that non-human animals also do not perceive the actions of these powers in their bodies. Therefore, it is another existing perception that has consideration for these types. He said (Conclusion): The plant, as it is not an animal, is not living, because life is an attribute that necessitates sensation.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the necessity of perception in the precise actions of the vegetative powers. The author reports that some scholars affirm certainty that the movements, attachments, and formations in nutrition, growth, and generation cannot occur without perception. However, this perception is not from the human soul because these actions are continuous while the soul is unaware. Similarly, non-human animals do not perceive these internal processes. Hence, there must be another perception responsible. The passage then provides a concluding remark (khātima) that plants are not alive, because life requires sensation. This distinguishes plants from animals and defines life as requiring sense-perception.

Plants are not alive because life, by definition, requires sensation, which plants lack.

Vegetative Powers Require an Underlying Perception
Argument that vegetative actions require a non-human perception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the precise and orderly actions of vegetative powers demand an underlying perception that is not the human soul.

Core Idea
Vegetative actions demand a perception beyond human awareness.

This passage challenges the notion that the intricate, well-ordered processes of nutrition, growth, and generation in plants and animals can be explained by blind physical forces alone. Through a series of arguments—from the precision of actions, the Quranic attribution to God, and the soul's unawareness—the author establishes that these processes require an underlying perception, distinct from the human soul and possibly a lower form of consciousness or divine agency.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without worrying about details. Then read the translation. Notice the flow from the initial comment about Ibn Sīnā to the main arguments.
The passage is dense; take your time with each sentence.
2
Identify the core argument
The author argues that the precise and orderly actions of vegetative powers cannot proceed from mere physical powers (accidents in organs) because those lack power, will, and knowledge. He then supports this with Quranic references and philosophical reasoning.
The key claim: these actions require an 'other existing perception'.
3
Analyze the three lines of evidence
Break down the passage into three evidences: (1) The intrinsic complexity of vegetative forms, (2) The Quranic attribution to God, (3) The soul's continuous actions without its own awareness, proving the perception is not human.
Each evidence strengthens the conclusion.

The three. And Ibn Sīnā inclines to the second, and it is apparent from their principles. Then they are the three powers themselves, not another power that we use, because there is no other action besides the bringing of substitution, likeness, and attachment. And among them: how can these precise, firm actions in the specific order proceed from powers that are accidents subsisting in organs, for which power, will, or knowledge cannot be conceived, especially when one contemplates the wondrous forms, strange shapes, harmonious patterns, and diverse colors existing in the types of plants and animals? For the intellect almost cannot [?] about their proceeding from the power they called the formative. And if we suppose them composed and the materials different, how? And the Divine Book has come in several places attributing all that to God, exalted, and indicated its indication that He is powerful, wise, and an ancient maker. And the philosophers also, when they returned to sound nature, stated that these powers only do that by the permission of their powerful Creator and wise knowing Originator. And among them is one who said: We know with certainty that what is in nutrition, growth, and generation of movements to different directions, attachments, and formations is not valid without perception. And that this perception is not of the human soul (2), for these actions are continuous in the body while the soul is heedless of them, and it conjectures a conjecture necessitating certainty that the dumb animals also do not perceive the actions of these powers in their bodies. So then it is another existing perception that has regard to these types.
Source pages p. 263

Commentary
This passage is an argument that the precise actions of the vegetative faculty cannot arise from mere physical powers lacking perception. The author first notes that Ibn Sīnā preferred the second view (that the three powers are the same powers, not separate). Then he raises an objection: how can the intricate, well-ordered actions—such as forming wondrous shapes and patterns in plants and animals—come from powers that are accidents in organs, without power, will, or knowledge? The Quran itself attributes all this to God, indicating His power and wisdom. Philosophers, when following sound nature, admit that these powers act only by God's permission. Another argument is that we know for certain that the movements in nutrition, growth, and generation require perception, but this perception is not from the human soul because the soul is unaware of these continuous bodily actions. Even dumb animals do not perceive these internal processes. Therefore, there must be another existing perception that supervises these types. The passage thus establishes an underlying perception that is not human, possibly a vegetable soul or divine agency.

Proof and Refutation of the Vital Force
Physicians define vital force, prove it from paralysis/atrophy, and face objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how physicians prove the existence of a distinct vital force from cases of paralysis and atrophy, and how objections to this proof are answered.

Core Idea
The vital force is inferred from the preservation of life where other forces fail.

Physicians argue that beyond sensation, movement, and nutrition, a separate vital force preserves life in limbs. This lesson examines their definition, their evidence from paralyzed and wasted limbs, and the two main philosophical objections to their argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the physicians' definition and evidence
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the vital force is defined as preparing limbs for sensation, movement, and life functions, and how paralysis and atrophy are used as evidence.
The key example: a paralyzed limb lacks movement but stays alive, suggesting a force beyond the motor force.
2
Examine the two objections and responses
Identify the first objection (that the missing forces may exist but be blocked) and the second objection (that preservation could be due to temperament or soul). See how the author answers each.
The response to the first objection appeals to the philosophers' own admission; the second clarifies what is meant by 'soul'.
3
Reflect on the underlying assumptions
Consider why this argument matters: it assumes a one-to-one correspondence between forces and their effects, and debates causation in living bodies.
This connects to broader issues in kalam and philosophy about causality and the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المفلوج والذابل . يعني أن الأطباء يثبتون جنساً آخر من القوى يسمونها القوة الحيوانية ، ويجعلونها مبدأ القوى النفسانية، حيث يفسرونها بالقوة التي إذا حصلت في الأعضاء هيأتها لقبول الحس والحركة وأفعال الحياة كجعل الغذاء بحيث يصلح لتغذية بدن الحيوان وكحركات الانبساط عند الغضب والفرح، والانقباض عند الخوف والغم. ويستدلون على ذلك، بأن في العضو المفلوج أو الذابل، قوة تحفظ عليه الحياة، وتمنعه النقص والفساد. وليست هي قوة الحس والحركة لفقدها في المفلوج، ولا قوة التغذية لفقدها في الذابل، فهي التي نسميها القوة الحيوانية . واعترض عليه من وجهين : أحدهما : أنا لا نسلم انتفاء قوة الحس والحركة في المفلوج، وقوة التغذية في الذابل لجواز أن توجد القوة، ولا يترقب عليها الفعل، لفقد شرط، أو وجود مانع، فإن قيل : لو انتفى الشرط أو وجد المانع ، لما ترتب حفظ الحياة . قلنا : يجوز أن يكون لبعض الشروط والموانع اختصاص ببعض الأفعال دون البعض . فإن قيل : القوة الواحدة لا تكون مبدأ إلا لفعل واحد. قلنا : فأي حاجة إلى ما ذكر من المقدمات . والجواب : أن الفلاسفة معترفون بانتفاء القوتين في المفلوج والذابل . وثانيهما: أن الحافظ يجوز أن يكون المزاج الخاص، أو تعلق النفس بالبدن . والجواب : أن الكلام فيما يحفظ المزاج الخاص الذي به قوام الحياة في الحيوان الناطق وغيره، وفيه نظر لأنهم لا يعنون بالنفس الجوهر المجرد ، بل مبدأ الحركات والأفاعيل المختلفة، أو مبدأ الإدراك، والتحريك الإرادي
The paralyzed and the atrophied. That is, the physicians affirm another genus of forces which they call the vital force, and they make it the principle of the psychic forces, interpreting it as the force which, when it settles in the limbs, prepares them to receive sensation and movement and the acts of life, such as making food suitable for nourishing the animal's body, and such as the movements of expansion at anger and joy, and contraction at fear and grief. They adduce evidence for that by the fact that in the paralyzed or atrophied limb there is a force that preserves life in it and prevents it from deficiency and corruption. It is not the force of sensation and movement because of their absence in the paralyzed, nor the nutritive force because of its absence in the atrophied; it is what we call the vital force. And an objection was raised against it from two aspects: First: We do not concede the absence of the force of sensation and movement in the paralyzed, and the nutritive force in the atrophied, because it is possible that the force exists but the effect does not follow due to the absence of a condition or the presence of an obstacle. If it is said: If the condition were absent or the obstacle present, the preservation of life would not follow. We say: It is possible that some conditions and obstacles are specific to some acts and not others. If it is said: A single force is only a principle for a single act. We say: What need is there for the premises mentioned? The answer: The philosophers admit the absence of the two forces in the paralyzed and the atrophied. Second: That which preserves could be the particular temperament or the attachment of the soul to the body. The answer: The discussion is about what preserves the particular temperament by which life subsists in the rational animal and others, and there is consideration because they do not mean by 'soul' the immaterial substance, but rather the principle of movements and various acts, or the principle of perception and voluntary motion.
Source pages p. 266

Commentary
This passage begins by explaining the physicians' concept of the vital force (al-quwwa al-ḥayawāniyya). They define it as a force that, when present in organs, prepares them to receive sensation, movement, and life functions. They argue for its existence from cases of paralysis (al-muflaj) and atrophy (al-dhābil): in a paralyzed limb, sensation and movement are absent, yet the limb remains alive; in an atrophied limb, nutrition is impaired, yet life is preserved. Thus, there must be a distinct vital force preserving life beyond sensory, motor, or nutritive powers. Two objections are then raised. The first objection denies that sensation/movement or nutritive forces are truly absent; they may exist but remain ineffective due to missing conditions or obstacles. The author answers this by noting that the philosophers themselves admit the absence of these forces in such cases. The second objection suggests that the preserving factor could be the particular temperament (al-mizāj al-khāṣṣ) or the soul's attachment (taʿalluq al-nafs) to the body. The answer counters that the discussion concerns what preserves the specific temperament that sustains life, and that the philosophers here do not mean the soul as an immaterial substance but rather as a principle of various motions and perceptions.

Key Terms
القوة الحيوانية
المفلوج
الذابل
القوى النفسانية
المزاج الخاص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the vital force is the same as the soul itself.
Correction: The author clarifies that the philosophers mean by 'soul' a principle of various motions and perceptions, not the immaterial substance.

Checkpoint
According to the physicians, what evidence do they use to prove the existence of a vital force distinct from sensory, motor, and nutritive powers?
A) The fact that a paralyzed limb cannot move but still lives.
B) The fact that an atrophied limb lacks nutrition but remains alive.
C) Both A and B.
D) The fact that animals have consciousness.

Key Takeaway
The existence of a vital force is inferred from the preservation of life in limbs lacking other forces, though philosophers question whether the same can be explained by missing conditions or temperament.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider the preservation of your own life moment by moment, do you attribute it to natural forces or to the direct will of Allah? How does the argument for a vital force challenge you to see the fragility of your existence and your utter dependence on the Creator?
?
The philosophers admit that the preservation of life might be due to the particular temperament or the soul's attachment. If your life depends on such subtle and unseen causes, what does that reveal about your vulnerability and your need to turn to the One who controls all causes?
?
If the vital force is merely a created principle that prepares your limbs for action, are you more aware of the Giver of that force than the force itself? In what daily habit do you forget that every heartbeat, every movement, is sustained by Allah's permission?

Defending the Need for Premises
al-Taftazani answers two objections concerning premises and the preservation of life.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani defends the need for premises by responding to two philosophical objections about the preservation of life.

Core Idea
Defending premises against objections shows the rigor of kalam reasoning.

In this passage, al-Taftazani directly addresses two objections that might undermine the earlier argument about the soul's independence. The first objection questions why all those premises were necessary; the second offers alternative explanations for the preservation of life. Understanding these responses shows how careful dialectic works in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the first objection and answer
Begin with the first objection: 'What need for the premises?' Then read the answer: philosophers themselves admit the absence of two powers in the paralyzed and withered. This shows the premises are not superfluous.
Notice how al-Taftazani uses his opponents' own admissions against them.
2
Read the second objection and answer
The second objection suggests the preserver could be the specific temperament or the soul's attachment. The answer clarifies that the real question is what preserves that temperament, so these proposals miss the point and need further examination.
3
Reflect on the dialectical strategy
Consider why al-Taftazani does not simply dismiss these objections. He engages them, showing that careful reasoning (not blind acceptance) is necessary even in responding to critics.
This reflects the broader method of kalam: rigorous defense of truth using logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قلنا : فأي حاجة إلى ما ذكر من المقدمات . والجواب : أن الفلاسفة معترفون بانتفاء القوتين في المفلوج والذابل . وثانيهما: أن الحافظ يجوز أن يكون المزاج الخاص، أو تعلق النفس بالبدن . والجواب : أن الكلام فيما يحفظ المزاج الخاص الذي به قوام الحياة في الحيوان الناطق وغيره، وفيه نظر .
We said: So what need is there for what was mentioned of the premises? The answer: The philosophers acknowledge the absence of the two powers in the paralyzed and the withered. And the second: That the preserver may be the specific temperament, or the soul's attachment to the body. The answer: The discussion concerns what preserves the specific temperament by which life subsists in the rational animal and others, and it requires consideration.
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is an exchange of objections and answers. First, an opponent asks why all these premises are needed. The answer is that philosophers themselves admit that in a paralyzed or withered person, the two powers (probably food-drawing and growth) are absent, showing the need for such premises. Then a second objection proposes two possible preservers of life: the specific temperament or the soul's attachment to the body. The answer clarifies that the real question is what maintains the specific temperament that is the basis of life in all animals, so these suggestions are inadequate and need further scrutiny.

Correction: Al-Taftazani engages the objections carefully, showing that they require thoughtful answers, not mere dismissal.

A) They have no need for premises.
B) The absence of the two powers.
C) The preserver is the soul.

?

?

Objections to the Imprint Theory of Vision and Their Answers
Two objections to the imprint theory of vision and their answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two main objections to the imprint theory of vision and how the author defends the theory against them.

Core Idea
The imprint theory is defended by distinguishing the object from its image and denying the necessity of equal magnitude.

The author has presented the theory that vision occurs by the imprint of an image of the object in the crystalline humor. This passage addresses two forceful objections. The first challenges the identity of the seen object, while the second concerns size. Understanding these objections and their answers is crucial for grasping the kalam position on vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to get a sense of the argument's flow.
Notice the two objections are introduced with 'أحدهما' and 'ثانيهما'.
2
Identify the two objections
Locate the first objection: that we would see only an image, not the real object. The second: that the image must equal the object in size, so large objects couldn't be seen.
Both objections are based on apparent implications of the imprint theory.
3
Analyze the answers
The answer to the first objection uses an analogy with knowledge: the visible is the object whose imprint is received, not the imprint itself. The second answer uses the example of a face in a small mirror to show that size equality is not necessary.
The author admits the modality of seeing large objects is unknown, but the theory is not refuted.
4
Connect to broader kalam themes
Reflect on how this defense maintains the empirical certainty that we see external objects themselves, a key point in kalam epistemology.
5
Test your understanding
Use the checkpoint question to verify your grasp of the first answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صورته في المرآة، وفي عين الناظر، ويكون استعداد حصوله بالمقابلة المخصوصة، مع توسط الهواء المشف. ولما اعترض على هذا بوجهين أحدهما أن المرئي حينئذ يكون صورة الشيء وشبحه لا نفسه، ونحن قاطعون بأنا نرى نفس هذا الملون. وثانيهما: أن شبح الشيء مساوله في المقدار، وإلا لم يكن صورة له ومثالاً. وحينئذ يلزم أن لا نرى ما هو أعظم من الجليدية، لأن امتناع انطباع العظيم في الصغير معلوم بالضرورة. أشار إلى الجواب بأنه إذا كان رؤية الشيء بانطباع شبحه، كان المرئي هو الذي انطبع شبحه لا نفس الشبح، كما مر في العلم، وبأن شبح الشيء لا يلزم أن يساويه في المقدار كما يشاهد من صورة الوجه في المرآة الصغيرة. إذ المراد به ما يناسب الشيء في الشكل واللون دون المقدار، غاية الأمر أنا لا نعرف كيفية إبصار الشيء العظيم، وإدراك البعد بينه وبين المرئي، بمجرد انطباع صورة صغيرة منه في الجليدية وتأديها بواسطة الزوح المصبوب في العصبتين إلى الباصرة.
His image in the mirror and in the eye of the beholder, and the readiness for its occurrence occurs through the specific facing, with the interposition of transparent air. And when an objection was raised against this on two grounds: one, that the visible then would be the image and phantasm of the thing, not its very self, while we are certain that we see this very colored thing. And the second: that the phantasm of a thing is equal to it in magnitude, otherwise it would not be an image and likeness of it. And then it would follow that we do not see what is greater than the crystalline humor, because the impossibility of the large being imprinted in the small is known necessarily. He indicated the answer: that if seeing a thing is by the imprint of its phantasm, then the visible is that whose phantasm was imprinted, not the phantasm itself, as passed in the case of knowledge; and that the phantasm of a thing does not necessarily equal it in magnitude, as is seen with the image of the face in a small mirror. For what is meant is that which corresponds to the thing in shape and color, not in magnitude. The utmost is that we do not know the modality of seeing a large thing and perceiving the distance between it and the visible, merely by the imprint of a small image of it in the crystalline humor and its conveyance through the spirit poured into the two optic nerves to the visual sensorium.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage presents two objections to the theory of vision by imprint (intibā') and their answers. The author first describes the theory: the image forms in the eye like in a mirror under specific conditions. The first objection: on this theory, we would see only an image, not the actual object, but we are certain we see the colored object itself. The answer distinguishes: what is seen is the object whose image is imprinted, not the image itself, similar to how knowledge is of the object. The second objection: the phantasm must equal the object in size, so we could not see anything larger than the eye. The answer: the phantasm need not be equal in magnitude, only in shape and color, like a face in a small mirror. The difficulty of how a small image conveys perception of size and distance is admitted as unknown but not theoretically impossible.

Key Terms
الانطباع (al-intibā')
المرئي (al-mar'ī)
الشبح (al-shabah)
الجليدية (al-jalīdiyya)
الباصرة (al-bāṣira)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imprint theory implies that we only see images or copies of objects, not the objects themselves.
Correction: The author clarifies that the visible is the object whose phantasm is imprinted; we see the object itself, not the phantasm. The imprint is the means, not the object of vision.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the first objection, what is actually seen when vision occurs by imprint?
A) The phantasm itself
B) The object whose phantasm is imprinted
C) The image in the mirror
D) The colored surface

Key Takeaway
The imprint theory of vision can withstand objections by distinguishing the object from its imprint and by denying that size equality is necessary, as illustrated by mirror reflections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are certain you see the actual colored object in front of you, but this lesson argues that the mechanism involves an image imprinted in your eye. How often do you mistake the representation of something for the reality in your own life—for example, judging a person by their appearance or a situation by its surface? Will you begin to question your immediate perceptions today?
?
The author admits we do not know the modality of how a small image conveys perception of large objects and distance. Are you comfortable not knowing how your own perception works? Does this humility challenge your demand for complete understanding before accepting truth? Where else in your faith have you demanded answers God has not given?
?
The objectors insisted that the phantasm must equal the object in size, imposing a condition on how vision must work. Where in your life do you impose unnecessary conditions on how Allah's creation must operate, limiting His power? Will you repent from dictating to the Creator the how of His acts?
