The Ray Theory of Vision and Its Variations
The author describes the ray theory of vision and its different formulations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core premise of the ray theory of vision and the three variations on how the ray interacts with the visible object.

Core Idea
The ray theory of vision proposes that sight occurs by a cone of light emitted from the eye.

The passage presents the basic ray theory of vision and its variations. Understanding these differences is important because they reflect different attempts to explain the mechanism of sight within the framework of natural philosophy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get a sense of the content.
2
Identify the basic premise
The passage states that vision is by emission of a ray from the eye in a conical shape, with apex at the eye and base at the object.
3
Note the three variations
Three variations are described: (1) cone solid vs. composed of lines, (2) straight and fluctuating, (3) the ray conditions the air.
Each variation offers a different mechanism for how the ray makes contact with the object.
4
Compare the variations
Reflect on how each variation addresses potential problems with the basic model, such as how the ray covers the entire object.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : إن الإبصار بخروج شعاع من العين على هيئة مخروط رأسه عند العين، وقاعدته عند المرئي. ثم اختلفوا في أن ذلك المخروط مصمت، أو مؤتلف من خطوط مجتمعة في الجانب الذي يلي الرأس متفرقة في الجانب الذي يلي القاعدة، وقيل : لا على هيئة المخروط بل على استواء، لكن يثبت طرفه الذي على العين، ويضطرب طرفه الاخر على المرئي . وقيل : الشعاع الذي في العين يكيف الهواء بكيفيته، ويصير الكل آلة في الإبصار.
And it was said: Vision is by the emission of a ray from the eye in the shape of a cone whose apex is at the eye and its base at the visible object. Then they disagreed: is that cone solid, or composed of lines gathered at the side near the head and diverging at the side near the base? And it was said: not in the shape of a cone but straight, yet its extremity at the eye is fixed while its other extremity fluctuates over the visible object. And it was said: the ray that is in the eye conditions the air with its quality, and the whole becomes an instrument for vision.
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage enumerates the variations of the ray (shu'ā') theory of vision. The basic idea is that vision occurs by a ray emanating from the eye in a conical form. The first variation: whether the cone is solid (a continuous medium) or composed of discrete lines that converge at the eye. The second: the ray is not conical but straight, but one end is fixed at the eye while the other scans the object. The third: the ray conditions the air, making the whole air an instrument for vision. These are different attempts to explain how the ray contacts the object.

Key Terms
الشعاع (al-shu'ā')
المخروط (al-makhrūṭ)
الإبصار (al-ibṣār)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All proponents of the ray theory proposed the same mechanism.
Correction: This passage shows three distinct variations, indicating significant disagreement among proponents.

Checkpoint
How many distinct formulations of the ray theory are mentioned in this passage?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Five

Key Takeaway
The ray theory of vision had multiple formulations, differing in how the ray contacts the object, indicating active debate among proponents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Third Theory and the Author's View on Vision
Presents a third theory of vision then states the author's own occasionalist view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theory of illuminative presential knowledge (al-ilm al-ishrāqī al-ḥuḍūrī) and the author's preferred occasionalist explanation of vision.

Core Idea
Vision is either a natural process or a direct act of God; the author chooses the latter.

This passage presents a third theory that explains vision through direct soul-knowledge rather than physical rays or imprints. After describing this theory, the author states his own view: vision is simply a direct creation of God. This lesson explores both positions and the reasoning behind the author's preference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the two distinct parts: the presentation of a third theory and the author's own view.
Notice the shift from reported speech (قيل) to the author's assertion (والحق).
2
Analyze the third theory
Identify the conditions for vision according to this theory: a facing luminous object, a polished moisture in the eye, removal of obstacles, and the resulting illuminative presential knowledge.
This theory rejects both ray emission and imprint reception.
3
Understand the author's view
The author asserts that vision is purely by God's creation when the eye opens. Contrast this with the naturalistic explanations of the other theories.
This reflects an occasionalist worldview where God is the direct cause of all events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : لا شعاع ولا انطباع . وإنما الإبصار بمقابلة المستنير للعضو الباصر، الذي فيه رطوبة صقيلة. فإذا وجدت هذه الشروط مع زوال الموانع ، يقع للنفس علم إشراقي بحضوري على المبصر، فتدركه النفس مشاهدة ظاهرة جلية . والحق: أن الإبصار بمحض خلق الله تعالى عند فتح العين .
And it was said: neither ray nor imprint. Rather, vision is by the luminous object facing the visual organ, which contains a polished moisture. When these conditions are met along with the removal of obstacles, an illuminative presential knowledge occurs to the soul regarding the object, and the soul perceives it as a clear and manifest vision. And the truth is: vision is by the pure creation of God Most High when the eye is opened.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage first reports a third theory: vision occurs not by ray or imprint, but by the facing of the luminous object to the eye, which has a polished moisture. When conditions are met and obstacles removed, the soul directly perceives the object through an illuminative presential knowledge (ilm ishrāqī ḥuḍūrī). The author then states his own preferred view (al-ḥaqq): vision is simply by God's direct creation at the moment the eye opens, asserting occasionalism and rejecting all naturalistic explanations.

Key Terms
العلم الإشراقي الحضوري (al-ilm al-ishrāqī al-ḥuḍūrī)
خلق الله (khalq Allāh)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision is solely a physical process (ray emission or imprint) independent of divine intervention.
Correction: The author teaches that vision is directly created by God, not caused by any natural mechanism.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Vision is ultimately an act of God's direct creation, not a natural process.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Arguments for the Imprint Theory and Their Weakness
The author criticizes the imprint theory's proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main arguments for the imprint theory of vision and why the author finds them insufficient.

Core Idea
Imprint theory's proofs do not prove vision is by imprint.

Philosophers proposed that vision occurs by the imprint of the visible object's form in the eye. The author presents their main proofs and additional analogies, then dismisses them as weak. This lesson examines those arguments and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the imprint theory arguments
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the main proof and the additional analogies (afterimage, distance perception).
Focus on the structure: the author first states the main proof, then adds other arguments.
2
Identify the author's critique
Notice how the author says the main proof does not establish vision by imprint, and the other arguments are dismissed as weak.
The phrase 'وهو ضعيف' explicitly declares weakness.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
The author presents arguments then undermines them. Consider why each analogy fails to prove the theory.
Contrast the necessary condition (imprint in polished surface) with the claimed cause of vision.
4
Connect to broader kalam debates
This discussion touches on the nature of vision and causality. Think about how this relates to the Kalam position that vision is a direct act of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والمشهور من آراء الفلاسفة الانطباع والشعاع. والعمدة في إثبات الأول أن نور العين مرئي، وانطباع الشبح من الشيء في المقابل الصقيل المستنير ضروري. لكنه لا يفيد كون الرؤية بذلك، وقد يستدل بالقياس على سائر الحواس، حيث يأتيها المحسوس، وبأن صورة الشمس قد تبقى زماناً، في عين من أطال النظر إليها، ثم أعرض، وبأن الغريب مرئي أكثر، وما ذلك إلا لكون الانطباع على مخروط من الهواء قاعدته سطح المرئي، فعند القرب يكون وتر الزاوية أعظم، وهو ضعيف. تمسك أصحاب الشعاع، بأنه يتفاوت الرؤية بقلة الشعاع وكثرته وغلظه ورقته، ووقوع المرئي في سهم المخروط وجوانبه، وقد يشاهد في الظلمة، انفصال النور من العين، وعند تغميض العين على السراج خطوط شعاعية. والجواب : أن مرجع ذلك إلى نور العين المسمى بالروح الباصرة، المعد لحصول مثله في المقابل المرتسم بينه وبين المرئي مخروط وهمي . وكان مبدأ هذا هو المراد بخروج الشعاع، أو الجسم الشعاعي للقطع بأنه يمتنع أن يخرج من العين ما ينبسط على نصف كرة العالم، وأن يتحرك إلى الجهات، وينفذ في السموات، ولا يتشوش بهبوب الرياح إلى غير ذلك من الأمارات]. أي القول بانطباع شبح المرئي في الرطوبة الجليدية، والقول بخروج الشعاع من العين على هيئة المخروط.
He said: And the famous among the philosophers' opinions are imprint and ray. The main proof for the first is that the light of the eye is visible, and the imprint of the phantasm from the thing in the polished luminous facing is necessary. But it does not show that vision is by that. And it may be argued by analogy to the other senses, where the sensed object comes to them, and by the fact that the image of the sun may remain for a time in the eye of one who gazes at it and then looks away, and by the fact that a distant object is seen more, and that is only because the imprint is on a cone of air whose base is the surface of the visible, so at proximity the chord of the angle is greater, and it is weak. The proponents of the ray held that vision varies with the decrease and increase of the ray, its thickness and thinness, and the position of the visible in the arrow of the cone and its sides, and in darkness the separation of light from the eye may be observed, and when closing the eye on a lamp, radial lines. The answer: that the reference of all that is to the light of the eye called the visual spirit, prepared for the occurrence of its like in the opposite, and an imaginary cone is traced between it and the visible. And the origin of this is what is meant by the emission of the ray or the ray body, to be certain that it is impossible for something to emerge from the eye that spreads over half the sphere of the world, and to move in all directions, and penetrate the heavens, and not be disturbed by the blowing of winds, and other such indications. That is: the statement of the imprint of the phantasm of the visible in the crystalline moisture, and the statement of the emission of the ray from the eye in the shape of a cone.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage critically examines the arguments for the two philosophical theories. The author first states that the main proof for imprint is that the eye's light is visible and imprint in a polished surface is necessary, but argues this does not prove vision is by imprint. Other analogies (e.g., afterimage of the sun, seeing distant objects) are dismissed as weak. For the ray theory, proponents point to variation of vision with ray strength and position, and the visible rays in darkness. The answer is that these phenomena are due to the 'visual spirit' (al-rūḥ al-bāṣira) in the eye, not actual emission. The implausibility of the ray theory is highlighted: it would require a entity that can spread over half the world, move multidirectionally, penetrate heavens, and remain unaffected by wind. The passage concludes by summarizing the two theories.

Key Terms
الانطباع (al-intibā')
نور العين (nūr al-ʿayn)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imprint theory's proofs are strong because they rely on observable phenomena like afterimages.
Correction: The author argues that these phenomena do not necessarily prove that vision occurs by imprint; they are merely correlated.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main proof for the imprint theory?
A) The light of the eye is visible and imprint is necessary
B) Analogy to other senses
C) Afterimage of the sun
D) Distance perception

Key Takeaway
The imprint theory's proofs are insufficient to establish that vision occurs by imprint.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief that you are emotionally attached to, do you honestly examine its evidence, or do you resist counterarguments? Think of a specific belief you have. What would it take for you to admit its proof is weak like the imprint theory's? Will you seek out one critique of that belief this week?
?

Recall a time you were convinced by an impressive-sounding argument that later turned out false. What did you learn? How can you develop a habit of testing the foundations of what you accept?
?
The philosophers used analogy from other senses to argue about vision, but the analogy was flawed. Have you ever applied a principle from one area of your life (e.g., work, relationships) to your relationship with God without checking if it truly fits? What was the consequence? Will you commit today to re-examine one such analogy you have made?

Arguments for the Ray Theory and Its Refutation
The author refutes the ray theory with philosophical and physical arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ray theory's arguments, the author's refutation based on the visual spirit, and the impossibility of a physical ray.

Core Idea
Ray theory's phenomena are explained by the visual spirit; a physical ray is impossible.

Proponents of the ray theory argue from the variation of vision with the ray's strength and position, observable separation of light from the eye, and radial lines seen when closing the eye. The author answers these by attributing them to the visual spirit (al-rūḥ al-bāṣira) and demonstrates the impossibility of a physical ray that could traverse the cosmos undisturbed.

Guided Path
1
Read the ray theory arguments
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the three arguments: variation of vision, observable separation of light, radial lines.
These are empirical observations the proponents use.
2
Examine the author's response
See how the author reinterprets these phenomena as due to the visual spirit, not actual emission.
The key phrase: 'مرجع ذلك إلى نور العين المسمى بالروح الباصرة'.
3
Understand the impossibility argument
The author argues that a physical ray cannot have the required properties (spread over half the world, move in all directions, penetrate heavens, be undisturbed by wind).
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
4
Summarize the two theories
The conclusion summarizes both imprint and ray theories; note that the author does not accept either.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال والمشهور من آراء الفلاسفة الانطباع والشعاع. والعمدة في إثبات الأول أن نور العين مرئي، وانطباع الشبح من الشيء في المقابل الصقيل المستنير ضروري. لكنه لا يفيد كون الرؤية بذلك، وقد يستدل بالقياس على سائر الحواس، حيث يأتيها المحسوس، وبأن صورة الشمس قد تبقى زماناً، في عين من أطال النظر إليها، ثم أعرض، وبأن الغريب مرئي أكثر، وما ذلك إلا لكون الانطباع على مخروط من الهواء قاعدته سطح المرئي، فعند القرب يكون وتر الزاوية أعظم، وهو ضعيف. تمسك أصحاب الشعاع، بأنه يتفاوت الرؤية بقلة الشعاع وكثرته وغلظه ورقته، ووقوع المرئي في سهم المخروط وجوانبه، وقد يشاهد في الظلمة، انفصال النور من العين، وعند تغميض العين على السراج خطوط شعاعية. والجواب : أن مرجع ذلك إلى نور العين المسمى بالروح الباصرة، المعد لحصول مثله في المقابل المرتسم بينه وبين المرئي مخروط وهمي . وكان مبدأ هذا هو المراد بخروج الشعاع، أو الجسم الشعاعي للقطع بأنه يمتنع أن يخرج من العين ما ينبسط على نصف كرة العالم، وأن يتحرك إلى الجهات، وينفذ في السموات، ولا يتشوش بهبوب الرياح إلى غير ذلك من الأمارات]. أي القول بانطباع شبح المرئي في الرطوبة الجليدية، والقول بخروج الشعاع من العين على هيئة المخروط.
He said: And the famous among the philosophers' opinions are imprint and ray. The main proof for the first is that the light of the eye is visible, and the imprint of the phantasm from the thing in the polished luminous facing is necessary. But it does not show that vision is by that. And it may be argued by analogy to the other senses, where the sensed object comes to them, and by the fact that the image of the sun may remain for a time in the eye of one who gazes at it and then looks away, and by the fact that a distant object is seen more, and that is only because the imprint is on a cone of air whose base is the surface of the visible, so at proximity the chord of the angle is greater, and it is weak. The proponents of the ray held that vision varies with the decrease and increase of the ray, its thickness and thinness, and the position of the visible in the arrow of the cone and its sides, and in darkness the separation of light from the eye may be observed, and when closing the eye on a lamp, radial lines. The answer: that the reference of all that is to the light of the eye called the visual spirit, prepared for the occurrence of its like in the opposite, and an imaginary cone is traced between it and the visible. And the origin of this is what is meant by the emission of the ray or the ray body, to be certain that it is impossible for something to emerge from the eye that spreads over half the sphere of the world, and to move in all directions, and penetrate the heavens, and not be disturbed by the blowing of winds, and other such indications. That is: the statement of the imprint of the phantasm of the visible in the crystalline moisture, and the statement of the emission of the ray from the eye in the shape of a cone.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage critically examines the arguments for the two philosophical theories. The author first states that the main proof for imprint is that the eye's light is visible and imprint in a polished surface is necessary, but argues this does not prove vision is by imprint. Other analogies (e.g., afterimage of the sun, seeing distant objects) are dismissed as weak. For the ray theory, proponents point to variation of vision with ray strength and position, and the visible rays in darkness. The answer is that these phenomena are due to the 'visual spirit' (al-rūḥ al-bāṣira) in the eye, not actual emission. The implausibility of the ray theory is highlighted: it would require a entity that can spread over half the world, move multidirectionally, penetrate heavens, and remain unaffected by wind. The passage concludes by summarizing the two theories.

Key Terms
الشعاع (al-shu'ā')
الروح الباصرة (al-rūḥ al-bāṣira)
المخروط الوهمي (al-makhrūṭ al-wahmī)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The observable separation of light from the eye proves that a ray is emitted.
Correction: The author argues this is due to the visual spirit, not an actual physical ray.

Checkpoint
What is the author's answer to the ray theory's observed phenomena?
A) They are illusions
B) They are due to the visual spirit
C) They prove the ray theory is correct
D) They are caused by external light

Key Takeaway
The ray theory's phenomena are explained by the visual spirit, and the theory itself is physically impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The ray theorists held onto their views despite logical counterarguments. What is a belief you stubbornly cling to, even when evidence or reason contradicts it? Will you seek the truth this week, even if it means letting go?
?
The author uses simple physical reasoning to dismantle a complex theory. Where in your life have you accepted an idea without testing it against obvious truths? What one assumption can you challenge today?
?
The lesson shows that superficial observations can lead to false conclusions. Think of a time you judged a situation or person based on appearances. How can you train yourself to look deeper, starting now?

Causality and Created Things

The True Cause of Vision: Divine Creation
The author reports a view and declares the true cause of vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative view of vision as a direct act of divine creation, rejecting both philosophical theories.

Core Idea
Vision is not from rays or impressions—it is a direct act of God.

Scholars debated the mechanism of vision. Some proposed that it occurs when the eye faces an illuminated object, and the soul receives illuminative knowledge. The author presents this view but then clarifies the true position according to Sunni Islam: vision is purely by God's creation at the moment of opening the eye. In this lesson, we examine this theological assertion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp its flow. Notice it has two parts: a reported view and the author's declaration of truth.
The reported view denies both rays and impressions.
2
Identify the two parts
Separate the reported view (from 'وقيل' to 'جلية') from the author's truth statement (starting with 'والحق').
The reported view involves conditions like polished moisture and illuminative knowledge.
3
Reflect on the truth statement
Ask yourself why the author affirms that vision is purely by God's creation. What does this imply about philosophical theories?
This is a key theological point in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل : لا شعاع ولا انطباع . وإنما الإبصار بمقابلة المستنير للعضو الباصر، الذي فيه رطوبة صقيلة. فإذا وجدت هذه الشروط مع زوال الموانع ، يقع للنفس علم إشراقي بحضوري على المبصر، فتدركه النفس مشاهدة ظاهرة جلية . والحق: أن الإبصار بمحض خلق الله تعالى عند فتح العين .
قال والمشهور من اراء الفلاسفة الانطباع والشعاع
والعمدة في إثبات الأول. أن نور العين مرئي، وانطاع الشبح من الشيء في المقابل الصقيل المستنير ضروري. لكنه لا يفيد كون الرؤية بذلك، وقد يستدل بالقياس على سائر الحواس، حيث يأتيها المحسوس، وبأن صورة الشمس قد تبقى زماناً، في عين من أطال النظر إليها، ثم أعرض ، وبأن الغريب مرئي أكثر، وما ذلك إلا لكون الانطباع على مخروط من الهواء قاعدته سطح المرئي، فعند القرب يكون وتر الزاوية أعظم، وهو ضعيف .
تمسك أصحاب الشعاع، بأنه يتفاوت الرؤية بقلة الشعاع وكثرته وغلظه ورقته، و وقوع المرئي في سهم المخروط وجوانبه، وقد يشاهد في الظلمة، انفصال النور من العين، وعند تغميض العين على السراج خطوط شعاعية .
والجواب : أن مرجع ذلك إلى نور العين المسمى بالروح الباصرة، المعد لحصول مثله في المقابل المرتسم بينه وبين المرئي مخروط وهمي . وكان مبدأ هذا هو المراد بخروج الشعاع، أو الجسم الشعاعي للقطع بأنه يمتنع أن يخرج من العين ما ينبسط على نصف كرة العالم، وأن يتحرك إلى الجهات، وينفذ في السموات، ولا يتشوش بهبوب الرياح إلى غير ذلك من الأمارات .
أي القول بانطباع شبح المرئي في الرطوبة الجليدية، والقول بخروج الشعاع من العين على هيئة المخروط.
And it was said: no ray and no impression. Rather, vision is by the facing of the illuminated [object] to the seeing organ, which contains a polished moisture. So when these conditions exist along with the removal of obstacles, there occurs to the soul an illuminative presential knowledge of the seen object, so the soul perceives it as a clear apparent observation. And the truth is that vision is purely by the creation of God Most High upon opening the eye.
He said: And the well-known among the opinions of the philosophers are [the theory of] impression and ray.
And the main proof for establishing the first [impression] is that the light of the eye is visible, and the impression of the phantom of the thing in the polished illuminated opposite [surface] is necessary. But it does not imply that seeing is by that. And it may be argued by analogy to the other senses, since the sensed thing reaches them, and because the image of the sun may remain for a time in the eye of one who prolonged looking at it, then turned away, and because the distant object is seen as larger, and that is only because the impression is on a cone of air whose base is the surface of the seen object, so when near, the chord of the angle is greater, and it is weak.
The proponents of the ray adhered [to it] because vision varies by the scarcity and abundance of the ray, its thickness and thinness, and the occurrence of the seen object in the arrow of the cone and its sides, and it may be observed in darkness the separation of light from the eye, and when closing the eye on a lamp, radiating lines.
And the answer: That the reference of that is to the light of the eye called the 'seeing spirit' (al-ruh al-basira), which is prepared for the occurrence of its like in the opposite [space], with an imaginary cone traced between it and the seen object. And the principle of this is what is meant by the emission of the ray, or the ray-body, because it is impossible for something to come out of the eye that spreads over half the sphere of the world, and moves to directions, and penetrates the heavens, and is not disturbed by the blowing of winds, and other such indications.
That is: the theory of the impression of the phantom of the seen object in the icy moisture, and the theory of the emission of the ray from the eye in the form of a cone.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of vision and the prevalent theories. The author begins by reporting a view that denies both rays and impression, instead claiming that vision occurs when an illuminated object faces the seeing organ, which contains a polished moisture, and under the right conditions, the soul receives an illuminative presential knowledge of the object. The author then declares the truth: vision is purely an act of God's creation when the eye is opened. Next, he notes that the well-known philosophical theories are those of impression (intromission) and ray (extramission). He presents the main argument for intromission: the eye's light is visible, and impression in a polished shiny surface is necessary, but this does not prove that seeing occurs by means of that impression. Additional arguments are offered by analogy to the other senses (they receive the sensed object), the persistence of the sun's image, and the fact that far objects appear larger, attributed to a cone of air. The author dismisses these as weak. Then he states the arguments for the ray theory: variation of vision with the quantity and quality of the ray, observation of light coming out of the eye in darkness, and radiating lines when closing the eye on a lamp. The author's answer is that all these phenomena are due to the light of the eye, called the 'seeing spirit' (al-ruh al-basira), which is prepared to produce a similar effect in the opposite space, with an imaginary cone between it and the object. He argues that it is impossible for a physical ray to spread over half the world, move in directions, penetrate the heavens, and remain unaffected by wind. Finally, he clarifies that the two theories under discussion are the impression of the phantom in the icy moisture (the crystalline lens) and the emission of the ray from the eye in the shape of a cone.

Key Terms
إبصار
علم إشراقي
حضوري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Vision is caused by physical rays or impressions from the object.
Correction: The author declares that vision is purely by God's creation, not by any physical mechanism.

Checkpoint
What does the author identify as the true cause of vision?
A) Impression of the object's form in the eye
B) Emission of rays from the eye
C) Facing of the illuminated object to the eye with a polished moisture
D) Pure creation of God upon opening the eye

Key Takeaway
Vision is not a natural physical process but a direct creation of God at the moment of seeing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you open your eyes each morning, do you truly acknowledge that God is creating your sight afresh, or do you take it as a mere biological reflex? How would your first glance of the day change if you consciously attributed it to Allah's direct act?
?
If vision is a direct creation of God at every moment, what does that mean for the things you choose to look at, especially the haram? Will you now recognize that every forbidden glance is not just a 'natural' mistake but an act of using God's gift against His command?
?
Consider the sheer intimacy of God's involvement in your most basic act of seeing. He does not delegate it to physical laws. Does this awareness move you to gratitude, fear, or love? What one concrete habit can you adopt today to remind yourself of this truth every time you see?

The Two Philosophical Theories: Intromission and Extramission
The author names the two theories and defines them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two well-known philosophical theories of vision: intromission (impression) and extramission (ray).

Core Idea
The philosophers propose two opposite theories: impression and ray.

The author introduces the two main theories of vision from the philosophers: either the object's form impresses itself in the eye (intromission) or a ray emerges from the eye to the object (extramission). He then clarifies what these theories exactly claim. In this lesson, we learn the basic definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the theories
Read the Arabic sentence that mentions 'الانطباع والشعاع' and the clarification line at the end.
The passage is short but crucial.
2
Understand the two terms
Note that 'الانطباع' means impression (form of the object hitting the eye) and 'الشعاع' means ray (light emitted from the eye).
These are the two competing physical explanations.
3
Connect to the clarification
The final line defines each theory: impression of the phantom in the icy moisture (crystalline lens) and emission of a cone-shaped ray.
The 'icy moisture' refers to the lens.

Arguments for Intromission and Their Weakness
Arguments for intromission and their refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the main arguments for the intromission theory and why the author considers them weak.

Core Idea
Intromission arguments are plausible but ultimately fail to prove vision is by impression.

The author presents the main proof for the impression (intromission) theory: the light of the eye is visible, and impressions occur in polished surfaces. Additional analogical arguments are given, but the author dismisses them as weak. In this lesson, we examine these arguments and the criticism.

Guided Path
1
Read the arguments for impression
Read the passage from 'والعمدة في إثبات الأول' to 'وهو ضعيف'. Identify the main proof and the additional ones.
The main proof is the visibility of the eye's light and necessary impression in polished surfaces.
2
Analyze the additional arguments
Notice the three analogies: other senses, persistence of sun image, and the cone-of-air explanation for size perception.
The author says these are weak.
3
Understand the criticism
The author points out that the main proof does not imply that seeing occurs by impression; it only shows that impression is possible.
The weakness lies in the leap from possibility to actual mechanism.

والعمدة في إثبات الأول. أن نور العين مرئي، وانطاع الشبح من الشيء في المقابل الصقيل المستنير ضروري. لكنه لا يفيد كون الرؤية بذلك، وقد يستدل بالقياس على سائر الحواس، حيث يأتيها المحسوس، وبأن صورة الشمس قد تبقى زماناً، في عين من أطال النظر إليها، ثم أعرض ، وبأن الغريب مرئي أكثر، وما ذلك إلا لكون الانطباع على مخروط من الهواء قاعدته سطح المرئي، فعند القرب يكون وتر الزاوية أعظم، وهو ضعيف .
تمسك أصحاب الشعاع، بأنه يتفاوت الرؤية بقلة الشعاع وكثرته وغلظه ورقته، و وقوع المرئي في سهم المخروط وجوانبه، وقد يشاهد في الظلمة، انفصال النور من العين، وعند تغميض العين على السراج خطوط شعاعية .
والجواب : أن مرجع ذلك إلى نور العين المسمى بالروح الباصرة، المعد لحصول مثله في المقابل المرتسم بينه وبين المرئي مخروط وهمي . وكان مبدأ هذا هو المراد بخروج الشعاع، أو الجسم الشعاعي للقطع بأنه يمتنع أن يخرج من العين ما ينبسط على نصف كرة العالم، وأن يتحرك إلى الجهات، وينفذ في السموات، ولا يتشوش بهبوب الرياح إلى غير ذلك من الأمارات .
أي القول بانطباع شبح المرئي في الرطوبة الجليدية، والقول بخروج الشعاع من العين على هيئة المخروط.
And it was said: no ray and no impression. Rather, vision is by the facing of the illuminated [object] to the seeing organ, which contains a polished moisture. So when these conditions exist along with the removal of obstacles, there occurs to the soul an illuminative presential knowledge of the seen object, so the soul perceives it as a clear apparent observation. And the truth is that vision is purely by the creation of God Most High upon opening the eye.
He said: And the well-known among the opinions of the philosophers are [the theory of] impression and ray.
And the main proof for establishing the first [impression] is that the light of the eye is visible, and the impression of the phantom of the thing in the polished illuminated opposite [surface] is necessary. But it does not imply that seeing is by that. And it may be argued by analogy to the other senses, since the sensed thing reaches them, and because the image of the sun may remain for a time in the eye of one who prolonged looking at it, then turned away, and because the distant object is seen as larger, and that is only because the impression is on a cone of air whose base is the surface of the seen object, so when near, the chord of the angle is greater, and it is weak.
The proponents of the ray adhered [to it] because vision varies by the scarcity and abundance of the ray, its thickness and thinness, and the occurrence of the seen object in the arrow of the cone and its sides, and it may be observed in darkness the separation of light from the eye, and when closing the eye on a lamp, radiating lines.
And the answer: That the reference of that is to the light of the eye called the 'seeing spirit' (al-ruh al-basira), which is prepared for the occurrence of its like in the opposite [space], with an imaginary cone traced between it and the seen object. And the principle of this is what is meant by the emission of the ray, or the ray-body, because it is impossible for something to come out of the eye that spreads over half the sphere of the world, and moves to directions, and penetrates the heavens, and is not disturbed by the blowing of winds, and other such indications.
That is: the theory of the impression of the phantom of the seen object in the icy moisture, and the theory of the emission of the ray from the eye in the form of a cone.
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage introduces the topic of vision and the prevalent theories. The author begins by reporting a view that denies both rays and impression, instead claiming that vision occurs when an illuminated object faces the seeing organ, which contains a polished moisture, and under the right conditions, the soul receives an illuminative presential knowledge of the object. The author then declares the truth: vision is purely an act of God's creation when the eye is opened. Next, he notes that the well-known philosophical theories are those of impression (intromission) and ray (extramission). He presents the main argument for intromission: the eye's light is visible, and impression in a polished shiny surface is necessary, but this does not prove that seeing occurs by means of that impression. Additional arguments are offered by analogy to the other senses (they receive the sensed object), the persistence of the sun's image, and the fact that far objects appear larger, attributed to a cone of air. The author dismisses these as weak. Then he states the arguments for the ray theory: variation of vision with the quantity and quality of the ray, observation of light coming out of the eye in darkness, and radiating lines when closing the eye on a lamp. The author's answer is that all these phenomena are due to the light of the eye, called the 'seeing spirit' (al-ruh al-basira), which is prepared to produce a similar effect in the opposite space, with an imaginary cone between it and the object. He argues that it is impossible for a physical ray to spread over half the world, move in directions, penetrate the heavens, and remain unaffected by wind. Finally, he clarifies that the two theories under discussion are the impression of the phantom in the icy moisture (the crystalline lens) and the emission of the ray from the eye in the shape of a cone.

Key Terms
عمدة
شبح
مخروط
قياس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intromission theory is proven by the fact that impressions occur in mirrors.
Correction: The author states that the main proof does not imply vision occurs by impression; it only shows possibility.

Checkpoint
What does the author say about the main proof for impression theory?
A) It is conclusive and undeniable.
B) It proves vision is by impression.
C) It does not imply that vision occurs by impression.
D) It is based on revelation.

Key Takeaway
The arguments for intromission are compelling but fall short of proving that vision actually occurs by impression.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a belief because it seemed plausible, only to later realize it was built on weak evidence? What would it cost you to re-examine a comfortable but unsupported conviction today?
?
The author dismisses the arguments for intromission as weak. Are there arguments you rely on to justify your actions that are actually weak? Will you abandon them if they cannot withstand honest scrutiny?
?
If human reasoning can produce convincing but flawed proofs about something as basic as vision, how much more careful must you be when reasoning about Allah? What step will you take this week to ensure your certainty rests on revelation rather than your own logic?

Arguments for Extramission and the Author's Refutation
Arguments for extramission and their refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments for the ray theory and the author's rational refutation based on the impossibility of a physical ray.

Core Idea
The ray theory is refuted because a physical ray cannot spread across the world and remain unaffected.

Proponents of extramission argue from the variation of vision with ray quality, observation of light from the eye, and radiating lines. The author responds by attributing these to the 'seeing spirit' (al-ruh al-basira) and an imaginary cone, and then provides a powerful physical impossibility argument. In this lesson, we explore this resolution.

Guided Path
1
Read the arguments for ray
Read from 'تمسك أصحاب الشعاع' to 'خطوط شعاعية'. Note the three reasons: variation of vision, observation of light in darkness, and radiating lines.
These are common-sense observations.
2
Read the author's answer
From 'والجواب' to 'الأمارات'. Understand that he explains these as effects of the 'seeing spirit', not a physical ray.
He uses an imaginary cone (makhrut wahmi).
3
Identify the impossibility argument
The author argues that a physical ray cannot spread over half the world, move directionally, penetrate heavens, and remain undisturbed by wind.
This is the decisive refutation.

Source Text

قال والمشهور من اراء الفلاسفة الانطباع والشعاع

?

Will you examine one belief today that you hold with certainty and ask whether it is based on a 'literal ray' or a 'seeing spirit'?
?
The author uses rational impossibility to refute a common-sense observation. When have you defended a belief because 'it seems obvious' or 'I can see it' without subjecting it to rational analysis? Will you commit to applying the same standard of impossibility to your own convictions, even if they feel intuitive?

The Main Proof for Intromission: The Eye as a Polished Luminous Body
First proof of the intromission theory and its critique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first and most important proof of the intromission theory of vision, its supporting observations, and why the author argues it only proves the impression of the object's form, not that seeing occurs through that impression.

Core Idea
The eye's luminosity proves impression but fails to establish vision itself.

The intromission theory claims that vision occurs when an object's form impresses itself upon the eye. Its proponents present several proofs, the strongest of which is based on the eye being a polished, luminous body. In this passage, the author presents this proof along with empirical observations meant to support it, then delivers a crucial critique: even if the impression is proven, it does not prove that we see by means of that impression.

Guided Path
1
Read the proof statement
Read the first proof carefully, noting its syllogistic structure: the eye is a polished luminous body, and such bodies reflect objects like a mirror.
This is the 'main' proof for the intromission theory.
2
Examine the supporting observations
The author provides several everyday experiences to prove that the eye is luminous (e.g., rubbing the eye after sleep, seeing a circle of light near the nose, dilation of the pupil). List them and consider their strength.
These observations are meant to establish the minor premise of the syllogism.
3
Understand the author's critique
Even after all that, the author says the proof only shows that an impression occurs, not that seeing happens by that impression. Why? Because the mechanism of vision could be different.
This is a key limitation: proving an effect does not prove the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك الأولون بوجوه :
أحدها وهو العمدة، أن العين جسم صقيل نوراني، وكل جسم كذلك، إذا قابله كثيف ملون انطبع فيه شبحه كالمرآة. أما الكبرى فظاهرة، وأما الصغرى فلما يشاهد من النور في الظلمة، إذا حك المنتبه من النوم عينه، وكذا عند إمرار اليد على ظهر الهرة السوداء، ولأن الإنسان إذا نظر نحو أنفه قد يرى عليه دائرة من الضياء، وإذا انتبه من النوم، قد يبصر ما قرب منه زماناً، ثم يفقده، وذلك لامتلاء العين من النور في ذلك الوقت، وإذا غمض إحدى العينين، يتسع ثقبة العين الأخرى، وما ذاك إلا لأن جوهراً نورانياً يملأه ، ولأنه لولا انصباب الأرواح النورانية من الدماغ إلى العين لما جعلت ثقبتا الإبصار مجوفتين، وهذا بعد تمامه، إنما يفيد انطباع الشبح لا كون الإبصار به.
وثانيها : أن سائر الحواس إنما تدرك بأن يأتي صورة المحسوس إليها، لا بأن يخرج منها شيء إلى المحسوس، فكذا الإبصار. ورد بأنه تمثيل بلا جامع . وثالثها: أن من نظر إلى الشمس طويلاً، ثم أعرض عنها تبقى صورتها في عينه زماناً، وردّ بأن الصورة في خياله، لا عينه، كما إذا أغمض العين.
ورابعها أن الشيء بعينه إذا قرب من الرائي يرى أكبر مما إذا بعد عنه، وما ذاك إلا لأن الانطباع على مخروط من الهواء المشف رأسه متصل بالحدقة وقاعدته سطح المرئي، حتى أنه وتر لزاوية المخروط، ومعلوم أن وتر الزاوية كلما
The first group adhered with [several] proofs:
The first of them, and it is the main one, is that the eye is a polished luminous body, and every such body, if it faces a dense colored object, its phantom is impressed in it like a mirror. As for the major premise, it is apparent; and as for the minor premise, [it is proven] by what is observed of light in darkness when one who wakes from sleep rubs his eye, and likewise when passing the hand over the back of a black cat, and because a person, if he looks toward his nose, may see upon it a circle of light, and when he wakes from sleep, he may see what is near him for a time, then loses it, and that is due to the filling of the eye with light at that time, and if he closes one eye, the pupil of the other eye widens, and that is only because a luminous substance fills it, and because if the luminous spirits did not flow from the brain to the eye, the two hollow eye sockets [optic nerves] would not have been made hollow. And this, after its completion, only proves the impression of the phantom, not that seeing is by it.
And the second of them: That the other senses only perceive by the image of the sensed thing coming to them, not by something coming out from them to the sensed thing, so too [for] vision. And it was rebutted that it is an analogy without a common ground.
And the third of them: That one who looks at the sun for a long time, then turns away from it, its image remains in his eye for a time. And it was rebutted that the image is in his imagination, not his eye, just as when he closes the eye.
And the fourth of them: That the same thing, if it is near the observer, is seen larger than when it is far from him, and that is only because the impression is on a cone of transparent air, its tip connected to the pupil and its base the surface of the seen object, such that it is the chord of the angle of the cone, and it is known that the chord of the angle, the more...
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents four proofs that the proponents of the intromission theory (the view that vision occurs by the impression of the object's form in the eye) adhere to. The first and main proof: the eye is a polished luminous body; when it faces a dense colored object, its phantom is impressed like in a mirror. To prove that the eye is luminous, they offer several observations: seeing light when rubbing the eye after sleep, seeing a circle of light when looking at the nose, seeing nearby objects upon waking (due to the eye being filled with light), dilation of the pupil when the other eye is closed (due to a luminous substance), and the hollowness of the optic nerves (to allow flow of luminous spirits). However, the author notes that this only proves the impression of the phantom, not that seeing occurs by this impression. The second proof: analogy with other senses that receive images from outside; rebutted as an analogy without a valid common point. The third proof: the afterimage of the sun remains in the eye; rebutted that the image is in the imagination, not the eye. The fourth proof: near objects appear larger because the impression is on a cone of transparent air with its tip at the pupil and base at the object; as the distance decreases, the angle's chord increases. The text cuts off before the rebuttal is given.

Key Terms
انطباع شبح
روح نورانية
كثيف ملون

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The first proof conclusively proves that vision occurs by the impression of the object's form in the eye.
Correction: The author explicitly states that even if the impression is proven, it does not prove that seeing occurs by that impression; it only proves the impression itself.

Checkpoint
What does the author conclude about the first proof?
A) It proves that vision occurs by the impression of the object's form.
B) It proves that the eye is luminous.
C) It only proves the impression of the phantom, not that seeing is by it.
D) It is invalid because the eye is not polished.

Key Takeaway
The strongest proof for intromission fails to establish the mechanism of vision, only the fact of optical impression.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson shows that even a strong proof can only prove a part of what people claim. Where in your own life have you accepted a partial proof as complete—maybe about your own faith or about others? What would it take for you to admit that your evidence only goes so far?
?
The author critiques the very proof he presents. Are you willing to expose the weaknesses in your own arguments, even when they feel convincing? Think of a belief you hold strongly about your religion or worldview. What is one limitation or incomplete aspect of that belief that you tend to ignore?
?
The empirical observations in the proof seem compelling (rubbing eyes, seeing light), but the author shows they don't prove the conclusion. When you see 'scientific' evidence for religious claims, do you check whether it actually proves the point or just suggests something? Challenge yourself to apply this critical eye to one argument you often use.

Additional Proofs for Intromission: Analogy and Afterimage
Second and third proofs of the intromission theory and their rebuttals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two secondary proofs for the intromission theory—the analogy with other senses and the afterimage observation—and the author's refutations of both.

Core Idea
Analogies and afterimages are weak proofs for intromission; the author refutes them decisively.

In addition to the main proof from the eye's luminosity, supporters of intromission offer two further arguments: one based on analogy with the other senses, and another based on the persistence of the sun's afterimage. The author presents these proofs and then immediately rebuts them—the first as an invalid analogy, the second by locating the afterimage in the imagination rather than the eye.

Guided Path
1
Examine the analogy proof and its rebuttal
Read the second proof: other senses receive images from outside, so vision must work similarly. Then read the rebuttal: this is an analogy without a common ground.
The rebuttal uses the principle that analogy requires a shared essential attribute.
2
Examine the afterimage proof and its rebuttal
Read the third proof: after looking at the sun, an afterimage remains in the eye. Then read the rebuttal: the image is in the imagination, not the eye, just like when you close your eyes and see an image.
This distinguishes between sensory and imaginative perception.
3
Compare the two rebuttals
Notice that both rebuttals deny that the proof actually shows an impression in the eye. One is about logical method, the other about the nature of the perceived image.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تمسك الأولون بوجوه :
أحدها وهو العمدة، أن العين جسم صقيل نوراني، وكل جسم كذلك، إذا قابله كثيف ملون انطبع فيه شبحه كالمرآة. أما الكبرى فظاهرة، وأما الصغرى فلما يشاهد من النور في الظلمة، إذا حك المنتبه من النوم عينه، وكذا عند إمرار اليد على ظهر الهرة السوداء، ولأن الإنسان إذا نظر نحو أنفه قد يرى عليه دائرة من الضياء، وإذا انتبه من النوم، قد يبصر ما قرب منه زماناً، ثم يفقده، وذلك لامتلاء العين من النور في ذلك الوقت، وإذا غمض إحدى العينين، يتسع ثقبة العين الأخرى، وما ذاك إلا لأن جوهراً نورانياً يملأه ، ولأنه لولا انصباب الأرواح النورانية من الدماغ إلى العين لما جعلت ثقبتا الإبصار مجوفتين، وهذا بعد تمامه، إنما يفيد انطباع الشبح لا كون الإبصار به.
وثانيها : أن سائر الحواس إنما تدرك بأن يأتي صورة المحسوس إليها، لا بأن يخرج منها شيء إلى المحسوس، فكذا الإبصار. ورد بأنه تمثيل بلا جامع . وثالثها: أن من نظر إلى الشمس طويلاً، ثم أعرض عنها تبقى صورتها في عينه زماناً، وردّ بأن الصورة في خياله، لا عينه، كما إذا أغمض العين.
ورابعها أن الشيء بعينه إذا قرب من الرائي يرى أكبر مما إذا بعد عنه، وما ذاك إلا لأن الانطباع على مخروط من الهواء المشف رأسه متصل بالحدقة وقاعدته سطح المرئي، حتى أنه وتر لزاوية المخروط، ومعلوم أن وتر الزاوية كلما
The first group adhered with [several] proofs:
The first of them, and it is the main one, is that the eye is a polished luminous body, and every such body, if it faces a dense colored object, its phantom is impressed in it like a mirror. As for the major premise, it is apparent; and as for the minor premise, [it is proven] by what is observed of light in darkness when one who wakes from sleep rubs his eye, and likewise when passing the hand over the back of a black cat, and because a person, if he looks toward his nose, may see upon it a circle of light, and when he wakes from sleep, he may see what is near him for a time, then loses it, and that is due to the filling of the eye with light at that time, and if he closes one eye, the pupil of the other eye widens, and that is only because a luminous substance fills it, and because if the luminous spirits did not flow from the brain to the eye, the two hollow eye sockets [optic nerves] would not have been made hollow. And this, after its completion, only proves the impression of the phantom, not that seeing is by it.
And the second of them: That the other senses only perceive by the image of the sensed thing coming to them, not by something coming out from them to the sensed thing, so too [for] vision. And it was rebutted that it is an analogy without a common ground.
And the third of them: That one who looks at the sun for a long time, then turns away from it, its image remains in his eye for a time. And it was rebutted that the image is in his imagination, not his eye, just as when he closes the eye.
And the fourth of them: That the same thing, if it is near the observer, is seen larger than when it is far from him, and that is only because the impression is on a cone of transparent air, its tip connected to the pupil and its base the surface of the seen object, such that it is the chord of the angle of the cone, and it is known that the chord of the angle, the more...
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents four proofs that the proponents of the intromission theory (the view that vision occurs by the impression of the object's form in the eye) adhere to. The first and main proof: the eye is a polished luminous body; when it faces a dense colored object, its phantom is impressed like in a mirror. To prove that the eye is luminous, they offer several observations: seeing light when rubbing the eye after sleep, seeing a circle of light when looking at the nose, seeing nearby objects upon waking (due to the eye being filled with light), dilation of the pupil when the other eye is closed (due to a luminous substance), and the hollowness of the optic nerves (to allow flow of luminous spirits). However, the author notes that this only proves the impression of the phantom, not that seeing occurs by this impression. The second proof: analogy with other senses that receive images from outside; rebutted as an analogy without a valid common point. The third proof: the afterimage of the sun remains in the eye; rebutted that the image is in the imagination, not the eye. The fourth proof: near objects appear larger because the impression is on a cone of transparent air with its tip at the pupil and base at the object; as the distance decreases, the angle's chord increases. The text cuts off before the rebuttal is given.

Key Terms
تمسك
تمثيل بلا جامع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The afterimage of the sun proves that objects physically impress their forms into the eye.
Correction: The author emphasizes that the image is in the imagination, not the eye, as shown by similar images seen when the eyes are closed.

Checkpoint
How does the author rebut the third proof (the afterimage of the sun)?
A) It only works for very bright objects.
B) The image is in the imagination, not the eye.
C) The image is due to a residual impression in the lens.
D) The proof is invalid because the sun is too far.

Key Takeaway
Secondary proofs for intromission are rebutted by pointing out either a lack of common ground or a misattribution of the sensory experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you use analogies to justify your actions or beliefs, do you ensure there is a genuine common ground, or do you rely on superficial similarities? Will you examine the 'jāmi‘' in your reasoning today?
?
The afterimage persists in your imagination, not your eye. What false images are you carrying in your imagination that you mistake for reality? Are you willing to let the truth from this lesson correct them?
?
Both proofs were rebutted because they lacked solid foundation. In what area of your faith are you relying on weak proofs? Will you seek stronger evidence from revelation and sound reasoning today?

The Ray Emission Theory and the Cone Model of Vision
Taftazani explains the ray emission theory and the cone model for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the ray emission theory's core claim that vision occurs via a ray emitted from the eye, and how the cone model explains why distant objects appear smaller.

Core Idea
The eye emits a ray that forms a cone; narrower cone means smaller apparent size.

Before discussing the refutation of the ray emission theory, Taftazani first explains its details as held by many scholars. This theory treats the eye like a luminous body that emits a ray traveling in a cone to the object. Understanding this model is crucial for following the subsequent criticisms.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to grasp the theory's basic claim.
Notice the phrase 'خروج الشعاع'.
2
Identify the cone model
Look for the description of the cone with apex at the eye and base at the object. Understand how distance affects the cone's shape.
The text says 'على هيئة مخروط'.
3
Apply to size perception
Follow the reasoning that as distance increases, the cone narrows, reducing the angle at the eye, so the object appears smaller.
This is a key optical proof.
4
Consider the refutation context
Remember that this theory is presented before being refuted. This lesson only covers its exposition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يمكن إبطال القول بأن الإبصار بتكيف الهواء بشعاع العين واتصاله بالمرئي. قال هذا والقول بخروج الشعاع : بمعنى وقوعه من العين على المبصر كما في النيرات مما اختاره كثير من المحققين، وبنوا عليه رؤية الشيء من القرب، وفي الماء أعظم، ورؤية الواحد اثنين، ورؤية الشجر في الماء منعكساً، إلى غيره ذلك من التفاصيل المستوفاة في علم المناظر . يريد أن علم المناظر والمرايا فن على حدة اعتنى به كثير من المحققين، وبنوا الكلام فيه على خروج الشعاع، بمعنى وقوعه من العين على المرئي، كما يقع من الشمس والقمر وسائر الأجسام المضيئة على ما يقابلها على هيئة مخروط رأسه عند المضيء، وقاعدته عند المرئي ، فيرى الشيء إذا بعد أصغر مما إذا قرب ، لان المخروط يستدق فتضيق زواياه التي عند الباصرة ، وتضيق لذلك الدائرة التي عند المبصر، وكلما ازداد الشيء بعداً ، ازدادت الزوايا والدائرة صغراً، إلى أن ينتهي في البعد إلى حيث لا يمكن الابصار، ويرى الشيء في الماء أعظم منه في الهواء ، لان الشعاع ينفذ في الهواء على استقامة وأما في الماء فبعضه ينفذ مستقيماً، وبعضه ينعطف على سطح الماء، فلم ينفذ إلى المبصر، فيرى بالامتداد الشعاعي النافذ مستقيماً ومنعطفاً معاً من غير تمايز ، وذلك إذا قرب المرئي من سطح الماء، وأما إذا بعد فيرى في موضعين، لكون رؤيتهما بالامتدادين المتمايزين، وكذا إذا غمرتا إحدى العينين ونظرنا إلى القمر نراه قمرين لأن الامتداد الشعاعي الخارج منها ، ينحرف عن المحاذاة ، فلا يلتقي مؤدي الامتدادين في الحس المشترك على موضع واحد، بل موضعين فيرى المرئي اثنين، وهكذا في مخالفة الاحول، وفيما إذا وضعنا السبابة والوسطى على العين مع الاختلاف في الوضع، ونظرنا إلى السراج فإنا نراه اثنين وكذا إذا نظرنا إلى الماء عند طلوع القمر، فإنا نرى في الماء قمراً بالشعاع النافذ فيه ، وفي السماء قمراً بالشعاع المنعكس من سطح الماء إلى السماء ، ومن هذا القبيل رؤية الشيء في المرآة، وذلك أن الشعاع الممتد من الباصرة إلى الجسم الصقيل ينعكس منه إلى جسم آخر، وضعه من ذلك الصقيل كوضع الباصرة منه ، بشرط أن تكون جهته لجهة الرائي. وأما السبب في رؤية الشجر على شط النهر منتكساً، فهو أن الشعاع إذا وقع على سطح الماء ينعكس منه إلى رأس الشجر من موضع أقرب إلى الرائي، وإلى أسفله من موضع أبعد من الرائي إلى أن تتصل قاعدة الشجرة بقاعدة عکسه، والنفس لا تدرك الانعكاس لتعودها برؤية الأشياء على استقامة الشعاع ، فتحسب الشعاع المنعكس نافذاً في الماء، فترى رأس الشجر أكثر نزولاً في الماء لكونه أبعد منه وباقي أجزائه على الترتيب إلى قاعدة الشجر، فيرى منتكساً، وبيان ذلك بالتحقيق في علم المناظر.
It is possible to refute the claim that vision is by the adaptation of air with the ray of the eye and its connection to the seen object. He said: This and the claim of the emission of the ray: meaning its occurrence from the eye onto the visible object, as in luminous bodies, which many investigators have chosen, and they built upon it the seeing of a thing from near, and in water larger, and seeing the one as two, and seeing the tree in water inverted, and other such details comprehensively covered in the science of optics. He means that optics and mirrors is a separate art which many investigators have attended to, and they built their discussion in it upon the emission of the ray, meaning its occurrence from the eye onto the visible object, just as it occurs from the sun, moon, and all luminous bodies onto what faces them in the form of a cone whose apex is at the luminous body and its base at the visible object. So the thing is seen smaller when far than when near, because the cone narrows, thus the angles at the seeing organ narrow, and thus the circle at the visible object narrows. The more distant the thing, the more the angles and the circle become smaller, until it reaches a distance where vision is impossible. And a thing is seen larger in water than in air, because the ray penetrates air straight, but in water some of it penetrates straight and some of it bends at the water's surface, so it does not penetrate to the seer, so it is seen by the ray extension that penetrates straight and bent together without distinction. That is when the visible object is close to the water's surface. But if it is far, it is seen in two places, because its seeing is by two distinct extensions. Likewise, if one dips one of the two eyes and we look at the moon, we see it as two moons, because the ray extension emitted from it deviates from alignment, so the meeting points of the two extensions do not coincide in the common sense at one place, but rather at two places, so the visible object is seen as two. Similarly in squinting, and when we place the index and middle fingers on the eye with a difference in position, and look at a lamp, we see it as two. And likewise if we look at water at moonrise, we see a moon in water by the ray penetrating it, and in the sky a moon by the ray reflected from the water's surface to the sky. And of this kind is seeing a thing in a mirror: the ray extending from the seeing organ to the polished body is reflected from it to another body, whose position relative to that polished body is like the position of the seeing organ from it, provided that its direction is toward the viewer. As for the cause of seeing trees on the riverbank inverted, it is that when the ray falls on the water's surface, it reflects from it to the top of the tree from a point closer to the viewer, and to its base from a point farther from the viewer, until the base of the tree connects with the base of its inversion. The soul does not perceive the reflection due to its habit of seeing things along the straight line of the ray, so it supposes the reflected ray to be penetrating into water, thus it sees the top of the tree more immersed in water because it is farther from it, and the rest of its parts in order to the base of the tree, so it is seen inverted. And the detailed explanation of that is in the science of optics.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents the theory of vision by ray emission (shu'a') and its detailed optical proofs. The author begins by stating that this theory can be refuted, but then proceeds to explain its content as held by many investigators. The theory claims that vision occurs when a ray emitted from the eye reaches the visible object, similar to how luminous bodies emit light. The author explains that optics (ilm al-manazir) is a separate science built on this premise. He describes the ray as traveling in a cone from the eye to the object, with the apex at the luminous source (or the eye) and the base at the object. Using this model, he accounts for why distant objects appear smaller: as distance increases, the cone narrows, reducing the angle at the seeing organ (basira) and the circle at the object. He then explains magnification in water: because the ray bends at the water's surface, some rays go straight and some bend, causing the object to appear larger when close; when far, the two ray paths separate, creating double vision. He gives other examples: dipping one eye yields two moons, pressing on the eye produces two lamps, and reflections from water or mirrors create multiple images. Finally, he explains the inversion of trees on a riverbank: the reflected ray from the water is misinterpreted by the soul as straight, so the tree appears inverted. The passage concludes by referring to optics for full details. The author's aim is to present this theory before refuting it, though the refutation itself is not included in this passage.

Key Terms
شعاع (shu'a')
مخروط (makhrut)
باصرة (basira)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ray emission theory is the standard kalam view of vision.
Correction: Taftazani presents this theory as a background before refuting it; it is not the accepted position in kalam.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to the cone as the object moves farther?
A) It widens.
B) It narrows.
C) It stays the same.
D) It reverses.

Key Takeaway
The ray emission theory accounts for size perception via a cone of rays that narrows with distance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The cone model shows that what appears 'small' may only be a matter of distance. Are there spiritual truths—like the reality of sins, the weight of good deeds, or the nearness of Allah—that you perceive as insignificant simply because you have distanced yourself from them? What specific step will you take this week to move closer to that truth so you can see it in its true size?
?
This passage presents a model that some built an entire science upon, yet the author introduces it to be refuted. How confident are you that the foundations you have built your life upon—your priorities, assumptions, or spiritual practices—are not based on something that will be proven false? What would it take for you to examine the foundations of your faith with the same critical care?
?
The ray theory says that at a certain distance, vision becomes impossible. Is there any aspect of your relationship with Allah—like love, awe, or gratitude—that has grown so faint you can barely 'see' it anymore? What is the one change you can make today to close that distance and restore clear vision?

Water Refraction and Double Vision in the Ray Theory
Taftazani uses the ray theory to explain water magnification, double vision, and related phenomena.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ray theory explains magnification in water and double vision phenomena through refraction and separation of ray paths.

Core Idea
Water bends some rays, causing magnification or double vision depending on the object's distance.

Having established the basic cone model, Taftazani applies the ray theory to explain common optical phenomena involving water. The behavior of rays at the water-air boundary leads to apparent magnification when objects are near, and double images when they are farther. These accounts show the theory's explanatory power.

Guided Path
1
Read the water section
Read the part about seeing things larger in water and the explanation of why some rays bend.
Look for the words 'انعطاف' and 'انعكاس'.
2
Understand refraction
The text explains that some rays go straight and some bend at the water surface, leading to two paths.
The bending is due to the change in medium.
3
Compare near vs far objects
Notice the difference: when the object is close, the two ray paths are not distinct, so the object appears larger; when far, they separate, causing double vision.
The condition 'إذا قرب' vs 'إذا بعد'.
4
Other double vision examples
Read the examples of dipping an eye and pressing fingers on the eye to see two moons or two lamps, linking to squinting.
These all rely on deviation of the ray extension.

It is possible to refute the claim that vision is by the adaptation of air with the ray of the eye and its connection to the seen object. He said: This and the claim of the emission of the ray: meaning its occurrence from the eye onto the visible object, as in luminous bodies, which many investigators have chosen, and they built upon it the seeing of a thing from near, and in water larger, and seeing the one as two, and seeing the tree in water inverted, and other such details comprehensively covered in the science of optics. So the thing is seen smaller when far than when near, because the cone narrows, thus the angles at the seeing organ narrow, and thus the circle at the visible object narrows. The more distant the thing, the more the angles and the circle become smaller, until it reaches a distance where vision is impossible. And a thing is seen larger in water than in air, because the ray penetrates air straight, but in water some of it penetrates straight and some of it bends at the water's surface, so it does not penetrate to the seer, so it is seen by the ray extension that penetrates straight and bent together without distinction. That is when the visible object is close to the water's surface. But if it is far, it is seen in two places, because its seeing is by two distinct extensions. And likewise if we look at water at moonrise, we see a moon in water by the ray penetrating it, and in the sky a moon by the ray reflected from the water's surface to the sky. And of this kind is seeing a thing in a mirror: the ray extending from the seeing organ to the polished body is reflected from it to another body, whose position relative to that polished body is like the position of the seeing organ from it, provided that its direction is toward the viewer. As for the cause of seeing trees on the riverbank inverted, it is that when the ray falls on the water's surface, it reflects from it to the top of the tree from a point closer to the viewer, and to its base from a point farther from the viewer, until the base of the tree connects with the base of its inversion. The soul does not perceive the reflection due to its habit of seeing things along the straight line of the ray, so it supposes the reflected ray to be penetrating into water, thus it sees the top of the tree more immersed in water because it is farther from it, and the rest of its parts in order to the base of the tree, so it is seen inverted. And the detailed explanation of that is in the science of optics.
Source pages p. 284

Commentary
This passage presents the theory of vision by ray emission (shu'a') and its detailed optical proofs. The author begins by stating that this theory can be refuted, but then proceeds to explain its content as held by many investigators. The theory claims that vision occurs when a ray emitted from the eye reaches the visible object, similar to how luminous bodies emit light. The author explains that optics (ilm al-manazir) is a separate science built on this premise. He describes the ray as traveling in a cone from the eye to the object, with the apex at the luminous source (or the eye) and the base at the object. Using this model, he accounts for why distant objects appear smaller: as distance increases, the cone narrows, reducing the angle at the seeing organ (basira) and the circle at the object. He then explains magnification in water: because the ray bends at the water's surface, some rays go straight and some bend, causing the object to appear larger when close; when far, the two ray paths separate, creating double vision. He gives other examples: dipping one eye yields two moons, pressing on the eye produces two lamps, and reflections from water or mirrors create multiple images. Finally, he explains the inversion of trees on a riverbank: the reflected ray from the water is misinterpreted by the soul as straight, so the tree appears inverted. The passage concludes by referring to optics for full details. The author's aim is to present this theory before refuting it, though the refutation itself is not included in this passage.

Key Terms
شعاع (shu'a')
انعطاف (ini'taf)
انعكاس (in'ikas)
امتداد شعاعي (imtidad shua'i)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The ray emission theory is the standard kalam view of vision.
Correction: Taftazani presents this theory as a background before refuting it; it is not the accepted position in kalam.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does a far object in water appear in two places?
A) Because the ray splits into two straight paths.
B) Because the straight and bent rays separate.
C) Because the water reflects the object.
D) Because the eye is defective.

Key Takeaway
The ray theory explains water magnification and double vision by the splitting of ray paths at the water surface.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your eyes deceive you—like seeing two moons when there is only one—do you immediately correct your perception with reason, or do you allow your senses to mislead you? In what areas of your life are you trusting your flawed perceptions over the truth that Allah has placed in your heart? How can you train yourself to consult a higher 'common sense'—the Qur'an and Sunnah—before acting on what seems obvious?
?
The ray theory explains that double vision occurs when the two ray extensions do not converge in the common sense. Spiritually, when your desires and doubts pull you in two directions, do you seek the common sense of faith that unifies your vision? What single truth from this lesson will you hold onto today to prevent seeing 'double' in your beliefs?
?
Taftazani uses physical examples to show that what we see is not always reality. Have you ever been so certain of your own 'sight'—an opinion, a judgment—that you refused to reconsider even when evidence was clear? What would it take for you to admit that your perspective might be bent, and to seek the straight path from Allah?

Reflection in Mirrors and Water: Understanding Inversion
Taftazani explains mirror reflection and the optical cause of inverted tree images in water.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the ray theory explains reflection from polished surfaces, mirror images, and the inverted image of trees in water.

Core Idea
Reflected rays are misinterpreted by the soul as straight, causing inverted images.

The ray theory also accounts for reflection phenomena, including the familiar sight of trees inverted on a riverbank. Taftazani explains that when rays strike a water surface or a mirror, they reflect according to optical laws, and the soul's habitual expectation of straight rays leads to the perception of inversion. This section completes the theory's coverage of common visual experiences.

Guided Path
1
Read the reflection passage
Read about seeing the moon in water and in the sky simultaneously, and the mirror example.
Look for 'انعكاس' and 'شعاع منعكس'.
2
Understand the mirror rule
The text describes that the ray from the seeing organ reflects off the polished body to another body under a condition of equal angles.
The condition 'شرط أن تكون جهته لجهة الرائي'.
3
Analyze the tree inversion
Follow the explanation of how rays from different parts of the tree reflect at different points on the water surface, creating an inverted image.
The soul does not perceive the reflection due to habit.
4
Connect to common experience
Relate this to seeing reflections in water and mirrors, and why images appear reversed.

من القرب، وفي الماء أعظم، ورؤية الواحد اثنين، ورؤية الشجر في الماء منعكساً، إلى غيره ذلك من التفاصيل المستوفاة في علم المناظر . يريد أن علم المناظر والمرايا فن على حدة اعتنى به كثير من المحققين، وبنوا It is possible to refute the claim that vision is by the adaptation of air with the ray of the eye and its connection to the seen object. He said: This and the claim of the emission of the ray: meaning its occurrence from the eye onto the visible object, as in luminous bodies, which many investigators have chosen, and they built upon it the seeing of a thing from near, and in water larger, and seeing the one as two, and seeing the tree in water inverted, and other such details comprehensively covered in the science of optics. He means that optics and mirrors is a separate art which many investigators have attended to, and they built their discussion in it upon the emission of the ray, meaning its occurrence from the eye onto the visible object, just as it occurs from the sun, moon, and all luminous bodies onto what faces them in the form of a cone whose apex is at the luminous body and its base at the visible object. So the thing is seen smaller when far than when near, because the cone narrows, thus the angles at the seeing organ narrow, and thus the circle at the visible object narrows. The more distant the thing, the more the angles and the circle become smaller, until it reaches a distance where vision is impossible. And a thing is seen larger in water than in air, because the ray penetrates air straight, but in water some of it penetrates straight and some of it bends at the water's surface, so it does not penetrate to the seer, so it is seen by the ray extension that penetrates straight and bent together without distinction. That is when the visible object is close to the water's surface. But if it is far, it is seen in two places, because its seeing is by two distinct extensions. Likewise, if one dips one of the two eyes and we look at the moon, we see it as two moons, because the ray extension emitted from it deviates from alignment, so the meeting points of the two extensions do not coincide in the common sense at one place, but rather at two places, so the visible object is seen as two. Similarly in squinting, and when we place the index and middle fingers on the eye with a difference in position, and look at a lamp, we see it as two. And likewise if we look at water at moonrise, we see a moon in water by the ray penetrating it, and in the sky a moon by the ray reflected from the water's surface to the sky. And of this kind is seeing a thing in a mirror: the ray extending from the seeing organ to the polished body is reflected from it to another body, whose position relative to that polished body is like the position of the seeing organ from it, provided that its direction is toward the viewer. And the detailed explanation of that is in the science of optics.
