Vision Conditions: Philosophers and Mu'tazilites
Taftazani lists conditions for vision and refutes causal necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the conditions for vision as proposed by philosophers and Mu'tazilites, their claim of necessary causation, and the refutation based on customary sciences.

Core Idea
Conditions for vision are customary, not causally necessary.

In this passage, Taftazani presents a deterministic view of vision held by philosophers and Mu'tazilites, who claim that when certain conditions are met, vision must occur. He then provides a refutation by noting that the connection is merely customary. This lesson unpacks the conditions, the claim of necessity, and the critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow. Notice the list of conditions and the argument structure.
The passage includes a list of conditions, a claim of necessity, and a refutation.
2
Identify the conditions
Find the conditions listed: soundness of sense, intention of seer, presence of object, object being opaque/illuminated/opposite/without screen, and no excess in distance, closeness, smallness, or thickness.
These conditions are presented as required by philosophers and Mu'tazilites.
3
Understand the claim of necessity
The passage states that proponents claim vision must occur when these conditions are present. The example of towering mountains being present but unseen is used to argue against denial.
4
Grasp the refutation
The refutation says that the negation of vision despite conditions is based on customary sciences (al-‘ulum al-‘adiyya), meaning the connection is habitual, not logically necessary.
This is a key critique of deterministic causation in vision.
5
Recognize the attribution
The view is attributed to philosophers and Mu'tazilites; Taftazani does not endorse it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وقد يشترط في الإبصار) : بعد سلامة الحاسة، وقصد المبصر، وحضور المبصر، كونه كثيفاً مضيئاً مقابلاً، أو في حكمه من غير حجاب، ولا إفراط قرب أو بعد أو صغر أو بسبب غلظ ويدعى لزوم حصوله عند حصول الشرائط، وإلا لجاز أن يكون بحضرتنا جبال شاهقة . وردّ بأن نفي ذلك من العلوم العادية . زعمت الفلاسفة وتبعهم المعتزلة أن الإبصار يتوقف على شرائط يمتنع حصوله بدونها، ويجب حصوله معها .
He said: (And it may be required for vision): after soundness of the sense, the intention of the seer, the presence of the seen object, its being opaque, illuminated, opposite, or in its ruling, without a screen, and no excess of closeness, distance, smallness, or due to thickness. It is claimed that its occurrence is necessary when the conditions are present; otherwise, it would be possible that there be towering mountains present before us. And it was refuted that the negation of that is from customary sciences. The philosophers and the Mu'tazilites following them claimed that vision depends on conditions, the occurrence of which is impossible without them, and its occurrence is necessary with them.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for vision as set forth by philosophers and Mu'tazilites, and the author's refutation. The author first lists the conditions: soundness of the sense (salamat al-hassa), the intention (qasd) of the seer, the presence (hudur) of the object, and that the object be opaque (kathif), illuminated (mudi'), opposite (muqabil) to the viewer, or in its ruling (i.e., effectively the same), without a screen (hijab), and without excess (ifrat) of closeness, distance, smallness, or thickness (ghilaz). He then states that proponents claim that when these conditions are fulfilled, vision must occur; otherwise, one could argue that towering mountains are present before us but unseen, which is absurd. Then the author mentions a refutation: the negation of this necessity is based on customary sciences (al-‘ulum al-‘adiyya)—meaning that the connection between conditions and vision is empirical and habitual, not logically necessary. Finally, he attributes the view to the philosophers and Mu'tazilites: they hold that vision depends on conditions such that it cannot occur without them and must occur with them. The passage thus presents a deterministic causal view of vision and its critique from the perspective of customary causation.

Key Terms
شرائط (shara'it)
سلامة الحاسة (salamat al-hassa)
قصد (qasd)
حضور (hudur)
كثيف (kathif)
مضيء (mudi')
مقابل (muqabil)
حجاب (hijab)
إفراط (ifrat)
علوم عادية (ulum 'adiyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The author accepts these conditions as necessary for vision.
Correction: The author presents the view as that of philosophers and Mu'tazilites and refutes it by saying the connection is merely customary.

Checkpoint
What do the philosophers and Mu'tazilites claim about the conditions for vision?
A) Vision occurs necessarily when the conditions are met.
B) Vision never occurs even with the conditions.
C) The conditions are impossible to meet.
D) Vision is independent of the conditions.

Key Takeaway
Vision depends on conditions only in a customary, not necessary, sense according to the refutation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Think of a time you assumed something would happen because its causes were in place. How would your trust in Allah be tested if He broke that custom? What would you cling to then?

The Ash'ari Position on Effects and Bodily Differences
Sanusi states the Ash'ari position on causality and bodily differences.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'ari position that all bodily effects are directly caused by God and that differences among bodies are due to accidents rather than inherent natures.

Core Idea
For Ash'aris, God is the sole direct cause of all effects, and bodily differences come from accidents, not souls or natures.

This passage presents the Ash'ari response to the philosophical view that bodies have inherent causal powers. The Ash'aris assert that all observed effects (like growth, sensation) are directly from God, and the variations among bodies are due to accidents that God creates in them. This reaffirms divine omnipotence and the contingency of all created properties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal English translation. Notice the concise wording: the whole doctrine is contained in one statement.
The passage is dense; each phrase carries theological weight.
2
Identify the two key claims
The passage makes two main assertions: (1) effects are attributed to the All-Powerful (God), and (2) differences among bodies are due to accidents. Find these in the text.
The second claim includes that bodies are substances (jawāhir).
3
Contrast with the philosophical view
Consider the implied alternative: the philosophers attribute effects to innate souls or natures. How does the Ash'ari view differ?
This contrast is not in the passage but is the background for the statement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (وأما عندنا) فاستناد الآثار إلى القادر، واختلاف الأجسام بالعوارض، بكونها من جواهر.
He said: (And according to us [the Ash'aris]), the attribution of effects to the All-Powerful and the difference of bodies [in their properties] is due to accidents, in that they are among substances.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This is a brief statement of the Ash'ari position in contrast to the philosophical view just discussed. The commentator quotes the original text: 'According to us' (i.e., the Ash'ari school), all effects (āthār) observed in bodies—such as growth, sensation, or intellection—are attributed directly to the All-Powerful (al-Qādir), namely God. They are not produced by inherent souls or natural powers in the bodies. Furthermore, the differences among bodies (e.g., why one body has the property of sensation while another has the property of growth) are due to accidents (al-'awāriḍ) that inhere in them, not due to different types of substantial forms (like the philosophers' vegetative, animal, or rational souls). The bodies themselves are substances (jawāhir), and their specific characteristics are contingent accidents created directly by God. Thus, the Ash'ari view rejects the philosophers' notion of immanent causal powers in bodies and reaffirms God's direct causation and the accidental nature of all bodily properties.

Key Terms
القادر
الآثار
العوارض
جواهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Ash'ari view denies that bodies have any real distinguishing properties.
Correction: The Ash'ari view affirms that bodies do have distinguishing properties, but these differences are due to accidents created by God, not to inherent natures or forms.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari position stated here, what is the basis for differences in the properties of bodies?
A) The different types of souls inherent in them.
B) The accidents that inhere in the substances.
C) The specific forms that are substances inherent in matter.
D) The nature of the celestial spheres influencing them.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position is that all effects come directly from God, and bodily differences are due to accidents, not inherent forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you achieve something you worked hard for—like a promotion or a skill mastered—do you mentally attribute that effect to your own ability or to the All-Powerful who created both your effort and the outcome? What would it cost you to genuinely shift that attribution?
?
Consider one bodily property you are insecure about (e.g., your height, a physical trait). According to this passage, that property is an accident created directly by God—not inherent to you. How does that change how you feel about it? Will you now view it as a temporary, contingent gift rather than a fixed flaw?
?
If all bodily differences are due to accidents created by God, what grounds are left for pride or envy? Look at your own heart: do you still feel superior to someone because of a property you possess? This passage demands that you see that property as a divine accident, not your own achievement. Can you humble yourself to that truth?

Circular Motion Requires Both Corporeal and Abstract Movers
The author proves celestial motion is voluntary, requiring both corporeal forces and abstract souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the motion of celestial spheres must be voluntary and why it requires both corporeal forces and abstract souls as movers.

Core Idea
The motion of the heavens is neither natural nor forced—it is a voluntary act that demands both body and soul.

In this passage, the author systematically eliminates the possibilities of natural and compulsory motion for the celestial spheres, concluding that their movement is voluntary. He then argues that this voluntary motion cannot be explained by a single universal intellect alone; it requires both partial perceptions and universal intellections. This leads to the conclusion that the spheres are moved by both corporeal powers (like animal souls) and abstract souls (like rational souls).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the author's flow of reasoning come through before analyzing.
Notice the logical connectors: 'فاء' and 'لأن'.
2
Follow the elimination of natural motion
Identify why circular motion cannot be natural. The author argues that if it were natural, each part of a simple body would desire a different place, leading to absurdities like preferring a longer path.
Contrast with rectilinear motion.
3
Follow the elimination of compulsory motion
Understand why celestial motion cannot be compulsory. Compulsory motion is contrary to nature and uniform, but the observed motion varies in direction and speed.
4
Understand the need for both partial and universal movers
The author shows that voluntary motion requires partial perceptions for specific positions (requiring corporeal powers) and universal intellections for overall order (requiring abstract souls).
Note the established principle that corporeal powers are finite.
5
Reflect on the dual conclusion
The direct movers are both corporeal powers (like animal souls) and abstract souls (like our rational souls). Summarize the argument in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ينقطع يقع الحركة عنها، وهو محال. ولا يلزم ذلك في الحركة المستقيمة، لأن الحركة إلى النقطة التي فيما بين المبدأ والمنتهي ليست لان الوصول إليها مطلوب بالطبع، لأن الوصول إلى المطلوب بالطبع، أعني الحصول في الحيز، لا يمكن بدون ذلك، ولا كذلك حال المستديرة. أما فيما لا ينقطع عند تمام دوره فظاهر، وأما فيما ينقطع فلأن المطلوب بالطبع لو كان هو الوصول إلى نقطة الانقطاع، لكان مقتضى طبع كل جزء من أجزاء الجسم الواحد البسيط شيئاً آخر، وهو الحيز الذي فيه ذلك الجزء عند الانقطاع، ولكان مقتضى الطبع إيثار الطريق الأطول على الأقصر. ولا قسرية لأنها إنما تكون على خلاف الطبع، فحيث لا طبع فلا قسر. وعلى وفق القاسر فلا تختلف في الجهة والسرعة والبطء، فتعين أن تكون إرادية مقرونة بالإدراك. ولا يكفي لجزئياتها وخصوصياتها تعقل كلي لأن نسبته إلى الكل على السواء، ولا إدراكات جزئية وتخيلات محضة، لاستحالة دوامها على نظام واحد من غير انقطاع واختلاف، كيف وقد ثبت لزوم تناهي القوى الجسمانية. فإذن لا بد لتلك الحركات من إرادات وإدراكات جزئية. وقد تقرر أن ذلك لا يمكن إلا بقوى جسمانية، ومن إرادات وتعقلات كلية، وقد تقرر أن ذلك لا يكون إلا للذات المجردة. فثبت أن المباشر لتحريك الأفلاك قوى جسمانية، هي بمنزلة النفوس الحيوانية لأبدانها، ونفوس مجردة ذوات إرادات عقلية، وتعقلات كلية هي بمنزلة نفوسنا الناطقة.
It is impossible because motion away from it would occur. This does not follow in rectilinear motion, because motion to a point between the beginning and end is not [unnatural] because reaching it is desired by nature, since reaching what is desired by nature—namely, obtaining a place—cannot be without that, unlike the case of circular motion. As for that which does not stop at the completion of its cycle, it is obvious. As for that which does stop, if what is desired by nature were reaching the stopping point, then the requirement of the nature of each part of a single simple body would be something else—namely, the place that part occupies at the stopping point—and nature would require preferring the longer path over the shorter. And it is not compulsory, because compulsory motion occurs only contrary to nature, so where there is no nature there is no compulsion. And if it were according to the compeller, it would not differ in direction, speed, and slowness. Therefore, it must be voluntary, coupled with perception. A universal intellection is not sufficient for its particulars and specificities, because its relation to all is equal. Nor are partial perceptions and pure imaginations sufficient, because of the impossibility of their continuance in a single order without interruption and variation, especially since the finitude of corporeal powers has been established. Hence, these motions necessarily require partial wills and perceptions. And it has been established that this can only be through corporeal powers, and from [it also requires] universal wills and intellections, and it has been established that this can only belong to abstract essences. So it is proven that the direct mover of the spheres are corporeal powers, which are like animal souls for their bodies, and abstract souls possessing rational wills and universal intellections, like our rational souls.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the motion of celestial spheres is voluntary, not natural or compulsory. The author first argues that circular motion cannot be natural because if it were, each part of the sphere would naturally desire a different place, leading to contradictions (preferring longer path). It cannot be compulsory because compulsory motion is contrary to nature and uniform, whereas celestial motions vary. Therefore it must be voluntary (إرادية) combined with perception. Then he argues that this voluntary motion requires both partial perceptions/wills (for particular positions) and universal intellections (for the overall pattern). Since partial perceptions require corporeal powers (due to finitude) and universal intellections require abstract souls, the direct movers must be both corporeal forces (like animal souls) and abstract souls (like our rational souls). This establishes the dual nature of celestial movers.

حركة قسرية
حركة إرادية
قوى جسمانية
نفس مجردة
إرادة جزئية
إرادة كلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies on assuming the finitude of corporeal powers without justification.
Correction: The author presents the finitude of corporeal powers as already established, not as an assumption of this proof.

Checkpoint
What is the conclusion about the direct movers of the spheres?
A) They are purely corporeal.
B) They are purely abstract.
C) They are both corporeal and abstract.
D) They are neither.

Key Takeaway
The direct movers of the celestial spheres are both corporeal powers (like animal souls) and abstract souls (like rational souls).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Which part are you neglecting, and what concrete step will you take tomorrow to correct that imbalance?
?
The author shows that universal intentions alone cannot produce specific actions. You may recite general intentions like 'I want to be a better Muslim,' but have you broken that down into a particular, scheduled deed you will perform? Name one specific act you will commit to today, tying your general intention to a particular action.
?
In the source, the direct movers of the spheres are a combination of corporeal and abstract. Your acts of obedience also depend on both your physical effort (corporeal) and your spiritual intention (abstract). Identify one area where you have been relying solely on one and neglecting the other. How will you bring them into harmony starting now?

The Objection: Can Universal Will Explain Specific Motions?
The objector presents three challenges to the premises of the argument for voluntary celestial motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three main challenges raised against the argument that celestial motion requires both universal and particular will.

Core Idea
The objector denies that universal will is insufficient for specific motions.

Before we can accept that the celestial sphere's motion requires a universal and a particular will, we must address a series of objections. The objector here concedes some premises but challenges the necessity of the conclusion. This lesson presents the three objections, each targeting a different step in the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection paragraph
Read the Arabic passage containing the objection. Note the three separate 'we do not accept' statements.
2
Identify the first objection
The objector questions why the desired by nature must be a position that is left. He suggests it could be motion itself.
This challenges the premise that natural motion leaves a desired position.
3
Identify the second and third objections
The second challenges the nature of compulsion. The third challenges that universal will cannot produce specific motions; he suggests successive universal wills.
4
Summarize the objections
Briefly restate each objection in your own words to ensure you understand the challenge before moving to the resolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض بعد تسليم انحصار الحركة في الطبيعية والقسرية والإرادية، وأن التعقل الكلي لا يكون إلا للمجردات، ولا الجزئي إلا بالجسمانيات. بأنا لا نسلم لزوم كون المطلوب بالطبع متروكاً بالطبع، لم لا يجوز أن يكون المطلوب بالطبع نفس الحركة لا شيئاً من الأين والأوضاع التي تترك؟ ولا نسلّم أن القسر لا يكون إلا على خلاف الطبع، وأن القاسر لا يكون إلا متشابهاً، ليلزم تشابه الحركات. وأن الكلى من الإرادة والإدراك لا يصلح مبدأ لخصوصيات الحركات، لم لا يجوز أن تستند الحركات المتعاقبة إلى إرادات وإدراكات كلية متعاقبة لا إرادة وإدراك للحركة على الإطلاق.

وتحقيق ذلك ما أشار إليه ابن سينا في الإشارات، من أن المطلوب بالحركة الوضعية، لا يكون إلا الوضع المعين، ويمتنع أن يكون موجوداً، لأن الحاصل لا يطلب، وأن يكون في الحركة السرمدية جزئياً، لأن الحركة المتوجهة إليه تنقطع عنده. فمطلوب إرادة الفلك، يجب أن يكون وضعاً معيناً مفروضاً كلياً تفرضه الإرادة وتتجه إليه بالحركة. والمتعين لا ينافي الكلية، لأن كل واحد من كل كلي، فله مع كليته تعين يمتاز به عن سائر آحاد ذلك الكلى.
And he objected, after conceding the confinement of motion to natural, compulsory, and voluntary, and that universal intellection is only for abstracts, and partial only for corporeal things, [saying:] we do not accept the necessity that what is desired by nature is left by nature; why may it not be that what is desired by nature is motion itself, not some place or positions that are left? And we do not accept that compulsion occurs only contrary to nature, and that the compeller is necessarily uniform, so that motions would be similar. And that universal will and perception cannot be the principle for specific motions; why may it not be that successive motions depend on successive universal wills and perceptions, not a single will and perception for motion absolutely?

The verification of that is what Ibn Sina pointed to in al-Isharat: that the object of positional motion is only the specific position, and it is impossible for it to be existent because what is attained is not sought, and [impossible] for it to be partial in perpetual motion, because the motion directed towards it would cease at it. So the object of the sphere's will must be a specific, universal, assumed position that the will posits and towards which it moves by motion. The determinate does not contradict universality, because every individual of a universal has, along with its universality, a determination distinguishing it from other individuals of that universal.
Source pages p. 315

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer. The objector concedes some premises but challenges the conclusion that the motion must be voluntary with both partial and universal wills. He suggests that the desired by nature could be motion itself, not a stopping point; that compulsory motion could be possible; and that universal wills could produce successive motions without partial ones. The answer, from Ibn Sina's al-Isharat, clarifies that the object of the sphere's positional motion is a specific position, but it cannot be existent (since attained positions are not sought) nor partial (which would stop the motion). Therefore, the object must be a universal position that the will assumes, which is determinate yet universal, allowing perpetual motion. This resolves the objection by showing the need for a universal but determinate will.

Key Terms
مطلوب بالطبع
حركة وضعية
إرادة وإدراك كلي

A) A specific position.
B) Motion itself.
C) A particular location.
D) The sphere's will.

Key Takeaway
The objector challenges the necessity of a particular will for celestial motion, proposing alternatives that rely solely on natural desire, compulsion, or successive universal wills.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The objector suggests that natural desire alone could produce motion. Look at your own natural desires — do they lead to consistent, ordered action, or do they leave you restless and scattered? What does that reveal about the need for a higher will to guide your life?

Would you trust a God who only wills universals, or do you need a God who wills your particular needs? How does this passage challenge or affirm your belief in divine providence?
?
The objector questions whether compulsion must be contrary to nature. Have you ever felt compelled by circumstances to act against your nature? In those moments, did you sense a greater purpose or just resistance? How does the Islamic position on voluntary action and divine will speak to the tension between your natural inclinations and what you ought to do?

Ibn Sina's Resolution: The Universal Determinate Position
Ibn Sina explains why the sphere's will must be for a universal yet determinate position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the sphere's will must be directed to a universal yet determinate position, according to Ibn Sina, and how this resolves the objections.

Core Idea
The sphere's will targets a universal position that is determinate, allowing perpetual motion.

Having presented the objections, the text now turns to Ibn Sina's resolution from al-Isharat. He clarifies that the object of the sphere's positional motion cannot be an existing particular position, nor a purely partial one, because that would stop the motion. Instead, it must be a specific but universal position, which the will posits and towards which the sphere moves perpetually. This lesson unpacks that crucial insight.

Guided Path
1
Read the resolution passage
Read the Arabic passage from 'وتحقيق ذلك' to the end. Follow Ibn Sina's argument step by step.
2
Understand why the object cannot be existent
Ibn Sina argues that if the desired position already exists, it is no longer sought. So the object must be non-existent.
3
Understand why it cannot be partial
If the object were a particular position, the motion towards it would terminate when reached. But the sphere's motion is perpetual, so it cannot be partial.
4
Grasp the idea of a universal determinate position
The solution is that the will posits a universal position that is determinate in the sense of being a specific type, but not a concrete individual. This allows perpetual motion.

Source Text

The Peripatetic and Avicennan Views on Celestial Souls
The author presents the Peripatetic and Avicennan views on celestial souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two classical positions on whether celestial souls are corporeal or abstract.

Core Idea
Celestial souls: corporeal forces or abstract intellects?

The nature of celestial souls has been debated for centuries. The Peripatetic school and Ibn Sina offered contrasting views, which set the stage for later disputes. In this passage, the author summarizes these foundational positions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the two views presented.
2
Identify the Peripatetic view
Note that the Peripatetic school, as described in al-Najat and al-Shifa, holds that celestial souls are corporeal powers imprinted in matter, like animal souls.
3
Note Ibn Sina's view
Ibn Sina in al-Isharat explicitly states that celestial souls are abstract, like human rational souls.
4
Compare the two views
Contrast the corporeal and abstract positions and consider their implications for the nature of celestial beings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المشهور من مذهب المشائين، والمذكور في النجاة والشفا، أن النفوس الفلكية قوى جسمانية منطبعة في المواد بمنزلة نفوسنا الحيوانية. وصرح في الإشارات بأن لها نفوساً مجردة، بمنزلة نفوسنا الناطقة. فقال الإمام فيجب أن يكون لكل فلك نفس مجردة، هي مبدأ الإرادة الكلية، ونفس منطبعة هي مبدأ الإرادة الجزئية. وردّ عليه الحكيم المحقق بأن هذا مما لم يذهب إليه أحد، وأن الجسم الواحد يمتنع أن يكون ذا نفسين. أعني ذا ذاتين متباينتين، هو آلة لهما، بل الإرادات الجزئية تنبعث عن إرادة كلية ومبدؤهما نفس واحدة مجردة، تدرك المعقولات بذاتها، والجزئيات بجسم الفلك، وتحرك الفلك بواسطة صورته النوعية التي هي باعتبار تحريكها قوة كما في نفوسنا وأبداننا بعينها. ولا يخفى أن هذا مناقشة في اللفظ حيث سمي تلك الصورة والقوة نفساً.
Know that the well-known [view] of the Peripatetic school, mentioned in al-Najat and al-Shifa, is that the celestial souls are corporeal powers imprinted in matter, like our animal souls. In al-Isharat, he [Ibn Sina] explicitly states that they have abstract souls, like our rational souls. So al-Imam [Fakhr al-Din al-Razi] said: It is necessary that each sphere has an abstract soul, which is the principle of universal will, and an imprinted soul, which is the principle of partial will. The wise investigator [al-Tusi] refuted him, saying that no one has held this, and that one body cannot have two souls—meaning two distinct essences of which it is an instrument. Rather, partial wills emanate from a universal will, and their source is one abstract soul. It perceives intelligibles by itself, and particulars through the sphere's body, and moves the sphere by means of its specific form, which is, in terms of moving it, a power, just as in our souls and bodies exactly. It is obvious that this is quibbling over words, since he [al-Tusi] called that form and power a 'soul'.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage surveys different opinions on the nature of celestial souls. The Peripatetic view (in al-Najat and al-Shifa) holds that they are corporeal forces embedded in matter, similar to animal souls. However, Ibn Sina in al-Isharat states they have abstract souls like human rational souls. Fakhr al-Razi synthesizes: each sphere must have both an abstract soul (for universal will) and an imprinted soul (for partial will). Al-Tusi rejects this, arguing one body cannot have two distinct souls; rather, partial wills derive from the universal will of one abstract soul, which uses the body for particulars and moves it via its specific form. The author concludes that this is a verbal dispute because al-Tusi merely relabels the imprinted power as a 'form' or 'power' rather than 'soul'.

Key Terms
نفوس فلكية
مشائون
نفس مجردة
نفس منطبعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which work of Ibn Sina states that celestial souls are abstract?
A) al-Najat
B) al-Shifa
C) al-Isharat
D) al-Qanun

Key Takeaway
There are two classical views: celestial souls as corporeal forces (Peripatetics) or as abstract intellects (Ibn Sina).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Ibn Sina presented two different views on celestial souls. Are you willing to change your own deeply held beliefs when you encounter a compelling argument or new evidence? Take a moment to identify one belief about God or the unseen that you hold rigidly, and ask yourself what it would take to revise it.
?
The Peripatetic view likens celestial souls to animal souls, while Ibn Sina's later view compares them to human rational souls. Which analogy brings you closer to understanding the grandeur of creation? How can you use that sense of wonder to deepen your worship today?
?
If even great scholars can disagree on the nature of celestial beings, what does that teach you about intellectual humility? Before you finish this reflection, commit to reading one primary source on this topic this week—perhaps from al-Shifa or al-Isharat—to engage directly with their reasoning.

Al-Razi's Synthesis and Al-Tusi's Objection
The author presents al-Razi's synthesis, al-Tusi's objection, and the verbal dispute resolution.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's attempt to combine both types of soul in each sphere, al-Tusi's objection, and the author's resolution that it is a verbal dispute.

Core Idea
One sphere, two souls? Or just one soul with two aspects?

After presenting the two foundational views, the author turns to al-Razi's innovative synthesis, which posits two souls per sphere. This sparked a strong refutation from al-Tusi. Finally, the author clarifies that the disagreement may be merely terminological.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Razi's argument
Read the Arabic and translation to understand al-Razi's claim that each sphere must have both an abstract and an imprinted soul.
2
Study al-Tusi's objection
Al-Tusi argues that one body cannot have two distinct souls; the partial wills derive from the universal will of a single abstract soul.
3
Understand the author's resolution
The author concludes that the dispute is verbal because al-Tusi merely relabels the imprinted power as a 'form' or 'power' rather than 'soul'.
4
Reflect on terminology
Consider how differences in terminology can create apparent disagreements where none exist.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن المشهور من مذهب المشائين، والمذكور في النجاة والشفا، أن النفوس الفلكية قوى جسمانية منطبعة في المواد بمنزلة نفوسنا الحيوانية. وصرح في الإشارات بأن لها نفوساً مجردة، بمنزلة نفوسنا الناطقة. فقال الإمام فيجب أن يكون لكل فلك نفس مجردة، هي مبدأ الإرادة الكلية، ونفس منطبعة هي مبدأ الإرادة الجزئية. وردّ عليه الحكيم المحقق بأن هذا مما لم يذهب إليه أحد، وأن الجسم الواحد يمتنع أن يكون ذا نفسين. أعني ذا ذاتين متباينتين، هو آلة لهما، بل الإرادات الجزئية تنبعث عن إرادة كلية ومبدؤهما نفس واحدة مجردة، تدرك المعقولات بذاتها، والجزئيات بجسم الفلك، وتحرك الفلك بواسطة صورته النوعية التي هي باعتبار تحريكها قوة كما في نفوسنا وأبداننا بعينها. ولا يخفى أن هذا مناقشة في اللفظ حيث سمي تلك الصورة والقوة نفساً.
Know that the well-known [view] of the Peripatetic school, mentioned in al-Najat and al-Shifa, is that the celestial souls are corporeal powers imprinted in matter, like our animal souls. In al-Isharat, he [Ibn Sina] explicitly states that they have abstract souls, like our rational souls. So al-Imam [Fakhr al-Din al-Razi] said: It is necessary that each sphere has an abstract soul, which is the principle of universal will, and an imprinted soul, which is the principle of partial will. The wise investigator [al-Tusi] refuted him, saying that no one has held this, and that one body cannot have two souls—meaning two distinct essences of which it is an instrument. Rather, partial wills emanate from a universal will, and their source is one abstract soul. It perceives intelligibles by itself, and particulars through the sphere's body, and moves the sphere by means of its specific form, which is, in terms of moving it, a power, just as in our souls and bodies exactly. It is obvious that this is quibbling over words, since he [al-Tusi] called that form and power a 'soul'.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage surveys different opinions on the nature of celestial souls. The Peripatetic view (in al-Najat and al-Shifa) holds that they are corporeal forces embedded in matter, similar to animal souls. However, Ibn Sina in al-Isharat states they have abstract souls like human rational souls. Fakhr al-Razi synthesizes: each sphere must have both an abstract soul (for universal will) and an imprinted soul (for partial will). Al-Tusi rejects this, arguing one body cannot have two distinct souls; rather, partial wills derive from the universal will of one abstract soul, which uses the body for particulars and moves it via its specific form. The author concludes that this is a verbal dispute because al-Tusi merely relabels the imprinted power as a 'form' or 'power' rather than 'soul'.

Key Terms
إمام
حكيم محقق
مناقشة لفظية
نفس مجردة
نفس منطبعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Tusi's objection is a substantive philosophical argument that refutes al-Razi's view.
Correction: The author argues that al-Tusi's objection is merely verbal; he relabels the imprinted power as 'form' or 'power', so the underlying reality is the same.

Checkpoint
What is al-Tusi's main objection to al-Razi's view?
A) Celestial souls are neither corporeal nor abstract.
B) One body cannot have two souls.
C) The spheres have no souls at all.
D) The abstract soul is unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over celestial souls is ultimately a verbal disagreement, as al-Tusi's objection merely renames the imprinted soul as a form or power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a heated disagreement about religion or ideas, do you ever stop to ask whether the conflict is over substance or just words? This lesson shows two great minds differing only in terminology. Consider a recent argument you had: was the heart of the matter reality or labels? What would change if you looked deeper?
?
Al-Razi tried to synthesize and al-Tusi insisted on precision. Which tendency dominates your approach to understanding Islam? Are you quick to reconcile differences or quick to enforce exact terms? How might this lesson move you to balance both, seeking truth without being trapped in language?
?
The author labels al-Tusi's objection 'quibbling over words.' But is all terminology trivial? Think of a truth you believe – is it anchored in the reality it describes or in the specific words you use to describe it? What one truth about Allah or the soul would you cling to even if its name changed tomorrow? Make a commitment to that reality, not its label.

The Object of Celestial Motion: Ibn Sina's Analysis
Ibn Sina's verification of the object of celestial will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's argument that the object of celestial motion is a specific position posited as universal, and why it cannot be existent or particular.

Core Idea
Celestial motion seeks a specific but universal position, not an existing particular.

Ibn Sina's analysis of celestial motion reveals a subtle point: the object of the sphere's will cannot be an actual position (since it would already be attained) nor a particular (since motion would stop). Instead, it must be a specific position that is posited as a universal by the will. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Ibn Sina uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the three characteristics of the object
The passage presents three key features: the object is specific (mu'ayyan), non-existent (mumtani' an yakun mawjudan), and not particular (la juz'iyyan). Find each one and note the reasoning.
3
Understand why it cannot be existent or particular
Ibn Sina argues that if the object were existent, it would already be attained and thus not sought. If it were particular, motion towards it would cease, contradicting eternal motion. Grasp these two arguments.
The eternal motion is 'sarmadiyya' meaning perpetual.
4
See how specific and universal are reconciled
The passage concludes that the object is a specific position posited as universal. The specific does not contradict universality because each individual of a universal has its own specific distinction. Understand this reconciliation.
This is a key point in Ibn Sina's metaphysics.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولا نسلّم أن القسر لا يكون إلا على خلاف الطبع ، وأن القاسر لا يكون إلا متشابهاً، ليلزم تشابه الحركات، وأن الكلى من الإرادة والإدراك لا يصلح مبدأ لخصوصيات الحركات لم لا يجوز أن تستند الحركات المتعاقبة إلى إرادات وإدراكات كلية متعاقبة لا إرادة وإدراك للحركة على الإطلاق.
وتحقيق ذلك ما أشار إليه ابن سينا في الإشارات، من أن المطلوب بالحركة الوضعية، لا يكون إلا الوضع المعين، ويمتنع أن يكون موجوداً، لأن الحاصل لا بطلب، وأن يكون في الحركة السرمدية جزئياً، لأن الحركة المتوجهة إليه تنقطع عنده، فمطلوب إرادة الفلك، يجب أن يكون وضعاً معيناً مفروضاً كلياً تفرضه الإرادة وتتجه إليه بالحركة والمتعين لا ينافي الكلية، لأن كل واحد من كل کلی، فله مع كليته تعين يمتاز به عن سائر آحاد ذلك الكلى. واعلم أن المشهور من مذهب المشائين، والمذكور في النجاة والشفا، أن النفوس الفلكية، قوى جسمانية منطبعة في المواد بمنزلة نفوسنا الحيوانية . وصرح في الإشارات بأن لها نفوساً مجردة، بمنزلة نفوسنا الناطقة. فقال الإمام فيجب أن يكون لكل فلك نفس مجردة، هي مبدأ الإرادة الكلية، ونفس منطبعة هي مبدأ الإرادة الجزئية .
وردّ عليه الحكيم المحقق. بأن هذا مما لم يذهب إليه أحد، وأن الجسم الواحد يمتنع أن يكون ذا نفسين. أعني ذا ذاتين متباينتين، هو آلة لهما، بل الإرادات الجزئية تنبعث عن إرادة كلية ومبدؤهما نفس واحدة مجردة، تدرك المعقولات بذاتها ، والجزئيات بجسم الفلك، وتحرك الفلك بواسطة صورته النوعية التي هي باعتبار تحريكها قوة كما في نفوسنا وأبداننا بعينها. ولا يخفى أن هذا مناقشة في اللفظ حيث سمي تلك الصورة والقوة نفساً.
We do not concede that compulsion cannot be except contrary to nature, and that the compeller cannot be except uniform, so that uniformity of motions would be necessary, and that the universal of will and perception is not suitable as a principle for particularities of motions. Why is it not permissible that successive motions be based on successive universal wills and perceptions, not a single will and perception for motion absolutely? The verification of that is what Ibn Sina indicated in al-Isharat: that the object sought by positional motion is nothing but the specific position, and it is impossible that it be existent, because what is attained is not sought, and impossible that it be particular in eternal motion, because the motion directed towards it would cease at it. So the object of the celestial will must be a specific position that is posited as universal, which the will posits and directs itself towards by motion, and the specific does not contradict universality, because every individual of a universal has, along with its universality, a specific distinction by which it is differentiated from the other individuals of that universal. Know that the well-known doctrine of the Peripatetics, and what is mentioned in al-Najat and al-Shifa, is that celestial souls are corporeal powers impressed in matters, like our animal souls. And he stated in al-Isharat that they have abstract souls, like our rational souls. So al-Imam said: it is necessary that each sphere has an abstract soul, which is the principle of universal will, and an impressed soul, which is the principle of particular will. But the wise investigator [Nasir al-Din al-Tusi] refuted him, saying that this is something no one has held, and that a single body cannot have two souls, i.e., two distinct essences for which it is an instrument. Rather, particular wills emanate from a universal will, and their principle is a single abstract soul, which perceives intelligibles by itself, and particulars by the celestial body, and moves the sphere by means of its specific form, which, by virtue of moving it, is a power, just as in our souls and bodies exactly. And it is not hidden that this is a quibble over wording, where he has called that form and power a soul.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage is a philosophical argument about the nature of celestial motion and the souls of the celestial spheres. The author (Taftazani) begins by refusing a prior objection: it is not necessary that compulsion (qasr) must be contrary to nature, nor that the compeller must be uniform, leading to uniformity of motions. Nor is it necessary that the universal (kulli) of will and perception cannot be a principle for particular motions; rather, successive motions can be based on successive universal wills and perceptions. He then presents Ibn Sina's verification from al-Isharat: the object of positional motion must be a specific position (wad' mu'ayyan), but this position cannot actually exist (since what is attained is no longer sought) and cannot be particular in eternal motion (because motion towards a particular would stop). Therefore, the object of the celestial will is a specific position that is posited as universal (wad' mu'ayyan mafrud kulli) – the will posits it and directs motion towards it. The specific does not negate universality, as each individual of a universal has its own specific distinction. Next, the author contrasts two doctrines: the Peripatetic view (in al-Najat and al-Shifa) that celestial souls are corporeal powers impressed in matter like animal souls; and Ibn Sina's view in al-Isharat that they have abstract souls like rational souls. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi (al-Imam) concluded that each sphere must have both an abstract soul (for universal will) and an impressed soul (for particular will). Nasir al-Din al-Tusi (al-Hakim al-Muhaqqiq) refuted this: no one held that a single body can have two souls (two distinct essences). Rather, particular wills arise from a universal will with a single abstract soul that perceives intelligibles by itself and particulars via the celestial body, and moves the sphere through its specific form (sura naw'iyya) which acts as a power, similar to how our souls move our bodies. The author concludes that this is merely a verbal quibble (munaqasha fi al-lafz), as Tusi has simply called that form and power a soul.

Key Terms
الوضع المعين
الحركة السرمدية
الإرادة الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the celestial sphere's motion aims at a particular existing location.
Correction: Ibn Sina argues that the object is not an existing particular but a specific position posited as universal.

Checkpoint
According to Ibn Sina in al-Isharat, what is the object of the celestial sphere's will?
A) A particular existing position
B) A universal non-existent position
C) A specific position posited as universal
D) An infinite series of positions

Key Takeaway
The object of celestial motion is a specific, non-existent position that the will posits as universal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Reflect on a goal you have been pursuing for a long time without ever feeling you have fully attained it. Ibn Sina says the celestial sphere's will targets a non-existent universal specific position. Could your own relentless pursuit be similarly aimed at something that, were it to become fully real, would end the motion? What does that reveal about the nature of your deepest desires? Will you examine whether the object of your pursuit is truly attainable or inherently infinite?
?
Ibn Sina argues that what is already attained is not sought. Think of something you have achieved that you thought would satisfy you, but soon afterwards you found yourself seeking something else. Does this pattern mimic the celestial motion? What does this cycle of attainment and renewed desire tell you about the limits of worldly goals? How might you redirect your will towards a universal good that does not diminish when partially realized?

The Nature of Celestial Souls: Debates Among Philosophers
Taftazani presents the debate on celestial souls between Peripatetics, Ibn Sina, al-Razi, and al-Tusi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different views on celestial souls (corporeal vs abstract) and the debate between al-Razi and al-Tusi over whether a sphere has one or two souls.

Core Idea
The celestial sphere has one abstract soul, not two separate souls for universal and particular will.

Medieval philosophers debated whether celestial spheres have souls and what kind. The Peripatetics viewed them as corporeal powers, Ibn Sina argued for abstract souls, al-Razi proposed a dual-soul theory, and al-Tusi refuted it. This lesson examines that debate and the resolution that the sphere moves via its specific form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the different positions presented.
Taftazani presents multiple views and a critical comment.
2
Identify the four positions
The passage presents: (1) Peripatetic view, (2) Ibn Sina's view, (3) al-Razi's synthesis, (4) al-Tusi's refutation. List them and notice who held each.
3
Understand al-Razi's argument and al-Tusi's refutation
Al-Razi argued that each sphere needs an abstract soul for universal will and an impressed soul for particular will. Al-Tusi refuted that a single body cannot have two souls; rather, one abstract soul suffices.
Al-Tusi is called 'al-Hakim al-Muhaqqiq' (the wise investigator).
4
Reflect on Taftazani's conclusion
Taftazani remarks that the disagreement is merely verbal (munaqasha fi al-lafz), as al-Tusi has simply called the form and power a 'soul'. Understand why he says this.
This shows that sometimes philosophical debates are about terminology.

?

?

Refuting the Connection Between Soul Differences and Temperament
Author refutes the argument that soul differences stem from temperament.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument that soul differences are caused by bodily temperaments is logically weak and insufficient.

Core Idea
Similar temperaments do not guarantee similar characters, so the argument fails.

This passage presents a refutation of those who claim that differences in souls' attributes are due to bodily temperaments and external factors rather than to essential differences. By examining the logical consequence of this view, the author shows that it leads to a false conclusion, thereby weakening the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to understand the logical flow. Notice the conditional structure: 'if...then...'.
2
Trace the logical argument
Identify the claim (others' position), the conditional consequence (if temperaments cause differences, then similar bodies yield similar characters), and the refutation (the consequent is false).
3
Evaluate the conclusion
Understand why the author labels the argument a 'weak persuasion'—because observed differences could stem from unknown causes.
Think about what 'other causes' could be.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الاخرون بأن اختلاف النفوس في صفاتها ، لو لم يكن لاختلاف ماهياتها، بل لاختلاف الأمزجة والاحوال البدنية، والأسباب الخارجية لكانت الاشخاص المتقاربة جداً في أحوال البدن والأسباب الخارجة متقاربة البتة في الملكات، والأخلاق من الرحمة والقسوة والكرم والبخل والعفة والفجور وبالعكس واللازم باطل ، إذ كثيراً ما يوجد الامر بخلاف ذلك، بل ربما يوجد الإنسان الواحد قد تبدل مزاجه جداً، وهو على عريزته الاولى. ولا خفاء في أن هذا من الإقناعيات الضعيفة لجواز أن يكون ذلك لأسباب أخر لا نطلع على تفاصيلها.
Others [say] that the difference of souls in their attributes, if it were not due to the difference of their essences, but due to the difference of temperaments, bodily states, and external causes, then persons very similar in bodily states and external causes would necessarily be similar in dispositions and morals, such as mercy, hardness, generosity, stinginess, modesty, lewdness, and the opposite. And the necessary consequence is false, for often the matter is found contrary to that; rather, one single person may have his temperament changed greatly while he is upon his original nature. And it is not hidden that this is from the weak persuasions, because it is possible that that [difference] is due to other causes whose details we do not know.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes an argument given by others (likely philosophers) that the differences among human souls in their attributes and character traits are not due to differences in their essences but rather due to bodily temperaments, physical conditions, and external factors. The author argues that if that were the case, then individuals who are very similar in physical constitution and external circumstances would necessarily be very similar in their moral qualities and character traits. However, the consequent is false because we often observe the opposite: people with very similar bodily conditions have very different characters. Moreover, a single individual may undergo a significant change in temperament while still retaining his original character. Therefore, the argument is merely a weak persuasion, since the observed differences could be due to other causes beyond our knowledge.

أمزجة

إقناعيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: Differences in souls' attributes are entirely due to bodily temperaments and external factors.
Correction: The author shows this is not necessary, as similar temperaments can correspond to different characters, and individual character can persist despite changing temperament.

Checkpoint

C) Because people with similar temperaments often have different characters, and even the same person can change temperament while keeping his nature
D) Because souls have no attributes

Key Takeaway

Two Proofs for the Temporality of the Soul
Author enumerates two proofs for the soul's temporality with objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational arguments for the soul's createdness: one based on its inactivity before attachment and one based on the body's temperament as a condition, along with the key distinction between condition of origination and condition of existence.

Core Idea
The soul is created because it becomes active only upon attachment and depends on a temporal condition.

In this passage, the author presents the first two proofs that the soul is created (haditha). The first considers the soul's state before attachment—it is inactive, and inactivity is not an attribute of existing things. The second argues that the soul's emanation depends on the body's specific temperament, a temporal event, so the soul must be temporal. Objections and replies refine these arguments, clarifying that the body is a condition for the soul's origination, not its continued existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the structure: first proof, second proof, objection, reply, then a second objection.
2
Identify the two proofs
The first proof: the soul is inactive before attachment, and inactivity does not exist. The second: the soul arises from the body's temporal temperament.
Key terms: تعطل (inactivity), مزاج (temperament).
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objection says: if the condition (body) is removed, the soul would cease. The reply: the condition is for origination (huduth), not for existence. This distinction is crucial.
4
Consider the second objection
The second objection says the soul is not inactive because it watches for perfection, and temperament is a condition for attachment, not creation. This challenges both proofs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: أنها قبل التعلق تكون معطلة، ولا تعطل في الوجود بخلاف ما بعد المفارقة، فإنها في روح وريحان، أو عذاب ونيران . الثاني: أنه إذا حدث للبدن مزاجه الخاص، فاضت عليه نفس تناسب استعداده لعموم الفيض، والمشروط بالحادث حادث . فإن قيل : فيلزم انتفاؤه بانتفائه :قلنا هو شرط الحدوث لا الوجود . واعترض بأن المترصد لاكتساب الكمال لا يكون معطلاً، وبأن المزاج شرط التعلق لا الحدوث.
The first: that before attachment (ta'alluq) they are inactive (mu'attala), and there is no inactivity in existence, unlike after separation, for they are in repose and fragrance, or in torment and fires. The second: that when the body's specific temperament occurs, a soul flows over it proportionate to its preparedness due to the universality of emanation, and that which is conditioned by the temporal (hadith) is temporal. If it is said: then its negation follows from its negation. We say: it is a condition of temporality (huduth), not of existence. And it was objected that the one who watches for the acquisition of perfection is not inactive, and that the temperament is a condition of attachment, not of temporality.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage enumerates the first two proofs (wujuh) for those who say the soul is created (haditha). The first proof: before attachment (ta'alluq) to the body, the soul is inactive (mu'attala). Inactivity does not exist in actual existence, unlike after death when the soul experiences either bliss or torment. Therefore, the soul must come into existence at attachment. The second proof: when the body acquires its specific temperament, a soul emanates upon it that fits its preparedness, because emanation is universal. Since the condition (the body's occurrence) is temporal, the conditioned (the soul) is also temporal. Then an objection is raised: if the condition is removed, the conditioned would be removed, implying that the soul would cease with the body. The author answers: the condition is for the soul's temporal origination (huduth), not for its continued existence. A further objection states that the soul prior to attachment is not inactive because it is watching for the acquisition of perfection, and that the temperament is a condition for attachment, not for the soul's creation. This objection challenges both proofs.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The soul's existence is entirely dependent on the body; if the body dies, the soul ceases.
Correction: The body is a condition for the soul's origination (huduth), not for its continued existence. The soul persists after death.

Checkpoint

B) The condition is for existence, not origination
C) The condition is for origination (huduth), not for existence
D) The objection is ignored

Key Takeaway
The soul is created; its temporal origination depends on the body, but its continued existence does not.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Are you content to be 'inactive' in your spiritual life, waiting for circumstances to change? The proof shows that inactivity is not a state of existence—either you are actively pursuing perfection (like the soul watching for acquisition of perfection) or you are non-existent in terms of spiritual growth. Which are you right now?
?
The objection says the soul is 'watching for the acquisition of perfection' even before attachment. What does your soul long for? Are you actively acquiring perfection, or are you passively expecting it to emanate upon you without effort? What specific step will you take today to move towards that perfection?

Philosophers' Two Proofs for the Soul's Immortality
Philosophers' two rational proofs for soul's immortality

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational proofs the philosophers use to argue that the soul cannot be annihilated, and the author's critical remark on the first proof.

Core Idea
The soul's immortality is argued from causal dependence and simplicity.

The philosophers present two distinct rational arguments for the soul's immortality. The first argument is based on the soul's dependence on an eternal cause, while the second argues from the soul's nature as a simple substance and the impossibility of it containing both subsistence and potential for corruption. The author presents these arguments while noting a clear objection to the first one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the full literal translation. Let the philosophical argument flow naturally.
The passage is dense with logical terms; take your time.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage explicitly lists two proofs (وجهان). Find the first proof (أحدهما) and the second proof (وثانيهما). Notice how each proof is structured.
The first proof has two sub-cases depending on how the soul depends on the eternal cause.
3
Examine the author's objection
After presenting the first proof, the author adds a critical remark: 'وعليه منع ظاهر' (this is clearly objectionable). Consider why the author might find this proof weak.
This objection is key to understanding the author's stance toward the philosophers' arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة فزعموا أنه يمتنع فناء في النفس بوجهين : أحدهما : أنها مستندة إلى علة قديمة إما بالاستقلال فتكون أزلية أبدية وإما بشرط حادث هو المزاج الصالح فلا تكون أزلية لكنها أبدية لأن ذلك شرط للحدوث دون البقاء وعليه منع ظاهر وثانيهما: أنها لو كانت قابلة للفناء والفساد وهي باقية بالفعل لكان فيها فعل البقاء وقوة الفساد وهما متغايران ضرورة ويمتنع أن يكون محليهما واحداً لأن محل قبول الشيء يكون باقياً معه موصوفاً به ومحال أن يكون الباقي بالفعل باقياً مع الفناء الفساد والنفس جوهر بسيط محل للبقاء بالفعل فيمتنع أن يكون بعينها محلاً لقوة الفساد أو مشتملة عليه فلا تكون هي ولا شيء من المجردات قابلة للفناء والفساد وإنما يكون ذلك للصور والأعراض ويكون القابل هو المادة الباقية
As for the philosophers, they claimed that the soul's annihilation is impossible in two ways: First: it depends on an eternal cause, either independently so it is pre-eternal and post-eternal, or with a contingent condition which is the suitable temperament, so it is not pre-eternal but it is post-eternal because that condition is for origination not survival, and this is clearly objectionable. Second: if it were susceptible to annihilation and corruption while being actual in subsistence, there would be in it the act of subsistence and the potential for corruption, which are necessarily distinct, and it is impossible for their substrate to be one, because the substrate of acceptance of a thing remains with it described by it, and it is impossible for that which is actually subsistent to remain with annihilation and corruption. And the soul is a simple substance, a substrate for actual subsistence, so it is impossible for it to be a substrate for the potential of corruption or to contain it. Hence neither it nor any of the simple entities are susceptible to annihilation and corruption; that is only for forms and accidents, and the acceptor is the remaining matter.
Source pages p. 331

Commentary
This passage presents the two rational proofs of the philosophers for the soul's immortality. The first proof argues that the soul depends on an eternal cause: either it is directly from an eternal cause, making it eternal in both directions, or it arises from a temporary condition (the balanced temperament) but remains eternal afterwards because the condition only affects origination, not survival. The author notes this is clearly objectionable ('man‘ ẓāhir'). The second proof argues that if the soul were susceptible to annihilation while actually existing, it would contain both the act of subsistence and the potential for corruption, which require distinct substrates; but the soul is a simple substance (jawhar basīṭ) serving as a substrate for actual subsistence, so it cannot also contain the potential for corruption. Therefore, neither the soul nor any simple entity can be annihilated or corrupted; that applies only to forms and accidents which depend on matter.

علة قديمة
مزاج صالح
جوهر بسيط
قوة الفساد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might assume that the philosophers' proofs are universally accepted or logically sound.
Correction: The author explicitly flags the first proof as 'clearly objectionable' (عليه منع ظاهر), indicating that not all scholars accept it.

Checkpoint

C) The soul manages the body
D) The soul is simple

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that the soul cannot be annihilated because it depends on an eternal cause and because its simplicity prevents it from containing the potential for corruption.

Does Non-Existential Potential Require a Substrate?
A rebuttal and answer on non-existential potential needing a substrate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debate over whether non-existential potential requires a substrate and how the answer distinguishes between rational and non-rational potential.

Core Idea
Not all potential needs a substrate — only accidental potential does.

This passage engages a critical point in the debate about the soul's annihilation. The philosophers argued that the potential for annihilation requires a material substrate, implying the soul is material. The rebuttal and answer here refine the concept of potential, showing that only accidental potentials require a substrate, while the soul's relation to the body is not such a potential.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then the translation. Notice the flow of rebuttal and answer.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two parts
The passage has a rebuttal (first two sentences) and an answer (from 'قد يجاب' onwards). Note the concession in the second sentence.
The rebuttal denies the need for a substrate; the answer insists on it for accidental potential.
3
Analyze the answer's key points
The answer states that preparatory potential is an accident requiring a substrate. Then it contrasts the rational potential of the embryo's body for receiving a soul with the non-rational 'potential' for the soul to cease when temperament corrupts.
The key distinction is between a genuine rational potential and a mere cessation of relation.
4
Understand the conclusion
The corruption of temperament does not cause the soul to cease; it only severs the relation. Thus, the soul does not require a material substrate for annihilation.
This refutes the philosophers' argument that the soul must be material.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورد هذا الدليل بأنا لا نسلم أن قوة قبول الأمر العدمي كالفناء مثلاً يقتضي وجود محل لها يجتمع مع المقبول ولو سلم فقد سبق أن الحدوث أيضاً يقتضي مادة ويكفي المادة التي تتعلق بها النفس من غير حلول فلم لا يكفي مثلها في قوة الفناء قد يجاب بأن القوة الاستعدادية عرض فلا بد له من محل سواء كان استعداداً لقبول أمر وجودي أو عدمي ثم استعداد بدن الجنين بما له من اعتدال المزاج لأن يفيض عليه من المبدأ نفس تدبره معنى معقول وأما استعداده ببطلان ذلك المزاج لأن ينعدم ذلك فغير معقول بل غايته أن ينعدم ما بينهما من العلاقة وهو لا يقتضي الفناء
This proof was rebutted: we do not concede that the potential for accepting a non-existential matter, such as annihilation, requires a substrate that coexists with the accepted. And even if conceded, it was already mentioned that origination also requires matter, and the matter that relates to the soul without indwelling suffices, so why would not something similar suffice for the potential of annihilation? It may be answered: the preparatory potential is an accident, so it must have a substrate, whether it is a potential for accepting an existential or non-existential matter. Then the potential of the embryo's body with its balanced temperament for the emanation upon it from the Principle of a soul that manages it is a rational meaning, but its potential through the corruption of that temperament for that (soul) to cease is not rational; rather at most the relation between them ceases, which does not necessitate annihilation.
Source pages p. 332

Commentary
This passage contains a rebuttal (radd) of the philosophers' proof and a subsequent answer (jawāb). The rebuttal denies that the potential for accepting non-existential matters (like annihilation) requires a substrate, and even if it did, the matter that relates to the soul without indwelling (as in origination) would suffice. The answer responds that preparatory potential is an accident ('araḍ) and thus needs a substrate regardless of whether it concerns existential or non-existential reception. The answer then illustrates with the example of the embryo: its balanced temperament prepares it to receive a managing soul from the Principle, which is a rational concept (ma‘nā ma‘qūl). However, the potential for the soul to cease due to the corruption of that temperament is not rational; at most, the relational tie between soul and body is severed, which does not entail the soul's annihilation.

مادة
اعتدال المزاج
العلاقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any potential requires a substrate, so the potential for annihilation implies the soul has a material substrate.
Correction: Only accidental potential requires a substrate; the soul's relation to the body is not an accidental potential, so cessation of relation does not imply annihilation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Two Proofs for the Existence of the Intellect
The author presents two arguments for the existence of the intellect and explains their basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two rational arguments for the existence of the intellect and the philosophical principles on which they rest.

Core Idea
The intellect must be the first creation because only it can be both singular and independent.

This lesson presents two proofs for the existence of the intellect from the classical theological tradition. The first proof argues from the nature of the first created thing, and the second from the cause of the first body. Both proofs rely on the principle that multiplicity cannot arise from a single cause and that the first effect must be an intermediary, not a creator. Through these arguments, the author establishes the intellect's unique status.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage in Arabic and translation
Read the source passage carefully in both Arabic and English to get the author's own wording.
The Arabic includes some parenthetical markers; ignore them for now.
2
Understand the first proof
The first proof argues that the first created thing must exist alone and produce what comes after it. Consider why body, hyle, form, accident, and soul are excluded.
3
Understand the second proof
The second proof argues that the cause of the first body must contain multiplicity and be independent of corporeality. Note why each candidate fails.
4
Grasp the common basis
Both proofs rest on the impossibility of multiplicity emanating from the One and the negation of choice and attributes. Reflect on this principle.
5
Consider the objection and response
The author notes that the first effect might be an intermediary, so it could be a soul or part of body. Understand how this relates to the proofs.
This shows the author's awareness of counterarguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ( الفصل الثاني في العقل : وفيه مباحث : المبحث الأول في إثباته. وفيه وجوه : الأول: أول المخلوقات يلزم أن يوجد وحده، ويوجد ما بعده، وما ذاك إلا العقل)، لأن للجسم كثرة، وللهيولي (۳)، أو الصورة أو العرض افتقاراً إلى غير علته في الوجود، وللنفس في الإيجاد، وإلا لكان عقلاً. الثاني : علة أول الأجسام يلزم أن تشتمل على الكثرة لئلا يتعدد أثر الواحد، ويستغني في ذاتها وفعلها عن الجسمية لئلا يفضي إلى تقدم الشيء على نفسه. أما الجسم والعرض فظاهر، وأما النفس فلأن فعلها مشروط بالجسم، وأما الهيولي والصورة، فلأنه لا يحصل أحدهما بدون الأخرى. ومبنى الوجهين على امتناع صدور الكثير من الواحد، ونفي الاختيار والصفات مع أن المعلول الأول لا يلزم أن يكون موجـداً لما بعده، بل واسطة فلا يمتنع أن يكون نفساً، أو أحد جزئي الجسم، وأيضاً افتقار النفس إلى الجسم في إدراكاتها لا يمنع استقلالها بإيجاد الجسم.
He said (The second chapter on the intellect: It contains investigations: The first investigation on its proof. It contains arguments: The first: The first of created things must exist alone, and what comes after it exists, and that is nothing but the intellect), because the body has multiplicity, and hyle (3), or form, or accident have need of something other than their cause in existence, and the soul has need in bringing into existence, otherwise it would be an intellect. The second: The cause of the first of bodies must include multiplicity so that the effect of the one does not become multiple, and be independent in its essence and its action from corporeality so as not to lead to the priority of a thing over itself. As for body and accident, it is obvious; as for the soul, its action is conditioned by the body; as for hyle and form, neither of them occurs without the other. And the basis of the two arguments is the impossibility of the emanation of multiplicity from the One, and the negation of choice and attributes, while the first effect need not be the giver of existence to what comes after it, but rather an intermediary, so it is not impossible that it be a soul, or one of the two parts of the body. Also, the soul's need of the body in its perceptions does not prevent its independence in bringing the body into existence.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage is a proof section from the chapter on the intellect. The author presents two arguments (وجوه) for the existence of the intellect and then explains their common basis. The first argument: The first created thing must exist alone and produce what comes after it. Only the intellect fits this, because body has multiplicity (thus cannot be single), hyle/form/accident depend on something other than their cause (so they are not independent), and the soul depends on the body for its creative act. If something else were first, it would itself have to be an intellect. The second argument: The cause of the first body must contain multiplicity to prevent multiple effects from a single cause, and it must be independent of corporeality to avoid circularity. Body and accident clearly depend on matter; the soul's action depends on the body; hyle and form require each other. Thus none of these can be the cause of the first body. The author then explains that both arguments rest on the principle that multiplicity cannot proceed from the One, and the rejection of choice and attributes. He notes that the first effect need not itself create what follows; it may be an intermediary. Therefore it could be a soul or a part of the body. The soul's need for the body in perception does not negate its independence in creating the body. This discussion sets the stage for further investigation of the intellect's nature.

Key Terms
العقل
الهيولي
الصورة
العرض
النفس
الجسم
المعلول الأول

Key Terms from Source Passage

The First Proof: The Intellect as the Sole First Creation
First proof that the intellect is the first creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that the intellect must be the first created thing because only it exists alone and causes subsequent things.

Core Idea
The first creation must be singular and causative; only the intellect qualifies.

The author begins his proof of the intellect as the first creation by establishing a criterion: the first created thing must exist alone and yet be the cause of everything after it. He then examines candidates—body, matter, form, accident, soul—and eliminates each except the intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the first proof
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the author's reasoning: the first creation must be alone and causative.
2
Identify the rejected candidates
List why body, matter/form/accident, and soul fail the criterion.
The body has multiplicity; matter/form/accident depend on other causes; the soul depends on the body.
3
Understand the conclusion
The only remaining candidate is the intellect. Consider why it satisfies the criteria.
The intellect is immaterial and singular yet generates multiplicity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل الثاني في العقل : وفيه مباحث : المبحث الأول في إثباته. وفيه وجوه : الأول: أول المخلوقات يلزم أن يوجد وحده، ويوجد ما بعده، وما ذاك إلا العقل، لأن للجسم كثرة، وللهيولي أو الصورة أو العرض افتقاراً إلى غير علته في الوجود، وللنفس في الإيجاد، وإلا لكان عقلاً. الثاني : علة أول الأجسام يلزم أن تشتمل على الكثرة لئلا يتعدد أثر الواحد، ويستغني في ذاتها وفعلها عن الجسمية لئلا يفضي إلى تقدم الشيء على نفسه. أما الجسم والعرض فظاهر، وأما النفس فلأن فعلها مشروط بالجسم، وأما الهيولي والصورة، فلأنه لا يحصل أحدهما بدون الأخرى. ومبنى الوجهين على امتناع صدور الكثير من الواحد، ونفي الاختيار والصفات مع أن المعلول الأول لا يلزم أن يكون موجـداً لما بعده، بل واسطة فلا يمتنع أن يكون نفساً، أو أحد جزئي الجسم، وأيضاً افتقار النفس إلى الجسم في إدراكاتها لا يمنع استقلالها بإيجاد الجسم.
Chapter Two on the Intellect. It contains investigations: The first investigation on proving it. And it contains aspects: The first: The first of created things necessarily exists alone, and what comes after it exists, and that is nothing but the intellect, because the body has multiplicity, and matter or form or accident has dependence on something other than its cause in existence, and the soul in bringing into existence, otherwise it would be an intellect. The second: The cause of the first body necessarily includes multiplicity so that the effect of the one does not become multiple, and is independent in its essence and its action from corporeality so that it does not lead to the precedence of a thing over itself. As for the body and accident, it is obvious; as for the soul, because its action is conditioned by the body; as for matter and form, because neither occurs without the other. And the basis of the two aspects is the impossibility of the emanation of multiplicity from the One, and the negation of choice and attributes, although the first effect does not necessarily have to be the bringer into existence of what comes after it, but rather an intermediary, so it is not impossible that it be a soul or one of the two parts of the body, and also the dependence of the soul on the body in its perceptions does not prevent its independence in bringing the body into existence.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments (وجوه) by the author to prove that the intellect is the first created thing. The first argument states that the first creation must exist alone and produce subsequent things. Only the intellect qualifies because bodies have multiplicity, matter/form/accident depend on something beyond their cause, and the soul depends on the body for its actions; otherwise each would be an intellect. The second argument focuses on the cause of the first body: it must incorporate multiplicity to avoid multiple effects from one cause, and be independent of corporeality to avoid self-precedence. The author explains why body, accident, soul, matter, and form each fail. He adds that both arguments rest on the principle that multiplicity cannot come from the One, assuming the negation of choice and attributes (a reference to the philosophers' view). He notes that the first effect need not create subsequent things but is merely an intermediary, so it could be a soul or a part of a body, and the soul's dependence on the body does not prevent it from being the cause of the body.
