The Second Proof and Its Philosophical Basis
Second proof for the intellect and its philosophical basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the intellect as the first creation and the principle of emanation underlying both proofs.

Core Idea
The cause of the first body must be independent of corporeality; only the intellect can be.

The author presents a second argument focusing on the cause of the first body. This cause must incorporate multiplicity and be independent of bodies, otherwise we get a logical contradiction. He eliminates body, accident, soul, matter, and form, and then discusses the basis of both proofs: the principle that multiplicity cannot come from one simple cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the second proof
Read the Arabic and translation. The author sets conditions for the cause of the first body.
2
Understand the conditions
The cause must contain multiplicity to avoid multiple effects from one simple cause, and be independent of corporeality to avoid self-precedence.
Self-precedence means the cause would have to precede itself.
3
Examine the eliminations
The author shows why body, accident, soul, matter, and form fail.
Soul fails because its action depends on body; matter and form need each other.
4
Grasp the underlying principle
Both proofs rely on the impossibility of multiplicity emanating from the One. The author notes that the first effect need not create subsequent things but can be an intermediary.

The First Effect Must Be a Separate Substance (Intellect)
Philosophers argue the first effect must be a separate substance (intellect).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' first proof that the first effect must be an intellect, and why it cannot be a body, soul, accident, matter, or form.

Core Idea
The first effect cannot be a body, soul, or accident — it must be an immaterial intellect.

The philosophers present a rigorous argument to establish the nature of the first creation. Using reductio ad absurdum, they show that the first effect must be a separate substance (جوهر مفارق) in both essence and action, which is the intellect. This passage walks through the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the full translation. Get a sense of the argument's flow before analyzing details.
The passage is dense; slow reading helps.
2
Identify the argument structure
Notice that the proof has two main parts: establishing substantiality (جوهرية) and establishing immateriality (مفارقة). Each part eliminates candidates for the first effect.
The conclusion is that only the intellect fits.
3
Trace the eliminations
Follow why the first effect cannot be an accident, a body (composite of matter and form), matter alone, form alone, or a soul. Each leads to an absurdity like multiplicity from the One or self-precedence.
The key principle: from the simple One, only one simple effect can proceed.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The only remaining possibility is the intellect: separate in essence and action, capable of causing the second effect without absurdity.
This intellect is the first created being in their cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتجت الفلاسفة على ثبوته بوجوه : أحدها: أن المعلول الأول يجب أن يكون جوهراً مفارقاً ( أي ليس جسماً ولا جسمانياً ) في ذاته وفعله، وهو المراد بالعقل، أما الجوهرية فلأن العرض لا يمكن بدون المحل. فالمحل إما معلول للعلة الأولى أعني الواجب فيلزم صدور الكثير أعني العرض، والمحل من الواحد الحقيقي، وأما العرض فيلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، وأما المفارقة فلأنه لو كان جسماً وهو مركب من المادة والصورة لزم المحال المذكور، وإن كان مادة أو صورة، وكل منهما لا يوجد بدون الآخر، لزم فاعلية أحدهما للآخر وهو محال، وأما المادة، فلأن شأنها القبول دون العقل، وأما الصورة فلأنها إنما تفعل بمشاركة المادة، فيلزم تقدمها على نفسها، وإن كان نفساً أي مفارقاً في ذاته لا في فعله، فالبدن الذي هو شرط الفاعلية إما معلول للواجب فيلزم الكثرة، أو للنفس فيلزم تقدمه على نفسه، فصار الحاصل أن لنا أمراً صح وجوده عن العلة الأولى، وإيجاده للمعلول الثاني، وما ذلك إلا العقل، لأن الجسم لما فيه من الكثرة لا يصلح معلولاً للعلة الأولى وغيره لا يصلح علة للمعلول الثاني لأن ما يصلح منه للعلية يفتقر في عليته، إلى أمر خارج عن ذاته، فإن كان معلولاً له لزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، وإن كان معلولاً للعلة الأولى لزم صدور الكثرة عنها.
The philosophers argued for its existence with [several] aspects: One of them: The first effect must be a separate substance (i.e., not a body nor corporeal) in its essence and its action, and that is what is meant by the intellect. As for substantiality, because an accident cannot exist without a substrate. So the substrate is either an effect of the First Cause, i.e., the Necessary, in which case the emanation of multiplicity – namely the accident and the substrate – from the true One would follow; or if it is an accident, the precedence of a thing over itself would follow. As for separation (immateriality), because if it were a body, which is composed of matter and form, the mentioned impossibility would follow; if it were matter or form, each of which does not exist without the other, the effectiveness of one for the other would follow, which is impossible. As for matter, because its function is receptivity, not the intellect; as for form, because it only acts with the participation of matter, so its precedence over itself would follow. And if it were a soul – i.e., separate in its essence but not in its action – then the body, which is a condition for its effectiveness, is either an effect of the Necessary, in which case multiplicity follows, or an effect of the soul, in which case its precedence over itself follows. So the result is that there is a thing whose existence from the First Cause is sound, and its bringing the second effect into existence is [also sound], and that is nothing but the intellect. Because the body, due to the multiplicity in it, is not suitable as an effect of the First Cause, and other than it is not suitable as a cause for the second effect, because what is suitable for causality among them is dependent in its causality on something external to its essence. If that external is an effect of it, the precedence of a thing over itself follows; if it is an effect of the First Cause, the emanation of multiplicity from It follows.
Source pages p. 355

Commentary
This passage presents the first of the philosophers' proofs for the existence of the intellect as the first effect. The proof argues that the first effect must be a separate substance (جوهر مفارق) in both essence and action, i.e., an intellect. First, it establishes substantiality: the effect cannot be an accident because an accident requires a substrate, leading to either multiplicity from the One or self-precedence. Second, it establishes separation (immateriality): if it were a body (composite of matter and form), absurdity results; if matter or form, one would have to act on the other, impossible; if a soul (separate in essence but dependent on body for action), the body as condition leads to either multiplicity or self-precedence. Therefore, the first effect must be an intellect. The conclusion is that the intellect is the only candidate that can both exist from the First Cause and bring the second effect into existence without falling into these absurdities.

جوهر مفارق
المعلول الأول
العلة الأولى

Correction: On the contrary, they posit the intellect as the first created being, an intermediary that then causes the rest of existence.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that the first effect of the One must be a single, immaterial intellect, which is both substance and separate in essence and action.

Are your daily activities driven by a single, sincere purpose, or are they scattered? Commit to one action today that reflects a unified intention.
?
The proof eliminates all physical and soul-based candidates for the first effect. In your spiritual journey, are you relying on physical rituals or emotional states (like a 'soul') rather than intellectual understanding and certainty? What step will you take to strengthen your intellectual engagement with your faith?

The Cause of the First Body Must Be an Intellect
Philosophers prove the cause of the first body must be an intellect, not a body or soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' second proof that the cause of the first body cannot be a body, soul, matter, or form, but must be an intellect.

Core Idea
The cause of the first body must be an intellect, otherwise self-precedence occurs.

In this passage, the philosophers argue that the first body (the outermost sphere) must have a cause that is an intellect. They systematically rule out other possibilities—body, accident, soul, matter, form—by showing each leads to logical contradiction. This lesson walks through their reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: a main claim followed by a series of alternatives that are eliminated.
The passage uses a compact style typical of kalam arguments.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage begins with the claim that the cause of the first body must be an intellect. Focus on this statement.
3
Examine the alternatives
The proof considers what would happen if the cause were not an intellect. It lists several possibilities: necessary being, something else (possible), body, accident, soul, matter, form. For each, it shows an absurdity.
Pay special attention to the concept of 'self-precedence' (tawqud al-shay' 'ala nafsihi).
4
Understand the logic of self-precedence
If the cause were a soul, matter, etc., the cause would require a body to act, leading to the body existing before its own existence—a logical impossibility.
5
Conclude the necessity of an intellect
After eliminating all alternatives, the only remaining possibility is that the cause is an intellect, which is immaterial and can act without a body.
This proof is foundational for the philosophers' cosmology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها : أن علة أول الأجسام يجب أن تكون عقلاً، وإلا لكان إما واجباً فيلزم صدور الكثير عنه، وإما غيره فيلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، أما إذا كان جسماً أو عرضاً قائماً به فظاهر، وأما إذا كان نفساً ، فلأن فعلها مشروط بالجسم، وإلا لكان عقلاً لا نفساً، فذلك الجسم . أما الجسم الأول فيتقدم على نفسه بمرتبة. وأما الثاني والثالث فيتقدم بمراتب، وأما إذا كان مادة أو صورة، فلأن كلا منهما لا يوجد بدون الأخرى ومجموعهما جسم ، فلو كان فاعل الجسم الأول إحداهما لكان قبل الجسم الأول ،جسم وفيه تقدم الشيء على نفسه بمرتبة، أو بمراتب.
And the second of them: The cause of the first body must be an intellect. Otherwise, it would either be necessary, in which case the emanation of multiplicity from it would follow, or something else, in which case the precedence of a thing over itself would follow. As for if it were a body or an accident subsisting in it, it is obvious; as for if it were a soul, because its action is conditioned by the body – otherwise it would be an intellect, not a soul – then that body: the first body would precede itself by one rank, and the second and third by ranks. As for if it were matter or form, because each of them does not exist without the other, and their combination is a body. So if the agent of the first body were one of them, then before the first body there would be a body, and in it (so that body would) precede itself by one rank or by ranks.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage presents the second philosophers' proof. It argues that the cause of the first body must be an intellect. The proof considers alternatives: if it were the Necessary Being (واجب), multiplicity would emanate from it; if it were something else (ممكن), it would lead to self-precedence. The author then examines specific possibilities: body or accident – obviously problematic; soul – its action depends on a body, so the body would be prior to the soul's effect, causing self-precedence; matter or form – they are interdependent and together form a body, so the agent would be preceded by a body, again causing self-precedence. Thus, the only possibility is that the cause of the first body is an intellect, free from these absurdities.

Key Terms
علة أول الأجسام
العقل
الواجب
النفس
المادة
الصورة
تقدم الشيء على نفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers intend to prove the existence of a separate substance called 'intellect' as the first creation, distinct from the First Cause.
Correction: Here they only argue that the cause of the first body (the outermost sphere) is an intellect, not that this intellect is the First Cause. The argument is about the immediate cause of the first body, not the ultimate cause.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what would happen if the cause of the first body were a soul?
A) The soul would be independent
B) The first body would precede itself by one rank
C) Multiplicity would emanate from the One
D) The soul would be an intellect

Key Takeaway
The philosophers prove that the cause of the first body must be an intellect by showing that any other possibility leads to a logical contradiction, especially the absurdity of a thing preceding itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The proof shows that the cause of the first body must be an intellect. When you look at the vast universe, do you truly see it as the product of a thinking act? Or do you take it for granted, failing to let that thought lead you to deeper reverence for the One who gave you intellect? What will you do today to use your intellect to reflect on a single created thing and see it as a sign pointing to its Cause?
?
The argument eliminates the soul as a cause because it depends on a body. Think of a time when you relied on something created as if it were ultimate—perhaps your own strength, wealth, or status. How does this proof remind you that everything other than Allah is dependent and can never be the true cause? What is one specific dependence in your life that you need to reorient toward Allah alone?
?
If the first body was caused by an intellect, then your own intellect is a trace of that higher intellect. Are you cultivating your intellect to know the truth and serve it, or are you leaving it idle? Write down one book or topic you will study this month to strengthen your understanding of Islamic creed using reason, and set a time to begin.

First Proof: The First Emanation Must Be an Intellect
Taftazani proves that the first effect of the First Cause is a separate intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's argument that the first effect of the First Cause must be a separate intellect, not a body, matter, form, or soul.

Core Idea
Only a separate intellect can be the first emanation from the One.

This lesson presents Taftazani's careful argument that the first creation from the absolutely simple God cannot be composite or dependent on matter. He eliminates every candidate except the intellect, which is non-material and self-sufficient. By following his step-by-step reasoning, you will see how kalam thinkers defended the uniqueness of the first effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the logical structure: Taftazani lists candidates and eliminates each.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the eliminated candidates
List the four candidates Taftazani considers: body, matter, form, and soul. Note the specific reason for rejecting each.
Look for key terms like جسم, مادة, صورة, نفس.
3
Understand why each fails
For each candidate, understand the logical contradiction: multiplicity from One, self-precedence, circularity, or dependence on a body.
The core principle: from the simple One, only one simple effect can proceed directly.
4
Grasp the conclusion
Taftazani concludes that the only entity that satisfies the conditions is a separate intellect (عقل). Confirm that it is non-material both in essence and act.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جسمانياً في ذاته وفعله، وهو المراد بالعقل. أما الجوهرية فلأن العرض لا يمكن بدون المحل. فالمحل إما معلول للعلة الأولى أعني الواجب فيلزم صدور الكثير أعني العرض والمحل من الواحد الحقيقي، وأما العرض فيلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه. وأما المفارقة فلأنه لو كان جسماً وهو مركب من المادة والصورة لزم المحال المذكور، وإن كان مادة أو صورة، وكل منهما لا يوجد بدون الآخر، لزم فاعلية أحدهما للآخر وهو محال. وأما المادة فلأن شأنها القبول دون العقل، وأما الصورة فلأنها إنما تفعل بمشاركة المادة، فيلزم تقدمها على نفسها. وإن كان نفساً أي مفارقاً في ذاته لا في فعله، فالبدن الذي هو شرط الفاعلية: إما معلول للواجب فيلزم الكثرة، أو للنفس فيلزم تقدمه على نفسه. فصار الحاصل أن لنا أمراً صح وجوده عن العلة الأولى، وإيجاده للمعلول الثاني، وما ذلك إلا العقل، لأن الجسم لما فيه من الكثرة لا يصلح معلولاً للعلة الأولى، وغيره لا يصلح علة للمعلول الثاني لأن ما يصلح منه للعلية يفتقر في عليته إلى أمر خارج عن ذاته، فإن كان معلولاً له لزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه، وإن كان معلولاً للعلة الأولى لزم صدور الكثرة عنها.
It is non-material in essence and act, and that is what is meant by intellect. As for essentiality, because accident cannot exist without a substrate. So the substrate is either caused by the First Cause, i.e., the Necessary, then the emanation of the many—i.e., the accident and the substrate—from the true One would follow; or it is an accident, then the precedence of a thing over itself would follow. As for separation, because if it were a body composed of matter and form, the mentioned impossibility would follow; and if it were matter or form, each not existing without the other, then the agency of one over the other would follow, which is impossible. As for matter, because its property is receptivity without intellect; and as for form, because it acts only with the participation of matter, so its precedence over itself would follow. And if it were a soul—i.e., separate in essence but not in its act—then the body which is a condition for agency: either it is caused by the Necessary, then multiplicity would follow, or by the soul, then its precedence over itself would follow. So the result is that we have something whose existence from the First Cause is valid, and its bringing into existence of the second effect, and that is nothing but the intellect, because the body, due to the multiplicity in it, is not fit to be an effect of the First Cause, and other than it is not fit to be a cause of the second effect, because that which is fit for causality from it needs in its causality something external to its essence; if it is caused by it, then the precedence of a thing over itself would follow, and if it is caused by the First Cause, then the emanation of multiplicity from it would follow.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the first effect emanating from the First Cause (al-ʿilla al-ūlā) must be a separate intellect (al-ʿaql). Taftazani systematically eliminates other possibilities: if it were material (like a body), it would be composite and thus impossible for the absolutely simple One; if it were matter (mādda) or form (ṣūra), they require each other, leading to circularity; if it were a soul (nafs) that needs a body for action, the body would have to exist first, causing precedence or multiplicity. The only remaining candidate is an intellect that is separate (mujarrad) in both essence and act, which can serve as the first effect and then cause the second effect without involving multiplicity or self-precedence.

Correction: This proof shows that any material or soul-like entity would introduce multiplicity or self-precedence, so only a separate intellect avoids logical impossibilities.

The first emanation from the First Cause must be a separate intellect, because only it is simple enough to proceed from the One and self-sufficient to cause the next effect.

The proof eliminates everything composite or dependent as the first effect. Second Proof: The Cause of the First Body is a Separate Intellect
Taftazani argues by elimination that the cause of the first body must be an intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's proof that the cause of the first physical body cannot be anything material or dependent on a body, and must therefore be a separate intellect.

Core Idea
The cause of the first body must be an intellect, otherwise either multiplicity or self-precedence results.

This passage presents the second proof for the existence of a separate intellect that causes the first physical body. Taftazani systematically eliminates all possible candidates—the Necessary Being, a body, an accident, a soul, matter, and form—by showing that each leads to absurdity. The only remaining possibility is an intellect that is independent of matter. As you read, trace the logical steps carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the English translation to grasp the overall argument.
2
Identify the thesis and main branches
The thesis: the cause of the first body must be an intellect. If not, it falls into two branches: either it is the Necessary (leading to multiplicity) or something else (leading to self-precedence).
Focus on the two conditional statements and their consequences.
3
Examine the impossibility of body or accident
If the cause were a body or an accident, it is 'obvious' that it cannot be the cause of the first body. Why? Because a body or accident would itself be part of the material world and thus would need a prior cause.
The text calls this 'zāhir' (clear). Think about why it's self-evident.
4
Understand the argument against the soul
A soul's action is conditioned by a body. So if the soul were the cause, the body it needs would have to exist before the first body, leading to self-precedence (the first body would precede itself).
The degree of self-precedence varies: first body by one degree, later bodies by multiple degrees.
5
Analyze the argument against matter and form
Matter and form are interdependent; their combination is a body. If either were the agent of the first body, then prior to the first body there would already be a body (the combination), again causing self-precedence.
This elimination leaves only a separate intellect as the cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيها: أن علة أول الأجسام يجب أن تكون عقلاً، وإلا لكان إما واجباً فيلزم صدور الكثير عنه، وإما غيره فيلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه. أما إذا كان جسماً أو عرضاً قائماً به فظاهر، وأما إذا كان نفساً فلأن فعلها مشروط بالجسم، وإلا لكان عقلاً لا نفساً، فذلك الجسم: أما الجسم الأول فيتقدم على نفسه بمرتبة، وأما الثاني والثالث فيتقدم بمراتب. وأما إذا كان مادة أو صورة فلأن كلا منهما لا يوجد بدون الأخرى ومجموعهما جسم، فلو كان فاعل الجسم الأول إحداهما لكان قبل الجسم الأول جسم، وفيه تقدم الشيء على نفسه بمرتبة، أو بمراتب.
And the second proof: that the cause of the first body must be an intellect; otherwise, it would either be the Necessary, then the emanation of multiplicity from it would follow, or something else, then the precedence of a thing over itself would follow. As for if it is a body or an accident subsisting in it, it is obvious; and as for if it is a soul, because its act is conditioned by the body, otherwise it would be an intellect not a soul, so that body: the first body would precede itself by one degree, and the second and third by degrees. And as for if it is matter or form, because each of them does not exist without the other and their aggregate is a body, so if the agent of the first body were one of them, then there would be a body before the first body, and in that is the precedence of a thing over itself by one degree or by degrees.
Source pages p. 356

Commentary
This passage is the second proof for the existence of a separate intellect. Taftazani argues that the cause of the first body (al-jism al-awwal) cannot be the Necessary Being, a body, an accident, a soul, matter, or form—because each leads to either multiplicity emanating from the One or to the logical impossibility of a thing preceding itself. The only remaining possibility is an intellect that is separate from matter and act. The proof proceeds by elimination: if the cause were the Necessary, multiplicity follows; if it were something else, self-precedence follows. He then examines specific candidates: a body or accident is too obvious an impossibility; a soul requires a body for its action, so the body would have to exist before the soul, leading to self-precedence; matter and form are interdependent and their combination is a body, so any agent among them would imply a prior body. Thus the cause must be a separate intellect.

الكثرة
تقدم الشيء على نفسه
الجسم
النفس
المادة
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the soul could be the cause of the first body because it seems spiritual yet connected to the body.
Correction: The proof shows that a soul's action requires a body, so the body would have to exist before the soul, making the soul's own existence dependent on a prior body. Thus, the soul cannot be the cause of the first body.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Taftazani's second proof eliminates all material and dependent causes for the first body, leaving only a separate intellect as the cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument eliminates the possibility that the first body could be caused by anything material, including matter and form. Third Proof: Perpetual Motion Requires a Constant Intellect
Taftazani argues that the perpetual motion of the heavens must aim at an unchanging, perfect intellect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Taftazani's third proof that the perpetual motion of the spheres must aim at a fully actual, unchanging intellect, and why the goal cannot be God Himself.

Core Idea
The perpetual motion of the heavens can only aim at a fully actual, unchanging intellect.

Taftazani continues his arguments for the existence of separate intellects. This third proof leverages the observed perpetual motion of the celestial spheres, arguing that such motion must have a constant, perfect goal. He then distinguishes this goal from God and identifies it as an intellect. The passage also notes that this proof was challenged by denying its premises.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation to grasp the logical flow of the argument.
Notice how Taftazani builds the proof in a concise manner.
2
Identify the key claim
The perpetual motion by will must aim at attaining a perpetual, indivisible likeness through a fully actual intellect.
This is the core of the proof.
3
Understand the necessary condition
If no such goal existed, motion would either cease or seek the impossible. This shows the goal must exist.
4
Analyze the elimination argument
The goal cannot be the Necessary Being (God) because then all motions would be identical, but they differ. Therefore, the goal must be an intellect.
This step uses the diversity of motions as evidence.
5
Note the rebuttal
The proof was rebutted by denying most of its premises. Reflect on what this means for the argument's strength.
This shows the argument is not universally accepted.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الثالث): إن دوام حركات الأفلاك بالإرادة لا يجوز أن يكون إلا لنيل شبه دائم غير منقسم بمعقول كامل بالفعل، لا يتناهى كمالاً، وإلا لزم الانقطاع أو طلب المحال. وليس هو الواجب، وإلا لم تختلف الحركات فيتعين العقل. وردّ بمنع أكثر المقدمات.

أي ثالث وجوه الاحتجاج على ثبوت العقل أنه قد ثبت أن حركات الأفلاك
He said (the third proof): The perpetuity of the motions of the spheres by will cannot be except for attaining a perpetual, indivisible likeness by an intellect fully actual, whose perfection is infinite; otherwise cessation or seeking the impossible would follow. And it is not the Necessary, otherwise the motions would not differ; so the intellect is determined. And it was rebutted by denying most of the premises.

That is, the third way of argument for the existence of the intellect: that it has been established that the motions of the spheres...
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage introduces the third proof for the existence of a separate intellect, based on the perpetual motion of the celestial spheres. Taftazani argues that the continuous, willful motion of the heavens must aim at something perpetual and indivisible, namely a fully actual intellect that is infinitely perfect. If there were no such goal, the motion would either stop or aim at the impossible. This goal cannot be God Himself, because then all motions would be identical (since God is one), but they differ. Therefore, the intended goal must be a separate intellect. The passage ends with a brief mention that this proof was rebutted by denying most of its premises, and a explanatory note that this is the third argument for establishing the intellect.

The Celestial Sphere's Beloved is the Intelligible
The author eliminates sensible objects and establishes an intelligible beloved.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the object of the sphere's motion must be an intelligible beloved, not a sensible one.

Core Idea
The sphere's simplicity rules out sensible desire, so it seeks an intellectual beloved.

This lesson begins the proof that the celestial sphere's perpetual motion is driven by a passionate love for an intelligible object. The author first eliminates the possibility that the sphere seeks a sensible object, because sensible pursuit involves lust and anger, which cannot apply to a simple, uniform sphere. This establishes that the sought object is an intelligible beloved.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the strong contrasts: sensible vs. intelligible, and the argument that the sphere cannot have lust or anger.
The passage begins mid-sentence with 'إرادية'.
2
Understand the two options
The sphere's motion is volitional, so its object is either sensible or intelligible. The sensible is dismissed because it requires attraction/repulsion, which are impossible for the sphere's simple nature.
Attraction of agreeable is lust, repulsion of disagreeable is anger.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Thus the object must be intelligible. This intelligible object is called a beloved (معشوق), and the intense love is 'ishq (عشق).
The phrase 'فرط طلب تقتضيه محبة مفرطة' indicates extreme desire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إرادية، فيكون المطلوب محسوساً أو معقولاً. والأول باطل . لأن طلب المحسوس إما أن يكون للجذب أو للدفع ، وجذب الملائم شهوة ، ودفع المنافر غضب، وهما على الفلك محال لأنه بسيط متشابه الأحوال، لا يتغير من حالة غير ملائمة إلى حالة ملائمة، فتعين الثاني، وهو أن يكون المطلوب معقولاً، وذلك المطلوب معشوق، لأن دوام ،الحركة، إنما يكون لفرط طلب تقتضيه محبة مفرطة (۱) هي العشق . فالعاشق الطالب . إما أن يريد نيل ذاته، أو نيل صفاته، أو نيل شبه إحداهما، وإلا لما كان له تعلق بالمعشوق والأولان ،باطلان لأن الذات أو الصفة، إما أن تنال في الجملة ، فيلزم انقطاع الحركة لامتناع طلب الحاصل وهو محال(۲)، لأنها علة وجود الزمان وإما أن لا ينال أصلاً، فلا بد من اليأس عن حصول ما هذا شأنه، ويلزم الانقطاع ، أو دوام طلب المحال على أن نيل الصفة محال لامتناع زوالها عن محلها، فتعين ،الثالث وهو أن يكون الطلب لنيل شبه بالمعشوق، ولا يجوز أن يكون شبهاً مستقراً، وإلا يلزم الانقطاع ، أو طلب الحاصل، بل شبها غير مستقر، أي شبها (۳) بعد شبه بحيث ينقضي شبه ويحصل آخر، ويجب أن يحفظ ذلك بتعاقب الأفراد، لا إلى نهاية ، وإلا يلزم الانقطاع ، فيثبت أن المطلوب حصول مشابهات غير متناهية تحصل على التدريج في أوقات غير متناهية، لئلا يلزم انقطاع الحركة فيكون المعشوق موجوداً متصفاً بصفات كمال غير متناهية بتحرك الفلك، ويستخرج بحركته الأوضاع الممكنة ، من القوة إلى الفعل، ويحصل له بكل وضع شبه بالمعشوق الذي هو بالفعل من كل الوجوه، ولم يزل يزول وضع ويحصل آخر، فيزول شبه ويحصل ،آخر، ويحفظ كل منهما بتعاقب الأفراد والفلك، يقبل منه للقبض بواسطة تلك المشابهات، ولا يجوز أن يكون ذلك هو الواجب، وإلا لم تختلف الحركات، فتعين أن يكون فعلاً، ويثبت بذلك تعدد العقول.
Volitional, so the sought object is either sensible or intelligible. The first is false, because the pursuit of the sensible is either for attraction or repulsion; attraction of the agreeable is lust, and repulsion of the disagreeable is anger, and both are impossible for the sphere because it is simple, uniform in states, not changing from a non-agreeable state to an agreeable one. So the second is determined, that the sought object is intelligible, and that sought object is beloved, because the perpetuity of motion is only due to extreme desire necessitated by excessive love (1) which is passionate love ('ishq). So the lover-seeker either intends to attain the essence, or the attributes, or a likeness of one of the two; otherwise he would have no connection with the beloved. The first two are false, because the essence or attribute, either it is attained in sum, necessitating cessation of motion due to the impossibility of seeking what is already attained—and that is impossible (2) because it is the cause of the existence of time—or it is not attained at all, so despair of attaining what is such must occur, and cessation would follow, or perpetual seeking of the impossible; moreover, attainment of the attribute is impossible because its removal from its locus is impossible. So the third is determined: that the seeking is for attaining a likeness of the beloved. And it is not permissible for it to be a stable likeness, otherwise cessation would follow, or seeking what is already attained. Rather, it is an unstable likeness, i.e., likeness after likeness such that a likeness passes away and another occurs. And that must be preserved by the succession of individuals, not to infinity, otherwise cessation would follow. So it is established that the sought object is the occurrence of infinite similitudes occurring gradually in infinite times, lest cessation of motion occur. Thus the beloved exists described with infinite perfections by the motion of the sphere, and by its motion it extracts possible positions from potentiality to actuality, and by each position it obtains a likeness of the beloved who is actual in all respects. And it continues: a position passes away and another occurs, so a likeness passes away and another occurs, and each is preserved by the succession of individuals. And the sphere receives from it for possession by means of those similitudes. And it is not permissible for that to be the Necessary Being, otherwise motions would not differ. So it is determined that it is an act, and thereby the multiplicity of intellects is established.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a complex philosophical proof that the celestial sphere's perpetual motion is due to a passionate love (عشق) for an intellectual being, and this leads to the existence of multiple separate intellects. The argument proceeds as follows: The sphere's motion is volitional, so its object must be either sensible or intelligible. The sensible is ruled out because sensible pursuit is either for attraction (lust) or repulsion (anger), both impossible for a simple, uniform sphere. So the object is intelligible, i.e., a beloved. The sphere seeks either to attain the beloved's essence, attributes, or a likeness. Attaining the essence or attribute either ends motion (if achieved) or leads to despair (if impossible). Both are unacceptable because motion is perpetual. Therefore it seeks a likeness. The likeness must be unstable (perpetually renewed) to sustain motion, preserved by the succession of individuals over infinite time. Thus the beloved must have infinite perfections actualized by the sphere's motion, but it cannot be the Necessary Being (otherwise motions would be uniform), so it is an intellect. This proves multiple intellects.

How the Sphere Attains Likeness of the Beloved and Proves Multiple Intellects
The author argues for unstable likeness and concludes multiple intellects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the tripartite division of seeking (essence, attribute, likeness) and how it leads to the proof of multiple separate intellects.

Core Idea
The sphere perpetually seeks an unstable likeness of an intellectual beloved, proving a multiplicity of intellects.

Having established that the sphere's beloved is intelligible, the author now examines how the sphere seeks it. The sphere cannot seek the essence or attribute of the beloved, as that would lead to cessation of motion. Instead, it seeks a continuously renewed likeness, which generates perpetual motion. This likeness is achieved by the sphere's own motion extracting potential positions, and since the beloved cannot be the Necessary Being (otherwise motions would be uniform), it must be one of multiple intellects.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read from 'فالعاشق الطالب' to the end. Note the three possibilities: essence, attribute, likeness.
The first two are false.
2
Understand why essence and attribute are impossible
Attaining the essence or attribute either ends motion (if achieved) or leads to despair (if impossible). Both contradict perpetual motion.
Attaining the attribute is also impossible because it cannot be removed from its locus.
3
Grasp the solution: unstable likeness
The sphere seeks a likeness that is not stable but renewed over time, producing infinite similitudes gradually.
This requires the beloved to have infinite perfections.
4
Identify the conclusion
The beloved cannot be the Necessary Being, so it must be a created intellect. The diversity of motions implies multiple intellects.
Thus the multiplicity of intellects is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إرادية، فيكون المطلوب محسوساً أو معقولاً. والأول باطل . لأن طلب المحسوس إما أن يكون للجذب أو للدفع ، وجذب الملائم شهوة ، ودفع المنافر غضب، وهما على الفلك محال لأنه بسيط متشابه الأحوال، لا يتغير من حالة غير ملائمة إلى حالة ملائمة، فتعين الثاني، وهو أن يكون المطلوب معقولاً، وذلك المطلوب معشوق، لأن دوام ،الحركة، إنما يكون لفرط طلب تقتضيه محبة مفرطة (۱) هي العشق . فالعاشق الطالب . إما أن يريد نيل ذاته، أو نيل صفاته، أو نيل شبه إحداهما، وإلا لما كان له تعلق بالمعشوق والأولان ،باطلان لأن الذات أو الصفة، إما أن تنال في الجملة ، فيلزم انقطاع الحركة لامتناع طلب الحاصل وهو محال(۲)، لأنها علة وجود الزمان وإما أن لا ينال أصلاً، فلا بد من اليأس عن حصول ما هذا شأنه، ويلزم الانقطاع ، أو دوام طلب المحال على أن نيل الصفة محال لامتناع زوالها عن محلها، فتعين ،الثالث وهو أن يكون الطلب لنيل شبه بالمعشوق، ولا يجوز أن يكون شبهاً مستقراً، وإلا يلزم الانقطاع ، أو طلب الحاصل، بل شبها غير مستقر، أي شبها (۳) بعد شبه بحيث ينقضي شبه ويحصل آخر، ويجب أن يحفظ ذلك بتعاقب الأفراد، لا إلى نهاية ، وإلا يلزم الانقطاع ، فيثبت أن المطلوب حصول مشابهات غير متناهية تحصل على التدريج في أوقات غير متناهية، لئلا يلزم انقطاع الحركة فيكون المعشوق موجوداً متصفاً بصفات كمال غير متناهية بتحرك الفلك، ويستخرج بحركته الأوضاع الممكنة ، من القوة إلى الفعل، ويحصل له بكل وضع شبه بالمعشوق الذي هو بالفعل من كل الوجوه، ولم يزل يزول وضع ويحصل آخر، فيزول شبه ويحصل ،آخر، ويحفظ كل منهما بتعاقب الأفراد والفلك، يقبل منه للقبض بواسطة تلك المشابهات، ولا يجوز أن يكون ذلك هو الواجب، وإلا لم تختلف الحركات، فتعين أن يكون فعلاً، ويثبت بذلك تعدد العقول.
Volitional, so the sought object is either sensible or intelligible. The first is false, because the pursuit of the sensible is either for attraction or repulsion; attraction of the agreeable is lust, and repulsion of the disagreeable is anger, and both are impossible for the sphere because it is simple, uniform in states, not changing from a non-agreeable state to an agreeable one. So the second is determined, that the sought object is intelligible, and that sought object is beloved, because the perpetuity of motion is only due to extreme desire necessitated by excessive love (1) which is passionate love ('ishq). So the lover-seeker either intends to attain the essence, or the attributes, or a likeness of one of the two; otherwise he would have no connection with the beloved. The first two are false, because the essence or attribute, either it is attained in sum, necessitating cessation of motion due to the impossibility of seeking what is already attained—and that is impossible (2) because it is the cause of the existence of time—or it is not attained at all, so despair of attaining what is such must occur, and cessation would follow, or perpetual seeking of the impossible; moreover, attainment of the attribute is impossible because its removal from its locus is impossible. So the third is determined: that the seeking is for attaining a likeness of the beloved. And it is not permissible for it to be a stable likeness, otherwise cessation would follow, or seeking what is already attained. Rather, it is an unstable likeness, i.e., likeness after likeness such that a likeness passes away and another occurs. And that must be preserved by the succession of individuals, not to infinity, otherwise cessation would follow. So it is established that the sought object is the occurrence of infinite similitudes occurring gradually in infinite times, lest cessation of motion occur. Thus the beloved exists described with infinite perfections by the motion of the sphere, and by its motion it extracts possible positions from potentiality to actuality, and by each position it obtains a likeness of the beloved who is actual in all respects. And it continues: a position passes away and another occurs, so a likeness passes away and another occurs, and each is preserved by the succession of individuals. And the sphere receives from it for possession by means of those similitudes. And it is not permissible for that to be the Necessary Being, otherwise motions would not differ. So it is determined that it is an act, and thereby the multiplicity of intellects is established.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a complex philosophical proof that the celestial sphere's perpetual motion is due to a passionate love (عشق) for an intellectual being, and this leads to the existence of multiple separate intellects. The argument proceeds as follows: The sphere's motion is volitional, so its object must be either sensible or intelligible. The sensible is ruled out because sensible pursuit is either for attraction (lust) or repulsion (anger), both impossible for a simple, uniform sphere. So the object is intelligible, i.e., a beloved. The sphere seeks either to attain the beloved's essence, attributes, or a likeness. Attaining the essence or attribute either ends motion (if achieved) or leads to despair (if impossible). Both are unacceptable because motion is perpetual. Therefore it seeks a likeness. The likeness must be unstable (perpetually renewed) to sustain motion, preserved by the succession of individuals over infinite time. Thus the beloved must have infinite perfections actualized by the sphere's motion, but it cannot be the Necessary Being (otherwise motions would be uniform), so it is an intellect. This proves multiple intellects.

Objections to the Proof of Celestial Motion Through Love
The author lists several objections to the previous argument about celestial motion and love.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objections raised against the proof of celestial motion through love and how they challenge the premises.

Core Idea
The proof of celestial love must answer every objection to stand.

In the previous lesson, the author presented a proof that celestial motion is driven by love for a transcendent beloved. Now, an objector raises several points that challenge the assumptions of that proof. This lesson presents those objections, which the author will later refute.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Notice the pattern: the objector repeatedly says 'We do not concede' (لا نسلّم) followed by a reason.
2
Identify each objection
List the five distinct objections presented. Each begins with a denial of a premise. Mark them in the text.
Objections: 1) Perpetuity of motion not necessary; 2) Sensible pursuit may be for knowledge; 3) Lust/anger possible in spheres; 4) Non-attainment doesn't entail despair; 5) Beloved with infinite perfections need not be intellect.
3
Understand the objector's strategy
The objector does not reject the entire theory but attacks specific premises. Note that the last objection uses the author's own argument (succession of individuals) against him.
This is a common dialectical technique in kalam.
4
Reflect on the burden of proof
Consider the objector's conclusion: 'Although some of these can be repelled, yet the desired conclusion is not achieved except by repelling all.' This sets up the need for a comprehensive response.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاعتراض عليه. أنا لا نسلّم وجوب دوام حركة السماء، وامتناع انقطاعها، ولا نسلّم أن طلب المحسوس ، لا يكون إلا للجذب (۱) أو الدفع . لم لا يجوز أن يكون لمعرفته أو التشبه به أو غير ذلك . ولا نسلّم استحالة الشهوة والغضب على الأفلاك. ولا يلزم من تشابه أجزائها في الحقيقة تشابه أحوالها . ولا نسلّم أنه يلزم من عدم نيل ذات المعشوق، أو حاله، حصول اليأس ولا من نيله انقطاع الطلب . لم لا يجوز أن يدوم الرجاء، أو يكون المعشوق، أو حاله أمراً غير قار، يتحفظ نوعه، بتعاقب الأفراد كما ذكرتم في الشبه. ولا نسلّم أن المعشوق الموصوف بصفات كمال غير متناهية هو العقل، وإنما يلزم ذلك لو كان ذلك على الاجتماع دون التعاقب، وبعض هذه وإن أمكن دفعه، لكن لا يتم المطلوب إلا بدفع الكل.
And the objection to it: We do not concede the necessity of the perpetuity of the motion of the heaven and the impossibility of its cessation; nor do we concede that the pursuit of the sensible is only for attraction or repulsion. Why is it not permissible that it be for its knowledge or for resembling it or other than that? Nor do we concede the impossibility of lust and anger in the celestial spheres. And it does not follow from the similarity of their parts in reality the similarity of their states. Nor do we concede that from the non-attainment of the essence of the beloved or its state, despair necessarily occurs, nor from its attainment does cessation of seeking necessarily follow. Why is it not permissible that hope continues, or that the beloved or its state be a non-permanent thing whose type is preserved by the succession of individuals as you mentioned regarding likeness? Nor do we concede that the beloved described with infinite perfections is the intellect; that would only follow if it were simultaneous rather than successive. And although some of these can be repelled, yet the desired conclusion is not achieved except by repelling all.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage presents a series of objections to the preceding proof. The objector (al-mu'tarid) refuses to concede several key premises. First, he denies that celestial motion is necessarily perpetual and cannot cease. Second, he objects that sensible pursuit might aim at knowledge or resemblance, not only attraction/repulsion. Third, he rejects the impossibility of lust and anger in celestial spheres, arguing that similarity of parts does not entail similarity of states. Fourth, he denies that failure to attain the beloved's essence leads to despair, or that attainment ends seeking; hope could continue, or the beloved's state might be non-permanent, preserved by succession (as the argument already used for likeness). Finally, he denies that the beloved with infinite perfections must be an intellect; that conclusion would follow only if the perfections were simultaneous, not successive. The objector concludes that while some objections may be answerable, the proof fails unless all are refuted.

الرجاء

Correction: The objections target specific premises and are part of a dialectical exchange; the author will respond to each, and the proof can be defended.

Checkpoint
According to the objections, why might the sphere's pursuit of the sensible not be limited to attraction and repulsion?
A) Because the sphere is simple.
B) Because it could be for knowledge or resemblance.
C) Because attraction and repulsion are impossible.
D) Because the sphere desires nothing.

Key Takeaway
The proof's validity depends on repelling all objections; each challenges a specific premise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Intellect as Principle of Celestial Motion
Al-Taftazani explains how the intellect causes perpetual motion via emanation, not direct action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the intellect causes perpetual motion through emanation rather than direct action, preserving its immaterial nature.

Core Idea
The intellect moves spheres not by touch, but by emanating infinite power upon souls.

Al-Taftazani explains a key distinction: the intellect is not a mover like a hand pushing a ball. Instead, it acts indirectly, by pouring its infinite power into the celestial souls, which then move the bodies. This subtle distinction protects the immateriality of the intellect while explaining the perpetual motion of the heavens.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the contrast between 'direct action' and 'emanation'.
2
Identify the two modes of causation
The passage presents two ways the intellect could act: directly (mubashara) or via emanation (ifada). Find where each is mentioned and note why direct action is rejected.
Direct action would give the intellect attachment to the body, contradicting its nature.
3
Understand the role of the moving soul
The intellect emanates upon the 'moving soul' (nafs muharrika). Identify what this soul receives and what it does with that reception.
The soul receives infinite power and then moves the body with infinite effect.
4
Reflect on the necessity of infinite power
Consider why the intellect must have infinite power. The passage states that perpetual motion requires an infinite cause; a cause attached to bodies cannot produce an infinite effect unless sustained by an infinite intellect.
This connects the metaphysical principle of causation to the observed perpetual motion of the spheres.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[قال وإنها مبادىء يعني أحوال العقول أنها مبادىء لكمالات النفوس الفلكية والبشرية بل النفوس والأجرام أنفسها] . بمعنى أن الموجب لتلك الحركة السرمدية هو العقل لا بطريق المباشرة، وإلا لكان له تعلق بالجسم من طريق التصرف فيه ، فلم يكن عقلاً، بل بطريق الإفاضة على النفس المحركة بقوته التي لا تتناهى وبقبولها منه ذلك الفيض وتأثيرها تأثيراً غير متناه عن سبيل الوساطة دون المبادئية لامتناع صدور غير المتناهي عما يتعلق بالأجسام، ما لم يكن مستمراً من مبدأ عقل غير متناهي القوة
[He said: And that they are principles, meaning the states of the intellects are principles for the perfections of celestial and human souls, nay, souls and bodies themselves.] Meaning that the cause of that perpetual motion is the intellect, not by way of direct action, otherwise it would have attachment to the body through manipulation of it, and would not be an intellect; but rather by way of emanation upon the moving soul through its infinite power and its acceptance from it of that emanation and its effect being infinite through the intermediary without being a principle, because of the impossibility of the infinite issuing from what is attached to bodies, unless it is continuous from a principle of an intellect of infinite power.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the claim that intellects are principles (mabadi') for the perfections of celestial and human souls, and even for bodies themselves. The author interprets this to mean that the cause of the perpetual motion of the celestial spheres is the intellect, but not directly—otherwise it would be too attached to matter to be an intellect. Instead, the intellect acts by way of emanation (ifada) upon the moving soul, which then moves the body. The intellect's influence is infinite and continuous, which requires the intellect itself to have infinite power. The point is to show that the celestial motion originates from an immaterial cause that is not directly involved with bodies.

Key Terms
مبادىء (principles)
إفاضة (emanation)
نفس محركة (moving soul)
حركة سرمدية (perpetual motion)
قوة غير متناهية (infinite power)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The intellect directly moves celestial bodies by manipulating them.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the intellect acts indirectly: it emanates its infinite power upon the moving soul, which then moves the body.

Checkpoint
How does the intellect cause perpetual motion according to this passage?
A) By directly moving the celestial bodies.
B) By creating the bodies and setting them in motion.
C) By emanating power upon the moving soul, which then moves the body.
D) By being the material cause of the bodies.

Key Takeaway
The intellect causes perpetual motion indirectly by emanating infinite power upon the celestial soul.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You depend on countless causes every day—do you ever pause to consider the immaterial power behind them? This lesson shows that even the heavens are moved only indirectly by an intellect. In your own life, are you seeking the ultimate source of power, or are you satisfied with secondary causes? Will you commit to reflecting on the chain of causation and turning your heart to the First Cause?
?
The intellect emanates without losing anything; its giving is infinite. How generous are you with your knowledge and goodness? Do you hold back, afraid of depletion, or do you trust that emanation flows from a higher source? This week, consciously give something (time, knowledge, kindness) expecting nothing in return, as an imitation of the intellect's emanation.
?
The moving soul accepts the emanation and acts. Are you receptive to the emanation of truth and wisdom that reaches you? Or do you close yourself off, relying only on your own finite power? Take a moment to ask: Have I truly accepted the guidance that has been emanated to me? If not, what is holding me back?

The Active Intellect as Giver of Perfections
Al-Taftazani explains the active intellect as bestower of perfections, using sun and mirror analogies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the role of the active intellect in bestowing perfections on human souls and the analogies used to explain this process.

Core Idea
Human cognition depends on the active intellect; the soul receives intelligibles from it like the eye receives light from the sun.

In this passage from Sharh al-Maqasid, al-Taftazani explains the function of the active intellect, the last in the hierarchy of intellects. He describes it as the source of perfections for human souls, transforming them from potentiality to actuality in their intellections. Through vivid analogies of the sun and the mirror, he illustrates how the soul receives and loses intellectual forms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Notice the flow and the author's use of analogy.
2
Identify the active intellect's role
Find the phrases that describe what the active intellect does: it gives perfections, brings souls from potential to actual, and is like a treasury. Reflect on how this contrasts with the soul's passive receiving.
3
Analyze the mirror analogy
Compare the mirror analogy to the sun-eyesight analogy. How does the mirror analogy deepen the understanding of how the soul loses intelligibles when occupied with sensory matters?
The mirror loses the image when turned away; similarly, the soul loses intellectual forms when turned to the senses.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الأخير من العقول، وهو المسمى بالعقل الفعال يعطي النفوس البشرية كمالاتها ، ويخرجها من القوة إلى الفعل في تعقلاتها، ونسبته إلى النفوس نسبة الشمس إلى الأبصار بل أتم، وهو كالخزانة للمعقولات إذا أقبلنا عليه قبلنا منه، وإذا اشتغلنا عنه بجانب الحس، انمحت عنا الصورة العقلية كالمرآة، فإنها إذا حوذي بها صورة تمثلت فيها. فإذا أعرض بها عنها يزال ذلك التمثل، وربما تمثل فيها غير تلك الصورة على حسب ما يحاذي بها، فكذا النفس إذا أعرض بها عن جانب القدس إلى جانب الحس، أو إلى أي شيء آخر من أمور القدس
And among them: that the last of the intellects, which is called the active intellect, gives human souls their perfections and brings them out from potentiality to actuality in their intellections. Its relationship to souls is like that of the sun to eyesight, nay more complete. It is like the treasury of intelligibles: when we turn towards it we receive from it, and when we occupy ourselves away from it with the side of sense, the intellectual form is effaced from us like a mirror: when an image is brought opposite it, it appears in it; then when it is turned away from it, that appearance disappears, and perhaps another image appears in it according to what is brought opposite it. Similarly, the soul when it is turned away from the side of holiness towards the side of sense, or towards any other thing of holy matters.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage describes the active intellect (al-'aql al-fa''al), which is the last of the intellects in the cosmic hierarchy. It gives human souls their perfections, bringing them from potentiality to actuality in intellection. The author uses an analogy: the active intellect is to the soul as the sun is to eyesight (basar)—but even more so. It is like a treasury (khazanah) of intelligibles: when we turn towards it, we receive knowledge; when we turn to the senses, the intellectual form fades, as a mirror loses an image when turned away from it. The soul similarly, when turned from the holy (al-quds) to the sensory, loses the intelligible. This illustrates the dependence of human cognition on the active intellect.

Key Terms
العقل الفعال (active intellect)
كمالات (perfections)
قوة وفعل (potentiality and actuality)
خزانة (treasury)
مرآة (mirror)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Correction: The author says the soul receives intelligibles from the active intellect; it depends on an external source.

Key Takeaway

The Origin of Souls from the Intellects
Al-Taftazani argues for the intellectual origin of souls

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why, according to Al-Taftazani, souls must originate from intellects rather than directly from God or from bodies.

Core Idea
Souls cannot come from God directly because they are always tied to bodies.

Al-Taftazani presents a concise but powerful argument about the origin of souls. He insists that the source of all souls cannot be the Necessary Being Himself, nor can it be bodies. Instead, souls must proceed from the intellects—the intermediate immaterial beings. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English side by side. Notice the two negative arguments: souls are not from the Necessary Being, and not from bodies.
The argument is compressed; pay attention to the logical connectors.
2
Why not from the Necessary Being?
The passage says souls only exist with bodies, so they cannot issue from the True One. Ask yourself: why does being with bodies prevent direct emanation from God?
This touches on the principle that the One cannot produce composite things directly.
3
Why not from bodies?
Bodies and their parts act only through participation—meaning they don't act by themselves. Souls act with bodies, not as bodies. So souls can't be from bodies.
This reinforces that souls are non-material and require an intermediate source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن مبدأ النفوس كلها من حيث هي نفوس، يجب أن يكون من العقول، إذ لا يجوز أن يكون هو الواجب، لأن النفوس لا تكون إلا مع الأجسام فلا تصدر عن الواحد الحقيقي ولا أن تكون من الأجسام وأجزائها ، وأحوالها، لأنها إنما تفعل بمشاركة فيه
And among them: that the principle of all souls, insofar as they are souls, must be from the intellects, because it is not permissible that it be the Necessary Being, since souls only exist together with bodies, so they cannot issue from the True One; nor that they be from bodies and their parts and states, because they only act through participation in [the body].
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the origin of souls. The author states that the principle (mabda') of all souls must be from the intellects, not from the Necessary Being (al-wajib), because souls are always associated with bodies—they cannot issue directly from the One who is absolutely simple. Nor can they be from bodies or their parts, because souls act with the body's participation but are not material. The implication is that souls originate from the intellects, which are intermediate between the Necessary Being and the material world.

Key Terms
مبدأ (principle)
نفس (soul)
واجب (Necessary Being)
جسم (body)
واحد حقيقي (True One)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Souls could emanate directly from God without any intermediary.
Correction: The passage argues that because souls are always with bodies and bodies are composite, they cannot issue directly from the True One, who is absolutely simple.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can't souls issue directly from the Necessary Being?
A) Because the Necessary Being is too remote to create souls.
B) Because souls only exist with bodies, and the True One cannot be the source of composite things.
C) Because souls are material and the Necessary Being is immaterial.
D) Because souls are eternal and the Necessary Being is temporal.

Key Takeaway
Souls must originate from intellects because they cannot come directly from the simple God nor from material bodies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage argues that bodies cannot be the origin of souls because they only act with participation. Does your daily experience of your body and soul resonate with that? In what ways do you feel your soul uses your body? Will you pay more attention to how your body and soul cooperate?

How Intellects Perfect Souls Through Emanation
Al-Taftazani explains how intellects are the principles of perfections for celestial and human souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how intellects serve as principles for perfections through emanation, not direct intervention.

Core Idea
Intellects perfect souls by emanation, not by touching bodies.

This passage from Taftazani unpacks a subtle but crucial point about how immaterial intellects cause motion in the celestial spheres. The author distinguishes between direct agency and emanation, explaining why intellects must act indirectly. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and the literal translation once without analyzing. Let the author's own phrasing sink in.
Notice the key terms: 'ifāḍah (emanation) and 'wasāṭah' (mediation).
2
Identify the thesis
Find where the author states that intellects are principles (mabādi') for perfections. This is the overarching claim.
3
Understand the mode of causation
The author explains that the intellect causes perpetual motion not by direct agency but by emanation upon the soul. Why would direct agency be problematic?
Direct attachment would make the intellect physical, contradicting its nature.
4
Follow the infinite power argument
The passage argues that the effect (motion) is infinite, so its cause must be an intellect of infinite power, not something tied to bodies.
This reinforces the need for an immaterial principle.
5
Link to the bigger picture
Reflect on how this argument establishes the transcendence of intellects while still allowing them to be causes of perfection.
Emanation preserves both causality and immateriality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال وإنها مبادىء يعني أحوال العقول أنها مبادىء لكمالات النفوس الفلكية والبشرية بل النفوس والأجرام أنفسها. بمعنى أن الموجب لتلك الحركة السرمدية هو العقل لا بطريق المباشرة، وإلا لكان له تعلق بالجسم من طريق التصرف فيه، فلم يكن عقلاً، بل بطريق الإفاضة على النفس المحركة بقوته التي لا تتناهى وبقبولها منه ذلك الفيض وتأثيرها تأثيراً غير متناه عن سبيل الوساطة دون المبادئية لامتناع صدور غير المتناهي عما يتعلق بالأجسام، ما لم يكن مستمراً من مبدأ عقل غير متناهي القوة.
He said: 'And that they are principles,' meaning the states of the intellects are principles for the perfections of celestial and human souls, indeed the souls and the bodies themselves. Meaning that the cause of that perpetual motion is the intellect, not by way of direct agency, otherwise it would have attachment to the body through the path of acting upon it, so it would not be an intellect, but rather by way of emanation ( al-ifāḍah ) upon the soul that moves it with its power that is infinite, and its acceptance of that emanation from it, and its effect is an infinite effect through the path of mediation, not as a principle, because it is impossible for the infinite to proceed from something that is attached to bodies, unless it is continuous from a principle that is an intellect of infinite power.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage presents the first argument regarding the role of intellects. It is a proof that intellects serve as principles for the perfections of both celestial and human souls, and even for souls and bodies themselves. The key point is that the cause of the perpetual motion of the celestial spheres is an intellect, but not through direct intervention (which would compromise its immateriality). Instead, it acts through emanation ( ifāḍah ) upon the soul that moves the sphere. The soul, being receptive to this emanation, generates an effect that is infinite in duration. This is necessary because an infinite effect cannot proceed from a being that is directly attached to bodies; hence the cause must be an intellect of infinite power. The argument thus establishes that the intellects are immaterial principles acting through emanation, preserving both their transcendence and their causal role.

Key Terms
عقل
إفاضة
نفس
حركة سرمدية
مبادئية
وساطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the intellect directly moves the celestial sphere, like a hand moving a ball.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the intellect acts through emanation upon the soul, not by direct physical contact, because direct attachment would compromise its immaterial nature.

Checkpoint
How does the intellect cause the perpetual motion of the celestial sphere according to this passage?
A) By direct physical contact
B) By way of emanation upon the soul that moves the sphere
C) By being the body itself
D) By commanding angels

Key Takeaway
Intellects are immaterial principles that cause motion and perfection in souls through emanation, not by direct involvement with matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you allow a higher, immaterial principle to shape your soul by emanation, or do you try to control everything through direct, tangible means? What would change if you trusted that process more?
?
If the intellect perfects souls through emanation, are you actively seeking that emanation through study and reflection, or are you passively waiting? What concrete step will you take today to receive more from that source?
?
The passage says an infinite effect cannot come from something attached to bodies. Do you limit your pursuit of perfection to what is material and finite, or do you seek the infinite from the immaterial? How does your daily schedule reflect that choice?

The Active Intellect and Human Cognition
Al-Taftazani describes the active intellect's role in actualizing human intellects via similes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the active intellect actualizes human knowledge and the metaphorical relationship between the soul and the active intellect as depicted through the similes of the sun and the mirror.

Core Idea
The active intellect is the source of all intelligible forms, like the sun to vision.

Al-Taftazani now explains the role of the last intellect, the active intellect, which bestows perfections on human souls. He uses vivid similes—comparing it to the sun and a mirror—to illustrate how the soul acquires knowledge from the active intellect and how distraction causes loss of that knowledge. Let's examine this passage closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Notice the sequence: first the function, then the sun simile, then the treasury and mirror similes.
Paying attention to the similes will help you grasp the concept.
2
Analyze the sun simile
Compare the active intellect's relation to souls to the sun's relation to eyesight. Why does Al-Taftazani say 'nay more complete'? Consider how eyes need light to see, but here the intellect gives both light and forms.
3
Understand the mirror analogy
The mirror simile illustrates how the soul receives intelligible forms when turned toward the active intellect, and loses them when turned away. Reflect on the condition for receiving knowledge: direct attention.
This analogy also implies that the soul can receive different forms depending on what it is oriented towards.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أن الأخير من العقول، وهو المسمى بالعقل الفعال يعطي النفوس البشرية كمالاتها، ويخرجها من القوة إلى الفعل في تعقلاتها، ونسبته إلى النفوس نسبة الشمس إلى الأبصار بل أتم، وهو كالخزانة للمعقولات إذا أقبلنا عليه قبلنا منه، وإذا اشتغلنا عنه بجانب الحس، انمحت عنا الصورة العقلية كالمرآة، فإنها إذا حوذي بها صورة تمثلت فيها. فإذا أعرض بها عنها يزال ذلك التمثل، وربما تمثل فيها غير تلك الصورة على حسب ما يحاذي بها، فكذا النفس إذا أعرض بها عن جانب القدس إلى جانب الحس، أو إلى أي شيء آخر من أمور القدس.
And among them is that the last of the intellects, which is called the active intellect ( al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl ), gives the human souls their perfections, and brings them from potentiality to actuality in their acts of intellection. Its relation to souls is like the relation of the sun to eyesight, nay more complete. It is like the treasury of intelligibles: when we turn towards it, we receive from it; and when we are occupied with the side of sense, the intelligible form is erased from us, like a mirror. For when a form is placed opposite it, it is reflected in it. If it is turned away from it, that reflection disappears, and perhaps another form is reflected in it according to what is placed opposite it. So too the soul: if it is turned away from the side of the holy to the side of sense, or to any other thing among holy matters.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage continues the enumeration of points regarding intellects. It describes the role of the last intellect, the active intellect ( al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl ), which imparts perfections to human souls, actualizing their potential for knowledge. The author uses a simile: the active intellect is to the soul as the sun is to vision, but even more complete. Then he provides a more detailed metaphor: the active intellect is like a treasury of intelligibles; when we turn towards it (i.e., direct our attention), we receive knowledge; when we are preoccupied with sense perception, the intelligible form is erased from us. This is likened to a mirror: when placed opposite a form, the form appears; when turned away, the image vanishes, and another may appear when a different object is placed opposite. Similarly, the soul, when turned away from the holy (the intelligible realm) toward sense or other matters, loses the intelligible content. The passage emphasizes the dependent relation of human cognition on the active intellect and the importance of directing attention properly.

Key Terms
العقل الفعال
تعقلات
قوة وفعل
خزانة المعقولات
مرآة
القدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The active intellect is identical with God.
Correction: The active intellect is described as the last of the intellects (ākhir min al-ʿuqūl), a created intermediary, not the Creator.

Checkpoint

B) The intelligible form is erased from it
C) It becomes one with the active intellect

Key Takeaway
The active intellect is the intermediary through which human souls acquire intelligible forms; the soul's orientation determines whether it receives or loses knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
